The Ten Categories
intermediate
15 min
Two Types of Relative and the Condition for True Relation
Author defines two types of relative and distinguishes true relative from accidental relative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any entity as a true relative (mudaf haqiqi) or a relative by accident (mudaf bi al-‘arad) using the criteria of quiddity and the condition of repeated relation.

Core Idea
A true relative is pure relation; an accidental relative has an essence that relation merely attaches to.

In this passage from Mantiq Kabir, the author delves into the category of the relative (al-mudaf), distinguishing between things that are truly relational in their essence and those that merely acquire a relation accidentally. Understanding this distinction is crucial for rigorous definitional work in Islamic sciences, especially when analyzing attributes and relations in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of relative
Recognize that the author describes two types: (A) that which has a quiddity not essentially relative but to which relation attaches (e.g., the head), and (B) that which has no quiddity except being relative (e.g., fatherhood).
Type A is considered a relative only accidentally; Type B is the true relative (al-mudaf al-haqiqi).
2
Apply the quiddity test
Ask whether the entity has any meaning or essence apart from its relation. If yes (like the head, which is a specific body part), it is an accidental relative. If no (like fatherhood, which is pure relation), it is a true relative.
The author says the true relative 'has no existence except as something relative'.
3
Understand the repeated relation condition
The author introduces the concept of 'repeated relation' (al-nisba al-mukarrara): a thing becomes a true relative not just by having a relation, but by having a relation considered from the perspective of having that relation. This additional consideration distinguishes genuine relatives from mere relationality.
Example: The ceiling has a relation to the wall (support), but only when considered as resting on the wall does it become relative. However, it still has a quiddity, so it is not a true relative.
4
Classify examples using the criteria
Practice classifying examples: brotherhood (true relative), head (accidental), knowledge (accidental, as it is a quality with an essence), ceiling (accidental). Validate each using the quiddity test and the repeated relation condition.
The author explicitly contrasts brotherhood (pure relation) with the ceiling (which has its own quiddity).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأمور المشتركة في هذين التعريفين على ضربين أحدهما ما له ماهية ليست مضافة من حيث ذاتها ولكن يلحقها الإضافة كالرأس مثلا فإن له ماهية هو بها جسم مخصوص، وذلك لا يكون مضافًا حقيقيا من هذا الوجه؛ ثم يلحقه إضافة إلى البدن الذي هو رأسه؛ وهذا الضرب لا يكون مضافًا. وثانيهما هو الذي ليس له ماهية سوى أنه مضاف أي معقولة الماهية بالقياس إلى غيره الأبوة مثلا لا كالأب فليس لها ماهية سوى القياس والإضافة إلى البنوة؛ وهذا هو المضاف الحقيقي وهو الذي لا وجود له سوی ما به مضاف. والضرب الأول من المضاف ؛ فذلك بالنظر إلى ما يعرض له من الإضافة إلى غيره لا بالنظر إلى ماهيته فذلك هو الحقيقي أيضًا. والحقيقي في الحقيقة هو الذي لا قوام له بذاته بل هو عارض لغيره من الماهيات. فإذا قطع النظر عن الماهية نفي من الجملة ما هو نفس المضاف الحقيقي. والجملة أعني الماهية مع ما يعرض لها من الإضافة هي من القسم الأول الذي ليس بمضاف حقيقي.
ثم الوقوف على المضافات أوقع في الذهن من الوقوف على مجرد الإضافات التي هي المقولة، والأمور التي هي من المضافات فهي الأمور التي ماهياتها مقولة بالقياس إلى غيرها على الإطلاق أو بنحو آخر من أنحاء النسبة. والتي على الإطلاق فهي الأمور التي أسماؤها تدل على كمال معناها من حيث هي مضافة نحو الأخ. وأما التي بنحو آخر فهي التي تعلق بها النسبة فيصير لذلك مضافة مثل القوة من حيث هي لذي قوة والعلم من حيث هو للعالم: فإنهما وما هو بمثلهما في الذات من الكيفيات وإن كانت مضافة؛ فالعلم والقدرة وما أشبه ذلك. وإن كان كله مضافًا فكله في نفسه غير مضاف بل لما لحق به نحو من أنحاء النسبة صار مضافًا؛ وذلك بسبب حرف يدخل كما يدخل بين الإنسان والدار لفظة بسبب ما فتصير بها إضافة بين الدار وذي الدار، وربما كانت هذه النسبة متضمنة في لفظ أحد الجانبين؛ والجانب الآخر يحتاج إلى إلحاق لفظ النسبة كما في قولنا وذو الجناح، وربما كان حرف الإضافة مختلفًا فيهما مثل قولك إن العلم علم للعالم والعالم عالم لا للعلم بل بالعلم.
وقوم يقولون إن معنى قولهم أو نحو آخر إنما هو لما لا يتاشبه فيه الحروف العاكسة»، وسيتضح لك عن قريب ما قلناه وإنه أولى من التأويلات المذكورة. وأما كون الشيء مقولاً بالقياس إلى غيره فهو أن يكون الشيء إذا قصد تصور معناه أحوج تصوره إلى تصور شيء خارج عنه ولا كيف كان؛ فإن السقف إذا تصور معناه تصور معه معنى الحائط الذي يقله ولا تكون ماهية السقف مقولة بالقياس إلى الحائط ولكن يجب أن يكون للمعنى المعقول الذي للشيء الذي يحوج إلى أن يعقل معه غيره إنما هو له من أجل وجود ذلك الغير بإزائه كما في الأخ. فإن المفهوم من الأخوة لا يكون مختصا بأحد الجانبين؛ وليس كل نسبة إضافة فإن لكل شيء نسبة في الذهن إلى الأمر الذي يلزمه في الذهن لكن لا يكون ذلك إضافة. فإذا أخذت النسبة مكررة في كل شيء صار له إضافة؛ والمعنى من كونها مكررة أن يكون النظر لا في النسبة فقط بل بزيادة اعتبار النظر إلى أن للشيء نسبة من حيث له نسبة. وإلى المنسوب إليه كذلك كما في السقف فإن له نسبة إلى الحائط. فإذا نظر إليه من حيث إنه مستقر على الحائط صار مضافًا لا إلى الحائط من حيث هو حائط بل إلى الحائط من حيث إنه مستقر عليه فعلاقة السقف بالحائط من حيث إنه حائط نسبة، ومن حيث إنه منسوب إليه بالاستقرار عليه؛ والسقف نفسه
The common matters in these two definitions are of two types: one of them is that which has a quiddity that is not relative in its essence, but relation attaches to it, such as the head for example. For it has a quiddity by which it is a specific body, and that is not a true relative from this aspect; then a relation attaches to it to the body which is its head; and this type is not relative. And the second is that which has no quiddity except that it is relative, i.e., its quiddity is intelligible by relation to another, like fatherhood for example, not like the father, for it has no quiddity except the relation and addition to sonhood; and this is the true relative, which has no existence except as something relative. And the first type of relative: that is with respect to what occurs to it of relation to another, not with respect to its quiddity, so that is also the true [relative]. And the true in reality is that which has no subsistence in itself but is an accident to other quiddities. So if one abstracts from the quiddity, what is the true relative itself is negated from the compound. And the compound, i.e., the quiddity together with what attaches to it of relation, is from the first division which is not a true relative.
Then the understanding of the relatives is more impactful in the mind than the understanding of mere relations which are the category. And the things that are relatives are those whose quiddities are predicated in relation to another either absolutely or in another way of the ways of relation. Those that are absolute are the things whose names indicate their complete meaning as relative, such as 'brother'. As for those that are in another way, they are those to which relation attaches and thereby become relative, such as power insofar as it is for the powerful, and knowledge insofar as it is for the knower. For they and what is like them in essence are qualities, even if they are relative. For knowledge and power and the like, even if all is relative, all in itself is not relative, but when a way of relation attaches to it, it becomes relative. That is due to a particle that enters, just as between man and house enters the word 'by means of something', thereby a relation occurs between the house and the owner of the house. And perhaps this relation is implicit in the word of one of the two sides, while the other side needs to have the relation word attached, as in our saying 'and the possessor of the wing'. And perhaps the relational particle differs in them, as in your saying: 'knowledge is knowledge for the knower, and the knower is knower not for knowledge but by knowledge.'
And some people say that the meaning of their saying 'or in another way' is only for that in which the reflective letters are not similar. And it will become clear to you soon what we have said, and that it is more appropriate than the mentioned interpretations. As for the thing being predicated in relation to another, it is that when one intends to conceive its meaning, its conception necessitates conceiving something external to it, and not in any way. For the ceiling, when its meaning is conceived, the meaning of the wall that supports it is conceived with it, but the quiddity of the ceiling is not predicated in relation to the wall. Rather, it must be that the intelligible meaning of the thing that necessitates conceiving another with it is only because of the existence of that other in front of it, as in 'brother'. For the concept of brotherhood is not specific to one of the two sides. And not every relation is addition (idafa), for every thing has a relation in the mind to the thing that accompanies it in the mind, but that is not idafa. So when the relation is taken as repeated in every thing, it becomes idafa. And the meaning of its being repeated is that the consideration is not only of the relation, but with an additional consideration that the thing has a relation insofar as it has a relation, and similarly to the relatum. As in the ceiling, for it has a relation to the wall. So if it is considered insofar as it is resting on the wall, it becomes relative, not to the wall as wall, but to the wall insofar as it is resting on it. So the connection of the ceiling to the wall as wall is a relation, and insofar as it is related to it by resting on it, and the ceiling itself.
Source pages p. 252

Commentary
This passage from Mantiq Kabir 1 is a definitional and analytical discussion of the category of the relative (al-mudaf). The author begins by stating that the common matters in two definitions (likely of relative things) are of two types. The first type has a quiddity that is not essentially relative, but to which relation attaches accidentally, like the head (al-ra's). The head has a quiddity as a specific body part, and then it acquires a relation to the body. This type, the author says, is not truly relative. The second type has no quiddity except that it is relative; its quiddity is intelligible only by reference to something else. Examples include fatherhood (al-ubuwwa), which has no essence besides the relation to sonhood. This is the true relative (al-mudaf al-haqiqi), which has no subsistence in itself but is an accident that attaches to other quiddities. If we abstract from the quiddity, we negate the true relative altogether. The compound of quiddity and relation belongs to the first type, not the true relative.

Then the author discusses that grasping the relatives (mudafat) is more vivid in the mind than grasping mere relations (idafat) which are the category. Things that are relatives are those whose quiddities are predicated in relation to another either absolutely or in another way. Absolute relatives are those whose names indicate their complete meaning as relative, such as 'brother'. The other type are those to which a relation attaches, making them relative, such as power (quwwa) as related to the powerful, or knowledge as related to the knower. These, though they become relative, are in themselves qualities (kayfiyyat) not essentially relative. The relation comes through a preposition (harf) that enters between them, like 'bi-sabab' between man and house, making a relation between house and owner. Sometimes the relation is implicit in one term, and the other side requires the relation particle. The author also mentions a group of people (qawm) who interpret 'or in another way' as referring to cases where the relational letters are not similar. The author asserts that his view is clearer and superior to other interpretations.

Then the author explains the condition for something being 'maqul bi al-qiyas ila ghayrihi' (predicated in relation to another). It means that when one intends to conceive its meaning, its conception necessitates conceiving something outside it, but not just any condition. For example, the ceiling (saqf), when conceived, brings to mind the wall that supports it, but the quiddity of the ceiling is not predicated in relation to the wall. Rather, for something to be truly relative, its intelligible meaning must be such that it requires conceiving another alongside it, and that requirement is because of the existence of that other in relation to it, as in brotherhood. Not every relation is idafa; every thing has a relation in the mind to its concomitant, but that does not make it an idafa. When the relation is taken as 'repeated' (mukarrara), the thing becomes an idafa. 'Mukarrara' means considering not just the relation but additionally considering that the thing has a relation insofar as it has a relation, and similarly for the relatum. For the ceiling, it has a relation to the wall. If we consider it insofar as it is resting on the wall, it becomes relative, not to the wall as wall but to the wall as something upon which it rests. So the connection of the ceiling to the wall as wall is a relation (nisba), and from the aspect of being related by resting, it becomes a relative (mudaf).

Key Terms
المضاف
الإضافة
النسبة
الماهية

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a true relative (mudaf haqiqi)?
A) The head (al-ra's) because it is related to the body.
B) Fatherhood (al-ubuwwa) because it has no quiddity except relation.
C) Knowledge (al-'ilm) because it is related to the knower.
D) The ceiling (al-saqf) because it is related to the wall.

Key Takeaway
The true relative is that whose essence is entirely relational; accidental relatives have an independent quiddity that relation merely attaches to.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Determine whether each of the following is a true relative (mudaf haqiqi) or an accidental relative (mudaf bi al-‘arad): (a) knowledge, (b) brotherhood, (c) a head, (d) power. Justify your answer using the author's criteria of quiddity and repeated relation.
?
Explain why the ceiling (al-saqf) is not a true relative to the wall, even though it has a relation of support. Use the concept of repeated relation (al-nisba al-mukarrara) in your explanation.

Distinguishing Relation from Addition
Definition of the relative and distinction from mere relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a mere relation (nisba) and a two-sided addition (idāfa) using the criterion of reversal (in'ikās).

Core Idea
A relation becomes an addition only when it is considered from both sides and is reciprocal.

In logic, not every relation makes something relative. The author of this passage carefully defines the technical term 'relative' (mudāf) and distinguishes it from mere relation (nisba). The key lies in whether the relation is one-sided or two-sided—a distinction with significant implications for how we analyze categories in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined in the passage. The author defines 'mudāf' (relative) and also clarifies 'idāfa' (addition) as a specific type of relation.
2
Extract the core condition
Something is 'said relative to another' when conceiving its meaning necessitates conceiving something external, due to that other existing facing it. Example: brotherhood inherently involves two parties.
This condition distinguishes relative terms from absolute ones.
3
Distinguish nisba from idāfa
Not every mental relation is an addition. A relation becomes an addition only when it is 'repeated'—meaning it is considered from both sides. The author calls this reversal (in'ikās): 'relation is for one side, addition is for two sides.'
Use the ceiling-wall example: ceiling's relation to wall is one-sided unless viewed as resting on it.
4
Apply the criterion
Given a pair of terms, determine if the relation between them is reciprocal (two-sided) or merely one-sided. If reciprocal, it is an idāfa; otherwise, it is a mere nisba.
Practice with examples from kalam, e.g., creation and Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بل لما لحق به نحو من أنحاء النسبة صار مضافًا؛ وذلك بسبب حرف يدخل كما يدخل بين الإنسان والدار لفظة بسبب ما فتصير بها إضافة بين الدار وذي الدار، وربما كانت هذه النسبة متضمنة في لفظ أحد الجانبين؛ والجانب الآخر يحتاج إلى إلحاق لفظ النسبة كما في قولنا وذو الجناح، وربما كان حرف الإضافة مختلفًا فيهما مثل قولك إن العلم علم للعالم والعالم عالم لا للعلم بل بالعلم. وقوم يقولون إن معنى قولهم أو نحو آخر إنما هو لما لا يتاشبه فيه الحروف العاكسة»، وسيتضح لك عن قريب ما قلناه وإنه أولى من التأويلات المذكورة. وأما كون الشيء مقولاً بالقياس إلى غيره فهو أن يكون الشيء إذا قصد تصور معناه أحوج تصوره إلى تصور شيء خارج عنه ولا كيف كان؛ فإن السقف إذا تصور معناه تصور معه معنى الحائط الذي يقله ولا تكون ماهية السقف مقولة بالقياس إلى الحائط ولكن يجب أن يكون للمعنى المعقول الذي للشيء الذي يحوج إلى أن يعقل معه غيره إنما هو له من أجل وجود ذلك الغير بإزائه كما في الأخ. فإن المفهوم من الأخوة لا يكون مختصا بأحد الجانبين؛ وليس كل نسبة إضافة فإن لكل شيء نسبة في الذهن / إلى الأمر الذي يلزمه في الذهن لكن لا يكون ذلك إضافة. فإذا أخذت النسبة مكررة في كل شيء صار له إضافة؛ والمعنى من كونها مكررة أن يكون النظر لا في النسبة فقط بل بزيادة اعتبار النظر إلى أن للشيء نسبة من حيث له نسبة. وإلى المنسوب إليه كذلك كما في السقف فإن له نسبة إلى الحائط. فإذا نظر إليه من حيث إنه مستقر على الحائط صار مضافًا لا إلى الحائط من حيث هو حائط بل إلى الحائط من حيث إنه مستقر عليه فعلاقة السقف بالحائط من حيث إنه حائط نسبة، ومن حيث إنه منسوب إليه بالاستقرار عليه؛ والسقف نفسه منسوب إضافة. وهذا المعنى من قولهم إن النسبة تكون لطرف، والإضافة لطرفين»، وذلك بانعكاس النسبة فكل نسبة يوجد فيها الطرفان فهي إضافة.
Rather, when a type of relation attaches to something, it becomes relative; and that is due to a letter that enters, just as between man and house the word 'due to something' enters, thereby making a relation between the house and the owner of the house. And sometimes this relation is included in the word of one of the two sides, while the other side needs the attachment of the word of relation, as in our saying 'the one with the wing.' And sometimes the letter of relation differs in both, such as your saying 'knowledge is knowledge for the knower, and the knower is knower not for knowledge but by knowledge.' And some people say that the meaning of their saying 'or another aspect' is only for that in which the 'reflecting letters' are not similar; and soon it will become clear to you what we have said and that it is more appropriate than the mentioned interpretations. As for something being said relative to another, it is that when a thing intends to conceive its meaning, its conception necessitates the conception of something external to it, no matter how; for the ceiling, when its meaning is conceived, the meaning of the wall that carries it is conceived along with it, but the quiddity of the ceiling is not said relative to the wall; rather, the intellectual meaning of the thing that necessitates conceiving something else along with it must be that it is for it on account of the existence of that other facing it, as in brotherhood. For the concept of brotherhood is not exclusive to one of the two sides. And not every relation is an addition; for every thing has a relation in the mind to the thing that accompanies it in the mind, but that is not an addition. So when the relation is taken as 'repeated' in every thing, it becomes an addition for it. The meaning of its being 'repeated' is that the consideration is not only of the relation, but with the additional consideration of the fact that the thing has a relation from the aspect of having a relation, and similarly for the relative. As in the ceiling: it has a relation to the wall. So if it is considered from the aspect that it is resting on the wall, it becomes relative — not to the wall qua wall, but to the wall from the aspect that it is resting on it. So the relation of the ceiling to the wall from the aspect of its being a wall is a relation, and from the aspect of its being relative to it by resting on it, the ceiling itself is a relative with addition. And this is the meaning of their saying 'the relation is for one side, and the addition is for two sides,' and that is due to the reversal of the relation: every relation in which the two sides exist is an addition.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage defines the technical concept of 'relative' (al-mudaaf) and distinguishes it from mere relation (al-nisba). The author begins by explaining that a thing becomes relative when a relation attaches to it, often through a letter like 'li' or 'bi', giving examples such as 'the owner of the house' (dhu al-dar) and 'the one with the wing' (dhu al-janah). He notes that sometimes the relation is implicit in one term and explicit in the other. The author then remarks on a certain interpretation by some people about 'reflecting letters', promising to clarify it later. The core definition: something is 'said relative to another' (maqul bil-qiyas ila ghayrihi) when conceiving its meaning necessitates conceiving another thing external to it, and this necessity arises because that other thing exists facing it—like brotherhood, which inherently involves two parties. The author stresses that not every mental relation qualifies as an 'addition' (idaafa); only when the relation is 'repeated', meaning it is considered from both sides, does it become an addition. The example of the ceiling and wall illustrates: the ceiling has a relation to the wall, but it becomes relative only when viewed as resting on the wall, not merely as ceiling to wall. The passage concludes with the principle: 'relation is for one side, addition is for two sides', due to the reversal of the relation (in'ikās al-nisba).

Key Terms
مضاف
نسبة
إضافة
مقول بالقياس إلى غيره

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what distinguishes an 'addition' (idāfa) from a mere 'relation' (nisba)?
A) Addition involves a letter of relation.
B) Addition is reciprocal and two-sided.
C) Addition requires existence dependence.
D) Addition only occurs in substances.

Key Takeaway
A relation becomes an addition (idāfa) only when it is reciprocal and involves two sides; otherwise it remains a mere relation (nisba).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether the relation between 'knowledge' (ʿilm) and 'knower' (ʿālim) in the passage is a nisba or an idāfa. Use the reversal criterion to justify your answer.
?
Consider the relation between 'creation' (khalq) and 'Creator' (Khāliq) as used in kalām. Is this relation one-sided or two-sided? Explain using the author's principle that 'relation is for one side, addition is for two sides.'

The Ten Categories

10 min
Types of Relatives and Opposition Among Them
Author enumerates types of relatives and notes that opposition occurs in all categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential relatives (whose quiddity implies relation) and accidental relatives (relative only when taken with a relation), and apply this distinction to metaphysical examples from Islamic thought.

Core Idea
Some terms are relative by their quiddity; others are relative only when taken with a relation.

The author has already defined the category of relation (mudaf). Now he expands this definition by classifying relatives into two types. Understanding this classification is crucial for avoiding a common confusion: mistaking existential dependence for relational predication. The example of the world's dependence on the Creator versus knowledge's relation to the knower clarifies the difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two classes of relatives
Look at the examples: 'greater/smaller' vs 'knowledge/sense'. Notice that 'greater' cannot be conceived without 'smaller', while 'knowledge' can be considered as a quiddity even without specifying a knower, though it exists only in a knower.
The author explicitly contrasts these two groups.
2
Extract the criterion for essential relatives
The author says 'greater is not great in itself unless there is a small'. This means the quiddity of 'greater' inherently involves a relation to something else.
Essential relatives are those whose very essence is a relation.
3
Understand the metaphysical distinction
The author contrasts existential dependence (the world exists only by the Creator) with relative predication (knowledge is said relative to the knower). Even though the world depends on the Creator, its quiddity is not relative to the Creator.
This prevents theological confusion: God's creation does not make creation's essence a relation to God.
4
Apply the rule to the example of whiteness
Whiteness is relative only when taken in a white object. Its quiddity (whiteness) is not inherently relative; it becomes relative when considered as an attribute of something.
This distinguishes accidental relatives from essential ones.
5
Note that opposition applies to all categories
The author lists relatives in substance (father/son), continuous quantity (big/small), discrete quantity (much/little), etc., showing that relation cuts across all categories and can have opposites.
Opposition (mudaadda) is a property of relatives, not just of substances.

Then among relative things are like the greater and the smaller, the double and the half, and among them are like power and ability and state; for power and ability are power and ability for something over something; and state is state of the possessor of the state, and sense is sense of the sentient by the sensible, and knowledge is knowledge of the knower of the known. Likewise standing is standing of the stander and sitting is sitting of the sitter. All these are relatives, but among them are like the great: it is not great in itself unless there is a small; likewise the similar and the equal. As for power, ability, sense, and knowledge, the relation is not like that; for the quiddity of knowledge is only called knowledge relative to the knower, even if it does not exist except in the knower. And there is a difference between a thing that does not exist unless something exists, and between something being said relative to something: for the world, e.g., does not exist except by the Exalted and Blessed Creator, but its quiddity is not said relative to the Creator. Likewise duality does not exist except with unity, but its quiddity is not said relative to unity. And existence and quiddity are not one thing, nor is the conjunction of quiddity with quiddity the predication of quiddity relative to quiddity; rather, the quiddity being relative is that the quiddity is in reality existing as concomitant to what is concomitant. So then some of these matters have a quiddity that is said relative to another, like the big and the small, and some of them are like that only when the quiddity is taken with the relation, like whiteness, for example: if it is taken in the white object, it becomes relative. Then relative matters are among that in which opposition exists. The rule is that the relative is something that applies to all categories: in substances like father and son, in continuous quantity like big and small, in [discrete] quantity like much and little, and in
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage expands on the previous definition by classifying relative things (al-umur al-mudafa) into different types. The author first lists examples: 'greater and smaller', 'double and half', as well as 'power', 'ability', 'state', 'sense', 'knowledge', 'standing', 'sitting'. He then distinguishes two subclasses: some relatives, like 'great', are essentially relative—they cannot be conceived without a counterpart (e.g., 'great' requires 'small'). Others, like 'knowledge', are not essentially relative; knowledge is called knowledge relative to the knower, but its quiddity does not require a counterpart in the same way. The author makes a crucial metaphysical point: there is a difference between something that exists only because of something else (like the world depends on the Creator) and something that is said relative to something (like knowledge). The world exists by the Creator, but its quiddity is not relative to Him; likewise duality exists only with unity but is not relative to unity. The author then refines the definition: a quiddity being relative means that the quiddity itself is concomitant to what is concomitant. Thus, some things have a quiddity that is inherently relative (like big/small), while others are relative only when taken with a relation (like whiteness in a white object). Finally, the author notes that relatives can possess opposition (mudaadda), and this occurs in all categories: substance (father/son), continuous quantity (big/small), discrete quantity (much/little), etc. The text is cut off, but the pattern is clear.

The Rule of Opposition in Relatives Across Categories
Author examines opposition in relatives across categories and critiques a common rule about it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given relative term pair exhibits true opposition based on the category it belongs to and whether that category admits opposition and intensity.

Core Idea
Opposition in relatives requires the underlying category to admit it.

In this passage, the author explains a standard logical rule: relatives occur in all ten categories, but true opposition (like hot/cold) only exists when the category itself allows opposition and intensity. He illustrates with examples across categories, then notes that categories like substance and quantity do not admit such opposition, so relatives like 'many' and 'few' are not true opposites.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
The rule states that relatives attach to all categories, but opposition in relatives only occurs when the category admits opposition and intensification.
2
Examine the examples
List the examples given for each category: substance (father/son), continuous quantity (large/small), discrete quantity (many/few), quality (hotter/colder), relation (nearer/farther), place (higher/lower), time (older/newer), position (more erect/more bent), possession (more clothed/more unclothed), action (more cutting/more lacerating), passion (more heated/more cut).
3
Spot the pattern
Notice that some categories like substance and quantity do not admit opposition and intensity. Therefore, the relatives in those categories (e.g., many/few, large/small) are not true opposites.
4
Understand the reasoning
The author explains that quantity cannot be more or less intense, so relatives depending on quantity cannot have opposition.
This rule later becomes the target of critique in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الأمور المضافة من جملة ما يوجد فيه المضادة. والضابط فيه هو أن المضاف مما يعرض للمقولات أجمع أما في الجواهر فكالأب والابن، وفي الكم المتصل كالكبير والصغير، وفي الكم المنفصل كالكثير والقليل، وفي الكيف كالأحر والأبرد، وفي المضاف كالأقرب والأبعد، وفي الأين كالأعلى والأسفل، وفي متى كالأقدم والأحدث، وفي الوضع كالأشد انتصابًا وانحناء، وفي الملك كالأكسى والأعرى، وفي الفعل كالأقطع والأصرم، وفي الانفعال كالأشد تسخنا وتقطعا. فما كان منها في مقولة يقبل التضاد والاشتداد والنقص؛ ففيه من التضاد والاشتداد. فلما كانت الحرارة من مقولة الكيف ضد البرودة وأشد من حرارة أخرى كان الأحر ضدًا للأبرد، وكانت حرارة أخرى أشد من أخرى أيضًا. ولما لم يكن الجوهر والكم يقبلانهما لم يقبلهما المضاف العارض لهما فليس الكثير ضدًا للصغير، ولا الضعف ضدا للنصف. وكذلك الحال في قبول الأشد والأضعف والأقل والأكثر. وقد يقال إن غير المساوي قد يكون أكثر وأقل لما أن الكمية قد تكون أكثر وأقل كما أن النسبة تكون أشد وأضعف لما أن الكيفية تكون أشد وأضعف لكنه وهو غير المساوي لا يكون في الحقيقة أشد وأضعف بل قد يكون أقرب وأبعد. فإن العشرة مثلا أبعد في المساواة عن الثلاثة من التسعة. والسبب في الأمرين أعني المحال والممكن ما مر من قبل أن الكم لا يمكن أن يكون أشد وأضعف أصلا. ثم هذا الضابط وإن كان مسطورًا في الكتب، ففيه نظر فإن منهم من ذهب إلى هذا الضابط وكان يقول إن المضاف وإن عرض للكيفية فليست الكيفية داخلة فيه بل هو نفس كون الكيفية مقيسة إلى ما هو بإزائها لضد طبيعة وماهية معقولة بنفسها ثم يعرض لها إضافة الضد به والمضاف لا ماهية له سوى أن يكون مقيسًا فلا يعرض له التضاد الذي يستدعي طبيعة معقولة بنفسها يكون هو عارضًا لها. وقد ذكروا أيضًا إن الكبير ليس ضدًا للصغير لأنه ليس له طبيعة معقولة بنفسها سوى أنه مضاف فليس الأحر والأبرد أيضًا طبيعة سوى أنه مضاف. وإن قالوا يكفي لعروض الضدية طبيعة غير الضدية موضوعة لها.
Then relative things are among those in which opposition occurs. The rule for it is that the relative attaches to all categories: in substances like father and son, in continuous quantity like large and small, in discrete quantity like many and few, in quality like hotter and colder, in relation like nearer and farther, in place like higher and lower, in time like older and newer, in position like more erect and more bent, in possession like more clothed and more unclothed, in action like more cutting and more lacerating, in passion like more heated and more cut. So whatever is in a category that admits opposition and intensification and diminution, there is opposition and intensification in them. Since heat is in the category of quality, opposite to cold, and hotter than another heat, so the hotter is opposite to the colder, and one heat is more intense than another. And since substance and quantity do not admit them, the relative that attaches to them does not admit them either; so many is not opposite to few, nor double opposite to half. Likewise with the acceptance of more and less intense, and more and less. It may be said that the unequal may be more and less because quantity may be more and less, just as relation may be more and less intense because quality may be more and less intense; but the unequal is not in reality more or less intense, but may be nearer or farther. For example, ten is farther from equality with three than nine is. And the reason for both matters, i.e. the impossible and the possible, is what has passed earlier: that quantity cannot be more or less intense at all. Then this rule, although recorded in the books, is questionable. For among them is one who held this rule and used to say that the relative, even if it attaches to quality, yet the quality is not included in it; rather it is the very fact that the quality is measured against its opposite, for the nature and essence are intellectually conceived in themselves, then the relation of opposition attaches to them; but the relative has no essence other than being measured, so opposition does not attach to it, because opposition requires an essence intellectually conceived in itself to which it attaches. And they also mentioned that the large is not opposite to the small because it has no essence intellectually conceived in itself other than being relative; so the hotter and colder also have no essence other than being relative. And if they say that it suffices for the occurrence of opposition that there be a nature other than opposition that underlies it.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of opposition in relative terms. The author first presents the standard rule that relatives occur in all ten categories and can have opposition when the category admits opposition and intensity. He gives examples: in substance (father/son), in continuous quantity (large/small), etc. However, he then notes that substance and quantity do not admit opposition, so relatives in those categories (like many/few) are not true opposites. The author then reports a refinement: some philosophers argue that even when a relative attaches to quality (like hotter/colder), the opposition does not belong to the relative itself but to the underlying quality. The relative term (e.g., 'hotter') has no essence other than being measured relative to something else, so it cannot itself be opposite. The author finds this rule questionable ('فيه نظر') and presents a counter-argument: if large and small are not opposites because they lack a self-subsistent essence, then by the same logic hotter and colder also lack such an essence. The passage ends with a hypothetical objection: if someone says that opposition can attach to a nature that is not itself opposition, that might salvage the rule, but the author leaves it as an open critique.

Key Takeaway
The rule of opposition in relatives is conditional on the underlying category admitting opposition and intensity; relatives in categories without these properties are not true opposites.

Critique of the Standard Rule: Do Relatives Have Their Own Opposition?
Author examines opposition in relatives across categories and critiques a common rule about it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the logical critique against the standard rule on opposition in relatives and evaluate its implications.

Core Idea
If relatives have no essence of their own, they cannot be true opposites—even the standard rule's examples fail.

After presenting the standard rule, the author reveals that it is not unassailable. Some philosophers argue that relatives have no self-subsistent essence; they are merely measured against something else. Therefore, opposition cannot properly belong to them but must belong to the underlying quality. The author finds this rule questionable and presents a counter-argument: if we deny that large/small are opposites due to lacking essence, then we must also deny that hotter/colder are opposites, because they equally lack essence.

Guided Path
1
State the rule being critiqued
The rule claims that relatives can have opposition when their category admits it, e.g., hotter/colder in quality.
2
Identify the objection
The objector says: the relative has no essence other than being measured (muqays), so it cannot be the subject of opposition. The opposition belongs to the underlying quality, not the relative term.
3
Follow the counter-argument
The author argues: if we accept that large/small are not opposites because they lack essence, then by the same logic hotter/colder also lack essence and thus are not true opposites. This reveals inconsistency.
4
Consider the hypothetical rescue
The passage ends with a possible reply: opposition can attach to a nature that is not itself opposition (i.e., the quality). The author leaves this open, implying the rule is still questionable.

The Core Rule: Quality vs. Relation on Contrariety and Intensity
Author explains how quality admits contrariety and intensity while relation does not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which categories admit contrariety and intensity based on the criterion of having a self-subsistent nature, and distinguish quality from relation in this regard.

Core Idea
Contrariety and intensity require a self-subsistent nature; relation lacks one, so it cannot be contrary or intense.

In this passage, the author lays out the fundamental rule: only categories that possess a nature independent of relation can admit contrariety and intensity. Quality is the prime example, while relation is excluded because it is mere comparison. Understanding this distinction is crucial for classifying concepts in Islamic logic and sciences.

Guided Path
1
List the examples of contrariety/intensity for each category
Identify the examples given in the Arabic: for quality (hotter/colder), relation (closer/farther), where (higher/lower), when (older/newer), position (more erect/more bent), possession (more dressed/more naked), action (more cutting/more breaking), affection (more heated/more cut). Notice that many categories have examples, but not all admit contrariety and intensity equally.
2
Identify categories that actually accept contrariety and intensity
The author states that only categories which inherently accept these properties will display them. From the examples, quality is clearly one such category. Substance and quantity do not accept them, and relation, being accidental to them, also does not. Extract the explicit statement: 'since substance and quantity do not accept them, relation that is accidental to them does not accept them either.'
3
Understand why relation lacks a self-subsistent nature
Relation is defined as having no essence other than being measured toward another. Contrariety (like hot/cold) requires a substrate nature that can receive opposite forms. Since relation has no independent nature, it cannot be contrary or admit intensity. Confirm this by finding the author's phrase: 'relation has no essence other than being measured.'
4
Formulate the general principle
Synthesize: A category admits contrariety and intensity if and only if it has a self-subsistent nature (a 'nature intelligible in itself'). Quality qualifies; relation does not. This principle is applied to other categories like substance and quantity which also lack these properties, so relation inherits their lack.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكيف كالأحر والأبرد، وفي المضاف كالأقرب والأبعد، وفي الأين كالأعلى والأسفل، وفي متى كالأقدم والأحدث، وفي الوضع كالأشد انتصابًا وانحناء وفي الملك كالأكسى والأعرى، وفي الفعل كالأقطع والأصرم، وفي الانفعال كالأشد تسخنًا وتقطعًا. فما كان منها في مقولة يقبل التضاد والاشتداد والنقص؛ ففيه من التضاد والاشتداد. فلما كانت الحرارة من مقولة الكيف ضد البرودة وأشد من حرارة أخرى كان الأحر ضدًا للأبرد، وكانت حرارة أخرى أشد من أخرى أيضًا. ولما لم يكن الجوهر والكم يقبلانهما لم يقبلهما المضاف العارض لهما فليس الكثير ضدًا للصغير، ولا الضعف ضدا للنصف. وكذلك الحال في قبول الأشد والأضعف والأقل والأكثر. وقد يقال إن غير المساوي قد يكون أكثر وأقل لما أن الكمية قد تكون أكثر وأقل كما أن النسبة تكون أشد وأضعف لما أن الكيفية تكون أشد وأضعف لكنه وهو غير المساوي لا يكون في الحقيقة أشد وأضعف بل قد يكون أقرب وأبعد. فإن العشرة مثلًا أبعد في المساواة عن الثلاثة من التسعة. والسبب في الأمرين أعني المحال والممكن ما مر من قبل أن الكم لا يمكن أن يكون أشد وأضعف أصلًا. ثم هذا الضابط وإن كان مسطورًا في الكتب، ففيه نظر فإن منهم من ذهب إلى هذا الضابط وكان يقول إن المضاف وإن عرض للكيفية فليست الكيفية داخلة فيه بل هو نفس كون الكيفية مقيسة إلى ما هو بإزائها لضد طبيعة وماهية معقولة بنفسها ثم يعرض لها إضافة الضد به والمضاف لا ماهية له سوى أن يكون مقيسًا فلا يعرض له التضاد الذي يستدعي طبيعة معقولة بنفسها يكون هو عارضًا لها. وقد ذكروا أيضًا إن الكبير ليس ضدًا للصغير لأنه ليس له طبيعة معقولة بنفسها سوى أنه مضاف فليس الأحر والأبرد أيضًا طبيعة سوى أنه مضاف. وإن قالوا يكفي لعروض الضدية طبيعة غير الضدية موضوعة لها مضافة كانت أو غير مضافة؛ وللأحر طبيعة غير الضدية يعرض لها الضدية، وللكبير والصغير أيضًا طبيعتان سوى الضدية وبما كونهما مضافين بهذا الناقض في الموضعين.
For quality, like hotter and colder; and in relation, like closer and farther; and in where, like higher and lower; and in when, like older and newer; and in position, like more erect and more bent; and in possession, like more dressed and more naked; and in action, like more cutting and more breaking; and in affection, like more heated and more cut. So whatever of these categories accepts contrariety and intensity and deficiency, then in it there is contrariety and intensity. Since heat is from the category of quality and is contrary to cold and more intense than another heat, then hotter is contrary to colder, and one heat is more intense than another. And since substance and quantity do not accept them, relation that is accidental to them does not accept them either. So many is not contrary to few, nor is double contrary to half. Similarly for accepting more intense and weaker, and more and less. And it is said that the non-equal may be more and less because quantity may be more and less just as relation may be more intense and weaker because quality may be more intense and weaker. But the non-equal is not in reality more intense and weaker, but rather it may be closer and farther. For example, ten is farther from equality to three than nine is. And the reason for both matters, i.e., impossible and possible, is what has passed before: that quantity cannot be more intense and weaker at all. Then this rule, although it is recorded in the books, there is consideration in it. For some of them adhered to this rule and said that relation, although it occurs to quality, quality is not included in it; rather it is the very fact of quality being measured against what is opposite to it, for a nature and essence intelligible in itself, then the addition of the contrary occurs to it. And relation has no essence other than being measured, so contrariety which requires an essence intelligible in itself to which it occurs does not occur to it. And they also mentioned that big is not contrary to small because it has no essence intelligible in itself except that it is relative. So hotter and colder also have no nature except that they are relative. And if they said: 'It suffices for the occurrence of contrariety a nature other than contrariety that is subject to it, whether relative or non-relative; for hotter there is a nature other than contrariety to which contrariety occurs, and for big and small also there are two natures other than contrariety, and by virtue of their being relative' — with this refutation in both places.
Source pages p. 255

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of how different categories relate to the properties of contrariety (tadadd) and intensity (ishtidad). The author begins by listing examples for each category: in quality (kayf) like hotter and colder; in relation (mudaf) like closer and farther; in where (ayn) like higher and lower; etc. He then explains that any category that inherently accepts contrariety and intensity will display them. For instance, heat, being a quality, is contrary to cold and can be more or less intense. Substance (jawhar) and quantity (kamm) do not admit these properties; therefore relation, which is accidental to them, also does not. Thus 'many' is not contrary to 'few' nor 'double' to 'half'. The author acknowledges a nuance: terms like 'non-equal' can be described as 'more' or 'less', but this is a quantitative comparison, not intensity proper. For example, ten is farther from equality with three than nine is, but this is a matter of distance, not intensity. The reason is that quantity cannot be intense or weak. The author then notes that this commonly accepted rule has been contested. Some thinkers argue that relation has no essence of its own—it is merely a consideration—so contrariety, which requires a self-subsistent nature, cannot occur to it. For instance, 'big' and 'small' are not contraries because they are purely relative. However, an opponent may object that terms like 'hotter' do have a nature (heat) that serves as substrate for contrariety, while 'big' and 'small' also have natures (size) that are not themselves the relation. The author ends by indicating that this objection constitutes a refutation (naqd) of the original view, leaving the matter with a consideration of both sides.

Key Terms
الكيف
المضاف
التضاد
الاشتداد
الطبيعة

B) Quantity (kamm)
C) Quality (kayf)
D) Relation (mudaf)

Key Takeaway
The categorical distinction between quality and relation on contrariety and intensity rests on the presence or absence of a self-subsistent nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Objections and Nuances: Can Relation Admit Degrees?
Author discusses and counters objections about relation admitting degrees.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the objection that relation can be described as 'more' or 'less' and evaluate the philosophical debate about whether relation requires a substrate nature to receive contrariety.

Core Idea
Even if relation appears to admit degrees, it is actually quantitative distance, not intensity, because relation lacks its own nature.

The author anticipates an objection: terms like 'non-equal' are said to be more or less. He responds by clarifying that this is a quantitative comparison, not intensity. Then he delves into a deeper debate about whether relation needs a self-subsistent nature to receive contrariety, presenting both sides and a refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection about 'non-equal'
Find the objection in the Arabic: 'وقد يقال إن غير المساوي قد يكون أكثر وأقل' (And it is said that the non-equal may be more and less). The objector points out that we use comparatives like 'more unequal' as if intensity applies to relation.
2
Understand the author's response: quantitative distance vs. intensity
The author responds that non-equal is not in reality more intense and weaker, but rather it is 'closer and farther' (أقرب وأبعد). For example, ten is farther from equality with three than nine is; this is a quantitative distance on a scale, not an intensity intrinsic to relation. The reason is that quantity cannot be more or less intense (as established earlier).
3
Examine the deeper debate about relation and contrariety
The author then notes that the rule about relation has been contested. Some thinkers (the 'adhherents' of the rule) say that even though relation occurs to quality, it is not included in quality; rather it is the comparison. They argue that relation has no essence other than being measured, so contrariety requiring a self-subsistent nature cannot occur. Hence 'big' is not contrary to 'small' because they have no independent nature.
4
Analyze the refutation (naqd) using 'hotter' and 'bigger'
An opponent objects: 'hotter' has a nature (heat) that is the substrate for contrariety. Similarly, 'big' and 'small' have natures (size) that are not themselves the relation. So both have natures other than contrariety to which contrariety can occur. The author presents this as a refutation, leaving the matter open for consideration.

Reciprocity and Conversion of Relatives
Author explains that all relatives reciprocate and convert under the condition of direct essential relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to test whether a given relative pair reciprocates by verifying that the relation is direct and essential.

Core Idea
Only direct and essential relatives reciprocate – accidental relations break the conversion.

In this passage, the author explains a key property of relative terms (المضافات): they reciprocate and convert upon each other, but with an important condition. The reciprocity only holds when the relation is primary and essential (بالتعادل). Understanding this condition helps us correctly analyze pairs like master/slave vs. head/man, and avoid false logical inferences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the relative pair
Find the two terms that are related, e.g., father and son, master and slave, head and man.
The relation is typically expressed as 'A is the relative of B'.
2
Check the nature of the relation
Determine if the relation is direct (to the thing itself) or indirect (to a subject, accident, genus, or species). For example, 'father of son' is direct; 'head of man' is indirect because 'head' belongs to the one having a head, not to 'man' as such.
The author calls this condition 'التعادل' (equality or directness).
3
Test conversion
Try swapping the terms to form 'B is the relative of A'. Does it make sense without adding extra words or changing meaning? For 'father of son', we get 'son of father' directly. For 'slave of master', we get 'master of slave' after repeating terms.
Some conversions require repetition or extra wording, like 'sensation by the sensed'.
4
Apply the condition
If the relation is direct and essential, reciprocity holds. If indirect, it fails. For example, 'wing of bird' does not convert to 'bird of wing' because the relation is to the possessor, not directly to bird.
This property is a necessary concomitant only for direct essential relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في الخواص فمنها أن المضافات كلها يرجع بعضها على بعض ] وأما الخواص فمنها أن المضافات كلها يرجع بعضها على بعض بالتكافؤ، وينعكس بعضها على بعض. ووجه ذلك الرجوع مخالف لوجه رجوع الحمل على الوضع. وذلك لأن الوضع هنا يكون من أحد الطرفين مكررا والحمل في الطرف الآخر؛ فإذا عكست صار الحمل وضعًا والوضع حملا. وقد ألحق مثل ذلك التكرار في جانبه. وقد حذف عنه فيقال إن العبد عبد المولى؛ ثم يقال والمولى مولى العبد فتكرر العبد في الأول والمولى في الثاني ففي البعض يحتاج أن يلحق بالطرف المحمول شيئًا زائدا كالكلام في المولى والعبد وكذلك حين تقول الحس حس بالمحسوس، والمحسوس محسوس بالحس؛ وفي البعض لا يحتاج إلى ذلك فإنك تقول إن الأب أبو الابن والابن ابن الأب. وأما سائر العكوس فسيأتيك كلّ في موضعه فيخالف الذي للمضاف في ذلك كله لكن هذا التكافؤ من جملة ما يكون من اللوازم. وذلك إن الإضافة إذا لم تقع على التعادل لم يجب هذا التكافؤ، ووقوعها على التعادل هو أن تقع إلى الشيء الذي إليه الإضافة أولا وبالذات؛ فإنها إذا وقعت إلى موضوعه أو إلى أمر يعرض له أو إلى جنسه أو نوعه لم تقع الإضافة متكافئة فإنك إذا قلت إن الرأس رأس الإنسان، أو الجناح جناح الطائر فلا يمكنك أن تقول إن الإنسان إنسان بالقياس إلى الرأس، والطائر طائر بالقياس إلى الجناح؛ إذ الرأس رأس لذي الرأس، والجناح جناح لذي الجناح.
Section on properties: among them is that all relatives return upon each other with reciprocity, and convert upon each other. The manner of that return is different from the manner of the return of predication to subject. That is because the subject here is repeated from one side and the predicate in the other side; if you convert, the predicate becomes subject and the subject becomes predicate. Such repetition is added on its side. And it is omitted in some cases, so it is said: the slave is the slave of the master; then it is said: and the master is the master of the slave. So the slave is repeated in the first and the master in the second. So in some cases it is necessary to add something extra to the predicated side, like the speech about master and slave. Likewise when you say: sensation is sensation by the sensed, and the sensed is sensed by sensation. And in some cases it is not necessary: for you say: the father is father of the son, and the son is son of the father. As for other conversions, each will come in its place, and it differs from that which is for the relative in all that. But this reciprocity is among the necessary concomitants. That is because if the relation is not based on equality, this reciprocity is not necessary. Its occurrence on equality is that it occurs to the thing to which the relation is directed primarily and essentially. For if it occurs to its subject or to something accidental to it or to its genus or species, the relation does not occur reciprocally. For if you say: the head is head of the man, or the wing is wing of the bird, you cannot say: the man is man in relation to the head, or the bird is bird in relation to the wing, because the head is head of the one having the head, and the wing is wing of the one having the wing.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage discusses a logical property (khaassa) of relative terms (mudafaat). The author states that all relatives reciprocate (takaafu') and convert (in'ikaas) upon each other. He distinguishes this from the ordinary predication (haml) where subject and predicate are distinct. In the case of relatives, converting a statement like 'A is the relation of B' to 'B is the relation of A' involves repetition of terms. For example, 'the slave is slave of the master' becomes 'the master is master of the slave'—here 'slave' and 'master' are each repeated. In some cases, extra wording is needed (like 'by' in 'sensation by the sensed'), while in others, like 'father of son', it is straightforward. The author then explains the condition for such reciprocity: the relation must be direct and essential (bi al-dhat). If the relation is to a subject (like 'head of man'), an accident, genus, or species, reciprocity fails. For instance, 'head of man' does not convert to 'man of head' because 'head' relates to the one having a head, not to 'man' as such. Thus, this property is a necessary concomitant only for relations that are primary and essential.

Key Terms
المضافات
التكافؤ
الانعكاس
التعادل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following pairs illustrates a relation that properly reciprocates according to the passage?
A) Head of man : man of head
B) Wing of bird : bird of wing
C) Slave of master : master of slave
D) Sensation of the sensed : sensed of sensation

Key Takeaway
Reciprocity of relatives is a necessary property only when the relation is direct and essential (بالتعادل), otherwise conversion yields nonsense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the pair 'Creator and creation' (خالق ومخلوق). Does it properly reciprocate according to the condition? Write out the conversion and justify your answer.
?
Find an example from Arabic grammar or Fiqh where a relation does not reciprocate because it is indirect (e.g., part-whole). Explain why.

Equitable Relation and Mutual Equivalence in Relationals
Author explains mutual equivalence in relationals and the condition of equitable relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a relational pair exhibits equitable relation (taʿādul) and determine if mutual equivalence (takāfuʾ) holds using the author's method.

Core Idea
Relational terms reflect mutually only when the relation is direct and essential, not accidental.

The author of this passage investigates a key property of relational terms: they reflect on each other mutually when converted. But this mutual equivalence is not automatic; it depends on whether the relation is 'equitable'—that is, whether it relates to the thing itself primarily and essentially, not through a subject, accident, genus, or species. In this lesson, you will learn to apply the author's test for equitable relation, using examples from Islamic sciences like inheritance and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the relational pair
Look at the two relational terms (e.g., father/son, master/slave). Note the direction: the first term is related to the second.
The terms must be converse pairs.
2
Check if the relation is direct and essential
Ask: is the first term related to the second directly as such, or is it related to something else (like 'the one having a head')? The author says equitable relation requires the relation to be to the thing 'first and essentially'.
For example, 'head' is related to 'the one having a head', not directly to 'human'.
3
Apply the author's test
Collect all descriptions of the thing. If removing all descriptions except the relational property preserves the relation, and removing that property while keeping others does not, then the relation is equitable.
Test with 'head': remove 'animal', 'human', 'walker' but keep 'has a head' → can still relate 'head'. Remove 'has a head' but keep the rest → cannot relate 'head'. So relation is inequitable.
4
Determine if mutual equivalence holds
If the relation is equitable, then the terms reflect equivalently: you can convert the statement and the same relation holds (e.g., father-son). If not equitable, the reflection fails.
The author illustrates with father/son (equitable) vs. head/human (inequitable).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في الخواص فمنها أن المضافات كلها يرجع بعضها على بعض ] وأما الخواص فمنها أن المضافات كلها يرجع بعضها على بعض بالتكافؤ، وينعكس بعضها على بعض. ووجه ذلك الرجوع مخالف لوجه رجوع الحمل على الوضع. وذلك لأن الوضع هنا يكون من أحد الطرفين مكررا والحمل في الطرف الآخر؛ فإذا عكست صار الحمل وضعًا والوضع حملا. وقد ألحق مثل ذلك التكرار في جانبه. وقد حذف عنه فيقال إن العبد عبد المولى؛ ثم يقال والمولى مولى العبد فتكرر العبد في الأول والمولى في الثاني ففي البعض يحتاج أن يلحق بالطرف المحمول شيئًا زائدا كالكلام في المولى والعبد وكذلك حين تقول الحس حس بالمحسوس، والمحسوس محسوس بالحس؛ وفي البعض لا يحتاج إلى ذلك فإنك تقول إن الأب أبو الابن والابن ابن الأب. وأما سائر العكوس فسيأتيك كلّ في موضعه فيخالف الذي للمضاف في ذلك كله لكن هذا التكافؤ من جملة ما يكون من اللوازم. وذلك إن الإضافة إذا لم تقع على التعادل لم يجب هذا التكافؤ، ووقوعها على التعادل هو أن تقع إلى الشيء الذي إليه الإضافة أولا وبالذات؛ فإنها إذا وقعت إلى موضوعه أو إلى أمر يعرض له أو إلى جنسه أو نوعه لم تقع الإضافة متكافئة فإنك إذا قلت إن الرأس رأس الإنسان، أو الجناح جناح الطائر فلا يمكنك أن تقول إن الإنسان إنسان بالقياس إلى الرأس، والطائر طائر بالقياس إلى الجناح؛ إذ الرأس رأس لذي الرأس، والجناح جناح لذي الجناح. وأما السبيل إلى التمييز بين ما تقع إليه الإضافة بالتعادل وبين ما تقع لا بالتعادل فذلك أن تجمع أوصاف الشيء: فإنك إذا وضعته ورفعت غيره أمكنك أن تحفظ الإضافة. وإذا رفعته ووضعت غيره لم يمكن فهو الذي إليه التعادل. وما لم يكن كذلك فهو الذي ليس إليه التعادل؛ فلو رفعت من الشيء أنه حيوان وأنه إنسان وأنه مشاء وأنه ذو شيء كيف اتفق وحفظت أنه ذو رأس أمكنك أن تضيف إليه الرأس. وإذا رفعت أنه ذو رأس وحفظت أنه حيوان وأنه إنسان وأنه كذا وكذا لم يمكن ذلك.
Chapter: On the Properties. Among them is that all relationals reflect on each other mutually and convert with each other. The manner of that reflection is contrary to the manner of the reflection of predication on position. That is because the position here is repeated from one of the two terms, and the predication is in the other term. So when you invert, predication becomes position and position becomes predication. Such repetition has been appended on its side, and has been omitted on the other. It is said: 'The slave is the master's slave'; then it is said: 'The master is the slave's master'; so the slave is repeated in the first and the master in the second. In some cases, one needs to append something extra to the predicated term, as in the discussion of master and slave; similarly when you say 'Sense is sense by the sensed' and 'The sensed is sensed by sense'. In some cases, there is no need for that: you say 'The father is the son's father' and 'The son is the father's son'. As for the rest of the inversions, each will come in its place, and they differ from what is for relationals in all that, but this equivalence is among the necessary concomitants. That is because if the relation does not fall upon equitable terms, this equivalence is not necessary. Its falling upon equitable terms is that it falls to the thing to which the relation is primarily and essentially; for if it falls to its subject or to an accident of it or to its genus or species, the relation is not equivalent. For if you say 'The head is the head of the human' or 'The wing is the wing of the bird', you cannot say 'The human is human in relation to the head' or 'The bird is bird in relation to the wing', since the head is the head of the one having a head, and the wing is the wing of the one having a wing. As for the way to distinguish between that to which the relation falls equitably and that to which it falls inequitably, it is that you gather the descriptions of the thing: if you posit it and raise its other, you can preserve the relation; and if you raise it and posit its other, you cannot, then it is that to which it is equitable. And what is not like that is that to which it is not equitable. So if you remove from the thing that it is an animal, that it is a human, that it is a walker, and that it has something however it may be, and you preserve that it has a head, you can relate the head to it. And if you remove that it has a head and preserve that it is an animal, a human, etc., you cannot do that.
Source pages p. 256

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of a property of relational terms (al-muḍāfāt). The author begins by stating that all relationals reflect on each other equivalently and convert with each other. He distinguishes this reflection from the reflection of predication on position (haml ʻalā al-waḍʻ). In relational conversion, the term that was the 'position' (subject) in the first statement becomes the 'predicate' in the second, and vice versa. He illustrates with examples: 'the slave is the master's slave' converts to 'the master is the slave's master', where both terms are repeated. Sometimes an extra word is needed, as in 'sense is sense by the sensed' converting to 'the sensed is sensed by sense'; other times no extra word is needed, as in 'the father is the son's father' and 'the son is the father's son'. The author then notes that other inversions differ from this pattern. He emphasizes that this equivalence is a necessary concomitant only when the relation falls upon equitable terms (at-taʻādul). Equitable relation occurs when the relation is to the thing primarily and essentially, not to its subject, accident, genus, or species. He gives the example of 'head' and 'human': since 'head' is related to 'the one having a head', not to 'human' directly, the relation is not equitable—you cannot say 'human is human in relation to head'. Finally, he provides a method to test equitable relation: collect all descriptions of the thing; if removing all descriptions except the relational property still allows the relation, and if removing that property while keeping others does not, then the relation is equitable. He applies this to the head example: if you remove 'animal', 'human', 'walker', etc., but keep 'having a head', you can still relate 'head'; but if you remove 'having a head' and keep the rest, you cannot. This method distinguishes equitable from inequitable relations.

Key Terms
المضافات
التكافؤ
التعادل
العكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, which of the following relational pairs exhibits mutual equivalence (التكافؤ)?
A) Head and human
B) Wing and bird
C) Father and son
D) Slave and master with extra words needed

Key Takeaway
Mutual equivalence in relationals is a necessary concomitant only when the relation is equitable—direct and essential, not through a subject, accident, genus, or species.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Examine the relational pair 'teacher-student' (muʿallim-tilmīdh). Apply the author's test to determine if the relation is equitable. Justify your answer and state whether mutual equivalence holds.
?
In a Kalam argument, a theologian says: 'The Creator is the Creator of the created, and the created is created by the Creator.' Test whether this pair exhibits equitable relation using the method described in this lesson.

The Coexistence Property of Correlatives
Author explains and nuances the coexistence property of correlatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the property that correlatives coexist in existence and non-existence, and you will be able to recognize exceptions where this property does not hold symmetrically.

Core Idea
Correlatives rise and fall together, but essential dependence may be asymmetrical.

In this passage, the author continues his discussion of categories by examining a key property of correlatives (al-mutadāyifān): they are together in existence and non-existence. However, he immediately notes a nuance: while this holds for pairs like double and half, it may not hold symmetrically for knowledge and its object. Let's explore this property carefully.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Property
Find the sentence that states correlatives are 'together in existence and non-existence'. This means if one exists, the other must exist; if one is non-existent, the other is too.
Look for the phrase 'معا في الوجود والعدم'.
2
Test with the Double-Half Example
Apply the property to the pair double/half. Can double exist without half? Can half exist without double? Why or why not? The author uses this as a perfect example of coexistence.
This example shows the property holds fully.
3
Analyze the Knowledge-Sense Case
The author then introduces knowledge and sense as a pair where the property may not hold symmetrically. For knowledge, its essence always requires a known object. For the known object, it can exist without being known. This shows an asymmetry in essential dependence.
Distinguish between actual correlation (both exist simultaneously) and essential dependence (one always requires the other).
4
Summarize the Lesson for Correlatives
Correlatives generally coexist, but when one essence is inherently relational (like knowledge), it always requires its correlative, while the other (like the known) may exist independently. This nuance is important for accurate analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها ما يجري في المشهور أنه يلزمها وهو أنهما معا في الوجود والعدم فلا يمكن أن يوجد أحدهما إلا وأن يوجد معه الآخر كالضعف مثلا : فإنه لا يمكن أن يوجد بدون النصف، ولا النصف بدونه كذلك ولكن قد لا يقع في بعض الأشياء تكافؤ في الوجود معًا من جهة أخرى؛ وذلك كالعلم والحس من حيث إن كل واحد منهما إدراك لا من حيث إن كل واحد منهما قوة. فإن ذات هذا العلم في جوهره قد يلزمه دائما أن يكون مضافًا إلى المعلوم موجودًا معه وذات المعلوم في جوهره لا يلزمه ذلك. وإن كانا من حيث إنهما متضايفان بالفعل لا يمكن أن يتقدم أحدهما على الآخر؛ وليس الغرض ذلك بل الغرض أن أحد الذاتين لا تنفك عن إضافة توجب أن تكون معه متضايفة أبدًا. وذات الآخر قد يوجد؛ وليس بمتضايف. ثم لقائل أن يقول ليس يجب أن يكون كل علم بإزاء معلوم موجود فإن من العلم التصور، وقد تصور أمور ليس يجب لها الوجود نحو الكرة المحيطة بذي عشرين قاعدة مثلثات. فإنا نتصور مثل هذه الكرة حق التصور ولا يحوجنا ذلك إلى أن نجعل له وجودًا غير الذي في الذهن؛ وهذا هو العلم نفسه لكن لا كلام فيه بل الكلام في علم مضاف إلى ما هو متضايف له. وأيضًا فإن في المعلومات بحسب التصديق أشياء كثيرة من جملة المضافات لا وجود
And among them is what is commonly held as necessary for them, which is that they are together in existence and non-existence. So it is not possible for one of them to exist without the other existing with it, like double, for example: it cannot exist without half, nor half without it similarly. But there may not occur in some things equivalence in existence together from another aspect, such as knowledge and sense, in that each of them is perception, not in that each of them is a power. For the essence of this knowledge in its substance may always require that it be related to the known, existing with it, while the essence of the known in its substance does not require that. And if, from the aspect that they are actual correlatives, one cannot precede the other; but that is not the intention. Rather, the intention is that one of the two essences is not free from a relation that necessitates that it be correlative with it forever, while the other essence may exist without being correlative. Then an objector may say: It is not necessary that every knowledge be opposite an existing known, for among knowledge is conception, and one conceives things that need not exist, like the sphere encompassing twenty triangular bases. For we conceive such a sphere truly without that requiring us to make for it an existence other than that in the mind; and this is knowledge itself, but there is no discussion about it. Rather, the discussion is about knowledge related to what is correlative to it. Also, among known things according to assent, there are many things from the category of relationals that do not exist
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents another property of relationals: that they coexist in existence and non-existence, meaning if one exists, the other must also exist, and if one is non-existent, the other is also non-existent. The author gives the example of 'double' and 'half': double cannot exist without half, and vice versa. However, the author notes that this equivalence in existence may not hold in all respects, such as with 'knowledge' and 'sense'. Both are perceptions, but from the aspect of essence, knowledge always requires a known object that exists with it, while the known object does not necessarily require knowledge; it can exist without being known. The author clarifies that the intention is not about actual correlation (where both exist simultaneously) but about essential dependence: one essence always has a relation that makes it inherently correlative, while the other essence can exist without that relation. Then an objection is raised: not every knowledge requires an existing known, because knowledge includes conception (tasawwur) of imaginary things that do not exist externally, like a sphere with twenty triangular bases. The objector argues that such a concept is genuine knowledge yet its object has no external existence. The author responds (though the response is cut off) by distinguishing between knowledge that is relational (added to something correlative) and conceptual knowledge that is not necessarily tied to an external object. The reply seems to accept that conceptual knowledge may not require an existing correlative, but the discussion is about knowledge that is inherently relational. The passage breaks off mid-sentence, presumably continuing the argument.

Key Terms

العلم
التصور
التصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does knowledge always require a known object, but the known object does not always require knowledge?
A) Because knowledge is a power, not an act.
B) Because the essence of knowledge is inherently relational, while the known object can exist without being known.
C) Because knowledge and known are always in actual correlation.
D) Because knowledge is never about non-existent things.

Key Takeaway
Correlatives are together in existence, but one may be essentially relational while the other is not, creating an asymmetry in dependence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the correlative pair 'fatherhood' and 'sonhood'. Does the existence of a father necessarily entail the existence of a son? Does the existence of a son necessarily entail the existence of a father? Explain using the distinction between actual coexistence and essential dependence.
?
In Islamic theology, the relationship between the Creator (Allah) and creation is asymmetric: Allah exists without needing creation, but creation depends on Allah. Analyze this using the property of correlatives discussed in the lesson. Is this a violation of the coexistence property? Why or why not?

The Objection from Conceptual Knowledge (Tasawwur)
Author presents and responds to an objection about conceptual knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the objection that conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) of non-existent things challenges the coexistence property of correlatives, distinguish between relational and non-relational knowledge, and articulate the author's likely response.

Core Idea
Conception of non-existents does not undermine the coexistence property because it is not relational knowledge.

After explaining the asymmetry in the knowledge-known pair, the author anticipates an objection: what about conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) of things that do not exist externally, like a sphere with twenty triangular bases? If such knowledge is genuine, how can the coexistence property hold? In this lesson, we analyze the objection and the implied distinction that resolves it.

Guided Path
1
Locate the Objection
Find where the objector says: 'It is not necessary that every knowledge be opposite an existing known, for among knowledge is conception (tasawwur), and one conceives things that need not exist.'
The objector uses the example of a sphere with twenty triangular bases – a geometrically conceivable object that does not exist externally.
2
Analyze the Counterexample
The objector claims that this conception is genuine knowledge, yet its object does not exist. If so, then not all knowledge requires an existing correlative. Evaluate whether this successfully refutes the coexistence property.
Consider: does conception of a non-existent count as 'knowledge' in the same sense as relational knowledge? The author hints at a distinction.
3
Distinguish Types of Knowledge
The author responds (though the passage cuts off) by stating that the discussion is about 'knowledge related to what is correlative to it' (علم مضاف إلى ما هو متضايف له). Conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) of non-existents is not of this type; it is knowledge that is not essentially relational.
In Islamic logic, tasawwur and tasdiq are two divisions. Not all tasawwur is relational.
4
Evaluate the Coexistence Property
The objection shows that the coexistence property applies only to relational knowledge (where the object is something potentially existing alongside the knowledge). Conceptual knowledge of pure fictions does not require an external correlative, so it does not violate the property.
Thus, the property remains intact for the intended domain.

How does the author respond to the objection that conception of a non-existent sphere is knowledge without an existing correlative?

The Test for Reciprocal Correlation
Test method for reciprocal vs non-reciprocal correlations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the attribute test to determine whether a given relation between two things is reciprocal or non-reciprocal.

Core Idea
Reciprocal correlation means neither term can be removed without destroying the relation.

How can you tell if two things are truly interdependent? The author provides a simple yet powerful test: gather the attributes of a thing and see if you can remove one without losing the relation. This lesson walks you through that test using the example of 'possessor of a head' and 'head', and then applies it to the non-reciprocal case of knowledge and its object.

Guided Path
1
Gather the attributes of the thing.
List all the descriptions that apply to the thing in question, including both the attribute that might be relational and other independent attributes.
For example, for a human, attributes include 'animal', 'human', 'walker', 'possessor of a head'.
2
Test by removing the suspected relational attribute.
Remove from the list the attribute you suspect is reciprocal (e.g., 'possessor of a head') while keeping the other attributes. Then check if the relation to the other term (e.g., 'head') can still be preserved.
If removing it destroys the relation, it indicates reciprocity.
3
Test by keeping the suspected attribute and removing others.
Remove all other attributes except the suspected relational one. If the relation can still be preserved, that confirms the attribute is fundamental to the relation.
The author shows that only 'possessor of a head' preserves the relation to 'head'.
4
Determine reciprocity.
If both steps show that the relation depends solely on that attribute and its removal destroys it, then the correlation is reciprocal. If the relation can survive removal of that attribute, it is non-reciprocal.
In the knowledge-object pair, removing 'knowledge' preserves the object's existence, so it is non-reciprocal.
