وأما السبيل إلى التمييز بين ما تقع إليه الإضافة بالتعادل وبين ما تقع لا بالتعادل فذلك أن تجمع أوصاف الشيء: فإنك إذا وضعته ورفعت غيره أمكنك أن تحفظ الإضافة. وإذا رفعته ووضعت غيره لم يمكن فهو الذي إليه التعادل. وما لم يكن كذلك فهو الذي ليس إليه التعادل؛ فلو رفعت من الشيء أنه حيوان وأنه إنسان وأنه مشاء وأنه ذو شيء كيف اتفق وحفظت أنه ذو رأس أمكنك أن تضيف إليه الرأس. وإذا رفعت أنه ذو رأس وحفظت أنه حيوان وأنه إنسان وأنه كذا وكذا لم يمكن ذلك. ومنها ما يجري في المشهور أنه يلزمها وهو أنهما معا في الوجود والعدم فلا يمكن أن يوجد أحدهما إلا وأن يوجد معه الآخر كالضعف مثلا : فإنه لا يمكن أن يوجد بدون النصف، ولا النصف بدونه كذلك ولكن قد لا يقع في بعض الأشياء تكافؤ في الوجود معًا من جهة أخرى؛ وذلك كالعلم والحس من حيث إن كل واحد منهما إدراك لا من حيث إن كل واحد منهما قوة. فإن ذات هذا العلم في جوهره قد يلزمه دائما أن يكون مضافًا إلى المعلوم موجودًا معه وذات المعلوم في جوهره لا يلزمه ذلك. وإن كانا من حيث إنهما متضايفان بالفعل لا يمكن أن يتقدم أحدهما على الآخر؛ وليس الغرض ذلك بل الغرض أن أحد الذاتين لا تنفك عن إضافة توجب أن تكون معه متضايفة أبدًا. وذات الآخر قد يوجد؛ وليس بمتضايف. ثم لقائل أن يقول ليس يجب أن يكون كل علم بإزاء معلوم موجود [٦٤,] فإن من العلم التصور، وقد تصور أمور ليس يجب لها الوجود نحو الكرة المحيطة بذي عشرين قاعدة مثلثات. فإنا نتصور مثل هذه الكرة حق التصور ولا يحوجنا ذلك إلى أن نجعل له وجودًا غير الذي في الذهن؛ وهذا هو العلم نفسه لكن لا كلام فيه بل الكلام في علم مضاف إلى ما هو متضايف له. وأيضًا فإن في المعلومات بحسب التصديق أشياء كثيرة من جملة المضافات لا وجود لها في الأعيان إلا بالإمكان؛ والإمكان غير الوجود وهو مثل ما إذا أخرج في شكل كذا خط كذا فإنه لا وجود له إلا في الذهن. وأما المثال المشهور في العلم وهو المربع المساوي للدائرة فإن العلم به لم يوجد وهو موجود فذلك أشد إشكالا من الدعوى؛ إذ كونه موجودًا إما في الذهن؛ وحينئذ يجب أن يكون معلوما، وإما في الأعيان؛ وذلك من جملة ما لا دليل له. ثم إن غرضنا فيما ذكرناه أن يعلم أنه يمكن أن يكون لذات أحد متضايفين وجود لا ينفك عن الإضافة إلى الآخر؛ وليس الآخر مما يكافي له في ذلك. فإن كل علم تصوري أو تصديقي ليس متضايفًا إلى شيء آخر فليس ذلك من المتضايفات التي مرَّ ذكرها . وإذا لم يكن منها فلا يتجه نقصا. ولأنا لا نقول لا شيء من المتضايفات كذلك بل نقول إن أكثرها كذلك؛ وبهذا يندفع ذلك. وأما أمر المربع والدائرة فلا يتغيَّر غرضنا بذلك؛ وذلك لأنه إن كان لهذا المربع إمكان وجود فلا يتخيل فرضه موجودًا لكن فرضه موجودًا لا يوجب أن يكون العلم به حاصلا؛ فتبين أنه مما لا يتغير به الغرض ويجب أن تعلم أن المتضايفين من حيث هما متضايفان بالفعل تضايفًا على التعادل؛ فهما معا؛ إذ الشيء إنما يقال ماهيته بالقياس إلى شيء يكون معه. وأما إذا أخذ أحدهما بالفعل والآخر بالقوة فقد زال التعادل لكن لقائل أن يقول إن المتقدم في الزمان مقول بالقياس إلى المتأخر ولا بد من أن تكون بينهما إضافة بالفعل فهما موجودان معًا. فنقول ذلك بحسب الذهن وقد حضر كل واحد من الزمانين في الذهن معا يؤخذ أحدهما متقدما، والآخر متأخرا. ولأن الزمان المتقدم إذا كان موجودًا فوجوده من الآخر أنه ليس هو، وأنه مما يقتضي وجوده متأخرًا؛ فهو إذن بهذا الوصف موجود في الذهن عند وجود الزمان المتقدم موجود في الذهن أنه ليس موجودا. وينسب إلى الذهن نسبة شيء هو موجود فاعتبر بما عرفت.
And as for the way to distinguish between that towards which the relation occurs reciprocally and that towards which it occurs non-reciprocally, it is that you collect the attributes of a thing: for if you affirm it and deny another, you can preserve the relation. And if you deny it and affirm another, it is not possible, then it is that towards which [the relation] is reciprocal. And what is not like that is that towards which [the relation] is not reciprocal. So if you remove from a thing that it is an animal, that it is a human, that it is a walker, and that it is a thing in whatever manner, and you preserve that it is a possessor of a head, you can relate the head to it. And if you remove that it is a possessor of a head and preserve that it is an animal, that it is a human, and that it is such and such, that is not possible. And among them is what proceeds in common parlance that it is necessary for it, namely that they are together in existence and non-existence, so that it is not possible for one of them to exist except that the other exists with it, like the double for example: for it is not possible for it to exist without the half, nor the half without it similarly. But sometimes there does not occur in some things an equivalence in existence together from another aspect; and that is like knowledge and sensation inasmuch as each of them is perception, not inasmuch as each of them is a faculty. For the essence of this knowledge in its substance may always necessitate that it is related to the known [object] existing with it, while the essence of the known in its substance does not necessitate that. And if they are, inasmuch as they are actually correlatives, it is not possible for one to precede the other; but the purpose is not that, rather the purpose is that one of the two essences is not free from a relation that necessitates that it is always correlative with it, while the essence of the other may exist and is not correlative. Then someone may say: it is not necessary that every knowledge is in relation to an existing known object. For among knowledge is conceptualization, and one may conceptualize things for which existence is not necessary, like the sphere surrounding a solid of twenty triangular bases. For we conceptualize such a sphere truly, and that does not require us to make for it an existence other than that in the mind; and this is the same knowledge, but there is no discussion about it; rather the discussion is about knowledge related to that which is correlative to it. Also, among the known things according to assent are many things that are among the relatives which have no existence in external entities except by possibility; and possibility is other than existence, like when a line such-and-such is drawn in a certain figure, for it has no existence except in the mind. As for the famous example in knowledge, namely the square equal to the circle, knowledge of it does not exist while it exists, so that is a greater problem than the claim; since its existence is either in the mind, and then it must be known, or in external entities, and that is among things for which there is no proof. Then our purpose in what we have mentioned is to make known that it is possible for the essence of one of two correlatives to have an existence that does not separate from the relation to the other, while the other is not equivalent to it in that. For every conceptual or assent-based knowledge that is not correlative to something else is not among the correlatives that were mentioned. And if it is not among them, then an objection does not apply. And we do not say that none of the correlatives are like that, rather we say that most of them are like that; and by this that [objection] is repelled. As for the matter of the square and the circle, our purpose is not changed by that; and that is because if this square has a possibility of existence, it is not conceivable to suppose it existing, but supposing it existing does not necessitate that knowledge of it occurs; so it is clear that the purpose is not changed by it. And you must know that the two correlatives, inasmuch as they are actually correlative with reciprocal correlation, are together; for a thing is only said in terms of its quiddity in relation to a thing that is with it. But if one of them is taken actually and the other potentially, then reciprocity has ceased. But someone may say: the prior in time is said in relation to the posterior, and there must be an actual relation between them, so they exist together. We say: that is according to the mind, when each of the two times is present in the mind together, one is taken as prior, the other as posterior. And because the prior time, if it exists, its existence from the other is that it is not it, and that it necessitates its existence as posterior; so it is, by this description, existing in the mind when the prior time exists in the mind as not existing. And it is related to the mind as the relation of a thing that exists. So consider what you have understood.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion on the nature of correlatives (mutadāyifāt), specifically how to distinguish reciprocal relations (bi al-ta'adul) from non-reciprocal ones. The author begins by providing a test: collect the attributes of a thing; if you can affirm the attribute while denying the other without losing the relation, the relation is not reciprocal. If you can only preserve the relation by affirming both, it is reciprocal. Example: 'possessor of a head' is reciprocal because removing 'head' destroys the relation regardless of other attributes. Next, the author discusses cases where existence or non-existence of one implies the other, like double and half, but notes that not all pairs are equivalent in existential dependence. Knowledge and sensation are compared: knowledge necessarily relates to an object, but the object does not necessarily relate to knowledge; thus they are not fully reciprocal. An objection is raised: not all knowledge requires an existing object, e.g., conceptualization of a sphere with twenty triangular bases. The author responds by distinguishing knowledge that is inherently correlative from knowledge that is not; only the former is at issue. Another objection about the square equal to the circle is addressed: even if it had possible existence, that does not entail actual knowledge. The author concludes by restating that one correlative may be inseparable from the relation while the other is not, and that when both are taken actually, they coexist; but if one is potential, reciprocity fails. A final sub-objection about time is answered by noting that prior and posterior coexist in the mind, not necessarily in external existence.

Checkpoint
According to the text, how can you test whether a relation between two attributes is reciprocal?
A) Check if both attributes are essential to the thing's definition.
B) See if removing one attribute still allows the other to be related to the thing.
C) Verify that both attributes exist together in the external world.
D) Confirm that the attributes are both perceptual or both intellectual.

Key Takeaway
The test for reciprocal correlation: if removing one term destroys the relation regardless of other attributes, it is reciprocal; otherwise, non-reciprocal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Apply the attribute test to determine whether the relation between 'father' and 'son' is reciprocal or non-reciprocal. Justify your answer.
?
In usul al-fiqh, consider the relation between 'cause' ('illah) and 'effect' (hukm). Is it reciprocal? Use the test from this lesson to analyze.

The Ten Categories

10 min
Objections on Non-Reciprocal Correlatives
Dialectical objections on non-reciprocal correlations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate the dialectical exchange regarding non-reciprocal correlatives, including objections about conceptual knowledge, the square equal to the circle, and temporal relations.

Core Idea
Not all knowledge requires an existing object; conceptualization of impossibles challenges the reciprocity of knowledge.

After establishing that some correlatives are non-reciprocal, the author faces objections: how can knowledge of non-existent things (like a sphere with twenty triangular bases) be considered correlative? And what about the famous geometrical problem of the square equal to the circle? This lesson dissects these objections and the author's responses, revealing the depth of the dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first objection.
An objector points out that conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) can be of things that do not exist in the external world, such as a sphere composed of twenty triangular bases. This seems to contradict the claim that knowledge always requires an existing known object.
The objection is based on examples from geometry and astronomy.
2
Analyze the author's response.
The author distinguishes between knowledge that is inherently correlative (being related to its object) and knowledge that is not. Only the former is at issue. Conceptualization of imaginary objects is not the kind of knowledge that implies a correlative relation.
The author also notes that such conceptual knowledge is not 'belonging to the correlatives mentioned'.
3
Examine the second objection: the square equal to the circle.
The objector raises a famous unsolved problem: the square equal to the circle is known to be impossible, yet we have some concept of it. The author argues that even if it had possible existence, that does not entail actual knowledge of it.
The author rejects the objection by saying existence does not necessitate knowledge.
4
Evaluate the final objection about time.
An objector says that prior and posterior in time are correlatives that must coexist actually. The author responds that they coexist in the mind, not necessarily in external existence.
This clarifies that reciprocity in the mind does not imply reciprocity in the world.

نسبة شيء هو موجود فاعتبر بما عرفت.
And as for the way to distinguish between that towards which the relation occurs reciprocally and that towards which it occurs non-reciprocally, it is that you collect the attributes of a thing: for if you affirm it and deny another, you can preserve the relation. And if you deny it and affirm another, it is not possible, then it is that towards which [the relation] is reciprocal. And what is not like that is that towards which [the relation] is not reciprocal. So if you remove from a thing that it is an animal, that it is a human, that it is a walker, and that it is a thing in whatever manner, and you preserve that it is a possessor of a head, you can relate the head to it. And if you remove that it is a possessor of a head and preserve that it is an animal, that it is a human, and that it is such and such, that is not possible. And among them is what proceeds in common parlance that it is necessary for it, namely that they are together in existence and non-existence, so that it is not possible for one of them to exist except that the other exists with it, like the double for example: for it is not possible for it to exist without the half, nor the half without it similarly. But sometimes there does not occur in some things an equivalence in existence together from another aspect; and that is like knowledge and sensation inasmuch as each of them is perception, not inasmuch as each of them is a faculty. For the essence of this knowledge in its substance may always necessitate that it is related to the known [object] existing with it, while the essence of the known in its substance does not necessitate that. And if they are, inasmuch as they are actually correlatives, it is not possible for one to precede the other; but the purpose is not that, rather the purpose is that one of the two essences is not free from a relation that necessitates that it is always correlative with it, while the essence of the other may exist and is not correlative. Then someone may say: it is not necessary that every knowledge is in relation to an existing known object. For among knowledge is conceptualization, and one may conceptualize things for which existence is not necessary, like the sphere surrounding a solid of twenty triangular bases. For we conceptualize such a sphere truly, and that does not require us to make for it an existence other than that in the mind; and this is the same knowledge, but there is no discussion about it; rather the discussion is about knowledge related to that which is correlative to it. Also, among the known things according to assent are many things that are among the relatives which have no existence in external entities except by possibility; and possibility is other than existence, like when a line such-and-such is drawn in a certain figure, for it has no existence except in the mind. As for the famous example in knowledge, namely the square equal to the circle, knowledge of it does not exist while it exists, so that is a greater problem than the claim; since its existence is either in the mind, and then it must be known, or in external entities, and that is among things for which there is no proof. Then our purpose in what we have mentioned is to make known that it is possible for the essence of one of two correlatives to have an existence that does not separate from the relation to the other, while the other is not equivalent to it in that. For every conceptual or assent-based knowledge that is not correlative to something else is not among the correlatives that were mentioned. And if it is not among them, then an objection does not apply. And we do not say that none of the correlatives are like that, rather we say that most of them are like that; and by this that [objection] is repelled. As for the matter of the square and the circle, our purpose is not changed by that; and that is because if this square has a possibility of existence, it is not conceivable to suppose it existing, but supposing it existing does not necessitate that knowledge of it occurs; so it is clear that the purpose is not changed by it. And you must know that the two correlatives, inasmuch as they are actually correlative with reciprocal correlation, are together; for a thing is only said in terms of its quiddity in relation to a thing that is with it. But if one of them is taken actually and the other potentially, then reciprocity has ceased. But someone may say: the prior in time is said in relation to the posterior, and there must be an actual relation between them, so they exist together. We say: that is according to the mind, when each of the two times is present in the mind together, one is taken as prior, the other as posterior. And because the prior time, if it exists, its existence from the other is that it is not it, and that it necessitates its existence as posterior; so it is, by this description, existing in the mind when the prior time exists in the mind as not existing. And it is related to the mind as the relation of a thing that exists. So consider what you have understood.

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion on the nature of correlatives (mutadāyifāt), specifically how to distinguish reciprocal relations (bi al-ta'adul) from non-reciprocal ones. The author begins by providing a test: collect the attributes of a thing; if you can affirm the attribute while denying the other without losing the relation, the relation is not reciprocal. If you can only preserve the relation by affirming both, it is reciprocal. Example: 'possessor of a head' is reciprocal because removing 'head' destroys the relation regardless of other attributes. Next, the author discusses cases where existence or non-existence of one implies the other, like double and half, but notes that not all pairs are equivalent in existential dependence. Knowledge and sensation are compared: knowledge necessarily relates to an object, but the object does not necessarily relate to knowledge; thus they are not fully reciprocal. An objection is raised: not all knowledge requires an existing object, e.g., conceptualization of a sphere with twenty triangular bases. The author responds by distinguishing knowledge that is inherently correlative from knowledge that is not; only the former is at issue. Another objection about the square equal to the circle is addressed: even if it had possible existence, that does not entail actual knowledge. The author concludes by restating that one correlative may be inseparable from the relation while the other is not, and that when both are taken actually, they coexist; but if one is potential, reciprocity fails. A final sub-objection about time is answered by noting that prior and posterior coexist in the mind, not necessarily in external existence.

Key Terms
مُتضَايِف
تَعَادُل
إِضَافَة
بِالفِعْل
بِالقُوَّة
تَصَوُّر
تَصْدِيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the objection raised against the claim that knowledge requires an existing known object?
A) Knowledge can be of the past, which no longer exists.
B) Conceptualization of a sphere with twenty triangular bases does not require its external existence.
C) Sensation does not require a physical object.
D) The square equal to the circle is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The dialectic on non-reciprocal correlatives shows that objections based on conceptual knowledge can be answered by distinguishing inherently correlative knowledge from non-correlative knowledge.

Correlatives and Mental Existence: The Square-Circle Example
Author uses square-circle to show correlatives can exist in mind only.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the square-circle example demonstrates that correlatives can have mental existence without external coexistence.

Core Idea
Possibility (imkan) is not existence; the mind can hold correlatives with no external counterpart.

In this lesson, we examine a classic logical puzzle: the square equal to a circle, an impossible shape that nonetheless exists in the mind. The author uses this example to argue against the view that correlatives must always coexist externally. Understanding this argument is crucial for grasping the role of mental existence in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's main claim
Find the sentence where the author states that correlatives can have existence in the mind without external actuality. The claim is that possibility (imkan) suffices for mental existence.
2
Locate the square-circle example
The author introduces the example of the square equal to a circle. Note that it exists in the mind but knowledge of it does not exist. This poses a problem.
3
Analyze the implicit objection
The example seems to be a counterexample: if it exists in the mind, it should be known, but it isn't. Restate this objection in your own words.
4
Examine the author's response
The author uses the concept of possibility (imkan) to distinguish existence from actuality. He says that assuming its existence does not entail that knowledge is attained. How does this resolve the objection?
5
Summarize the lesson
The square-circle example shows that mental existence does not require external actuality or even actual knowledge. This supports the author's point that correlatives can exist in the mind without external counterpart.

لها في الأعيان إلا بالإمكان؛ والإمكان غير الوجود وهو مثل ما إذا أخرج في شكل كذا خط كذا فإنه لا وجود له إلا في الذهن. وأما المثال المشهور في العلم وهو المربع المساوي للدائرة فإن العلم به لم يوجد وهو موجود فذلك أشد إشكالا من الدعوى؛ إذ كونه موجودًا إما في الذهن؛ وحينئذ يجب أن يكون معلوما، وإما في الأعيان؛ وذلك من جملة ما لا دليل له.
ثم إن غرضنا فيما ذكرناه أن يعلم أنه يمكن أن يكون لذات أحد متضايفين وجود لا ينفك عن الإضافة إلى الآخر؛ وليس الآخر مما يكافي له في ذلك. فإن كل علم تصوري أو تصديقي ليس متضايفًا إلى شيء آخر فليس ذلك من المتضايفات التي مرَّ ذكرها . وإذا لم يكن منها فلا يتجه نقصا. ولأنا لا نقول لا شيء من المتضايفات كذلك بل نقول إن أكثرها كذلك؛ وبهذا يندفع ذلك. وأما أمر المربع والدائرة فلا يتغيَّر غرضنا بذلك؛ وذلك لأنه إن كان لهذا المربع إمكان وجود فلا يتخيل فرضه موجودًا لكن فرضه موجودًا لا يوجب أن يكون العلم به حاصلا؛ فتبين أنه مما لا يتغير به الغرض ويجب أن تعلم أن المتضايفين من حيث هما متضايفان بالفعل تضايفًا على التعادل؛ فهما معا؛ إذ الشيء إنما يقال ماهيته بالقياس إلى شيء يكون معه. وأما إذا أخذ أحدهما بالفعل والآخر بالقوة فقد زال التعادل لكن لقائل أن يقول إن المتقدم في الزمان مقول بالقياس إلى المتأخر ولا بد من أن تكون بينهما إضافة بالفعل فهما موجودان معًا. فنقول ذلك بحسب الذهن وقد حضر كل واحد من الزمانين في الذهن معا يؤخذ أحدهما متقدما، والآخر متأخرا. ولأن الزمان المتقدم إذا كان موجودًا فوجوده من الآخر أنه ليس هو، وأنه مما يقتضي وجوده متأخرًا؛ فهو إذن بهذا الوصف موجود في الذهن عند وجود الزمان المتقدم موجود في الذهن أنه ليس موجودا. وينسب إلى الذهن نسبة شيء هو موجود فاعتبر بما عرفت.
واعلم أن جميع أمثال هذه إضافات وهمية. والمتضايفات فيها أيضًا إنما تكون متضايفات في الوهم؛ وبيانه على سبيل الاستقصاء إنما هو في العلوم الحقيقية فلا على المنطقي أن يتكلف في المنطق علما هو غير المنطق.
They have existence in external entities only with possibility; and possibility is not existence. It is like when something is produced in such a shape, such a line, then it has no existence except in the mind. As for the famous example in knowledge, which is the square equal to the circle, knowledge of it does not exist while it exists. That is a stronger problem than the claim; for its existence is either in the mind—and then it must be known—or in external entities, and that is among things for which there is no proof.
Then our purpose in what we have mentioned is that it is known that it is possible for one of two correlatives to have an existence that is inseparable from the relation to the other, while the other does not correspond to it in that. For every conceptual or assentive knowledge is not correlative to another thing, so it is not among the correlatives that have been mentioned. If it is not among them, then an objection does not apply. And we do not say that none of the correlatives are like this; rather we say that most of them are like this, and with this the objection is repelled. As for the matter of the square and the circle, our purpose is not changed by that; because if this square has possibility of existence, then its being assumed as existent is not imaginable, but assuming it as existent does not necessitate that knowledge of it is attained. So it becomes clear that our purpose is not changed by it. And you must know that two correlatives, insofar as they are actually correlative with a balanced correlation, are together; because a thing's essence is only predicated in relation to something that is with it. But if one of them is taken actually and the other potentially, then the balance is removed. But an objector may say that the prior in time is predicated in relation to the posterior, and there must be between them an actual relation, so they exist together. We say that is according to the mind; each of the two times is present in the mind together, one is taken as prior, the other as posterior. And because the prior time, if it exists, its existence from the other is that it is not it, and that it necessitates its existence as posterior; so it is, by this description, existing in the mind when the prior time exists in the mind that it is not existent. And it is attributed to the mind as a relation of something that exists. So consider what you have known.
And know that all such relations are imaginary. The correlatives in them are also only correlatives in imagination; and its complete explanation is only in the real sciences. So it is not for the logician to burden himself in logic with a science that is not logic.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological argument about the nature of correlatives (al-mutada'ifan). The author begins by addressing a possible objection: that correlatives must both exist externally. He uses the example of a square equal to a circle, which is known to be impossible in external reality yet can be conceived in the mind. He argues that possibility (al-imkan) is not existence; the square exists only in the mind. If it existed in the mind, it should be known, but knowledge of it does not actually exist (since it's an impossible shape). This shows that existence in the mind does not automatically entail actual knowledge. The author then clarifies his main point: it is possible for one correlative to have an existence tied to another without the other having a similar existence. He gives conceptual knowledge (tasawwuri or tasdiqi) as an example, which is not correlative to anything else. He rejects the objection that all correlatives must coexist equally, stating that most are like this but not all. Regarding the square-circle example, he insists it does not affect his thesis because assuming its existence does not require that knowledge is attained. He then explains that actual correlatives in balance are together, but if one is potential and the other actual, balance is lost. He responds to an objection about time: the prior and posterior are considered in the mind, not externally. The prior time exists in the mind as not being the posterior, and this mental existence suffices for the relation. Finally, he warns that such relations are imaginary (wahmiyya); the logician should not delve into them beyond what is needed for logic.

Key Terms
المتضايفان (al-mutada'ifan): Two correlatives that are defined in relation to each other, such that each is understood by reference to the other. [Note: In earlier Aristotelian logic, correlatives are always simultaneous; here the author allows for possibility of one-sided existence.]
الإمكان (al-imkan): Possibility of existence, as opposed to actual existence. Used to distinguish mental existence from external reality. [Note: Standard term in Islamic philosophy for contingency; no significant shift.]
الذهن (al-dhihn): The mind, as a locus of conceptual existence distinct from external reality. [Note: Often used in epistemology; stable across texts.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does the square-equal-to-circle example not undermine his claim about correlatives?
A) Because the square does not exist at all
B) Because assuming its existence does not entail that knowledge of it is actual
C) Because it is a known mathematical fact
D) Because it exists in external reality

Key Takeaway
Correlatives can have mental existence without external coexistence, as shown by the square-circle example.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Temporal Correlatives and the Mental Resolution
Author resolves the objection about temporal correlatives by assigning them to mental existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and evaluate the author's response to the objection that prior and posterior times must coexist externally.

Core Idea
Relations like before/after exist in the mind, not in external reality; the mind holds both terms together.

One of the strongest objections to the claim that correlatives need not coexist externally comes from temporal relations: the prior and posterior seem to require actual coexistence. In this lesson, we follow the author's ingenious response, which locates the relation in the mind. This resolution is a key move in Islamic epistemology of relations.

Guided Path
1
State the objection about time
Identify the objector's claim: prior and posterior are actual correlatives, so they must coexist in external reality. Find the sentence where this objection is presented.
2
Identify the author's response
The author responds by saying the relation is 'according to the mind' (بحسب الذهن). Locate this phrase and see how it resolves the objection.
3
Analyze the mental resolution
Explain why the mind can hold two non-simultaneous times together. The author says each time is present in the mind together, one taken as prior, one as posterior. How does this avoid the need for external coexistence?
4
Consider the final warning
The author concludes that such relations are imaginary (وهمية) and not the concern of the logician. What does this imply about the scope of logic?
5
Apply the lesson
Reflect on how this reasoning can be used in other domains, such as cause and effect. Is the relation necessarily external?

Essential and Accidental Relatives: Critique of the Common Definition
Defines essential and accidental relatives and critiques the common definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential relatives from accidental relatives and evaluate whether a given definition (rasm) truly captures the category of relation.

Core Idea
A definition of relation that also applies to substances cannot define the category itself.

In the study of categories (maqulat), logicians define relatives as things whose quiddity is said in relation to another. But does this definition uniquely pick out the category of relation? In this passage, the author shows that it does not, because substances and qualities also fit the description. We will examine the two types of relatives and see why the common definition fails.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of relatives
Read the first two sentences of the Arabic. The author distinguishes relatives by essence (mudaf bi al-dhat) from those that have relation accidentally (bi al-‘arad). Note that essential relatives have their quiddity said in relation to another, while accidental ones become relative through some relation.
Essential: quiddity requires another; accidental: the relation is added externally.
2
Extract the common definition (rasm) under critique
Find the phrase 'الرسم المذكور' (the mentioned description). The definition in question is: 'what is said of its quiddity in relation to another.' This is the standard rasm for the category of relation. Write it down.
This rasm applies to all relatives, but the author will test its exclusivity.
3
Apply the definition to a substance example: 'head'
The author uses 'head' (al-ra's) as a counterexample. Explain: For something to be a head, it must be a substance (jawhar) essentially, and it must also be in relation to a body. Both are constitutive for being a head. Therefore, 'head' satisfies the definition 'quiddity said in relation to another'—yet it is a substance, not a relative category. Thus the definition is not exclusive.
The head's quiddity includes relation (to a body), but its primary category is substance.
4
Conclude that the definition is too broad
Since the definition applies to things that are not in the category of relation (e.g., head from substance, possession from quality), it is not a proper definition of the category itself. It is merely a description that many things satisfy. The categories are mutually exclusive, so a single thing cannot be in two categories essentially. Hence the rasm fails.
The author's critique shows that this rasm is not a hadd (essential definition) but a general description.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما المضافات التي مرَّ ذكرها فمنها ما هو مضاف بالذات، ومنها ما هو عارض له الإضافة أو لازم، فبعضها إذن كانت ماهياتها مقولة بالقياس إلى الغير، وبعضها كانت قد تصير كذلك بنحو من أنحاء النسبة. والرسم المذكور مما يطابق الكل؛ فينظر فيه أنه هل يطابق المقولة أو هو رسم معنى يصلح أن يقال إنه مضاف؛ وليس هو بنفس المقولة ولا نوعا منها. فنقول إنا نعلم أن المقولات متباينة، وأنه لا يصلح أن تحمل مقولتان على شيء واحد حمل الجنس حتى يكون الشيء الواحد يدخل من جهة ماهيته في مقولتين؛ وإن كان قد يدخل الشيء في مقولة بذاته وفي الأخرى بالعرض. ثم إن هذا الرسم مما لا يمنع العقل مطابقته أمورًا تدخل في مقولات أخرى فإن الرأس مثلا يحتاج أن يكون بذاته جوهرًا حتى يكون رأسًا كما يحتاج أن يكون مقولا لماهيته بالقياس إلى غيره حتى يكون رأسا؛ وكلا الأمرين مقوم له من حيث هو رأس ليس أحدهما بالذات والآخر بالعرض. والرأس إنما يكون كذلك إذا أخذ رأسًا على الإطلاق. وأما إذا أخذ مخصصًا على أنه هذا الرأس فإنه لا يقال ماهيته بالقياس إلى غيره حينئذ . وأما إذا دل على أنه لشيء جملة فليس ذلك له من حيث هو هذا الرأس بل من حيث هو رأس على الإطلاق بالقياس إلى شيء. وأيضًا فإن بعض الأمور التي مرَّ ذكرها قد كانت في ذواتها من مقولة الكيف مثل الملكة، وقد قيلت بالقياس إلى غيرها بنحو من أنحاء النسبة.
As for the relatives that have been mentioned, some are relatives by essence, and some have relation accidental or necessary to them. So some of them are such that their quiddities are said in relation to something else, and some become so by some kind of relation. The mentioned description (rasm) applies to all; so we consider whether it matches the category or is a description of a meaning that can be called relative—not the category itself nor a species of it. For we know that the categories are distinct, and it is not valid for two categories to be predicated of one thing as a genus, such that a single thing enters from the aspect of its quiddity into two categories, although a thing may enter into one category essentially and into another accidentally. Moreover, this description does not prevent the mind from applying it to things that fall under other categories. For example, the head essentially needs to be a substance to be a head, just as it needs to be predicated of its quiddity in relation to something else to be a head. Both are constitutive for it as a head—one is not essential and the other accidental. The head is like this only when taken absolutely. But if taken as specified as this head, then its quiddity is not said in relation to something else at that time. And if it indicates that it belongs to something as a whole, that is not from the aspect of being this head, but from being a head absolutely in relation to something. Also, some of the things mentioned were in their essences from the category of quality, like possession (al-malka), and they are said in relation to something else by some kind of relation.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage defines two types of relatives: essential (بالذات) and accidental (بالعرض). It then examines the common definition (الرسم المذكور) which says a relative is 'what is said of its quiddity in relation to another.' The author questions whether this definition applies strictly to the category of relation (مقولة المضاف) or is a broader description. He argues that categories are mutually exclusive—a single thing cannot fall under two categories essentially. However, the definition allows things from other categories (like 'head' from substance, 'possession' from quality) to be considered relatives because they also fit the definition. For instance, 'head' requires being a substance first, but it also requires being related to a body. Thus, the definition does not exclude substances. This shows that the definition is not a true definition of the category 'relation' but rather a general description that covers many things.

Refining the Definition of Relative: The Famous Correction
Presents and defends the famous correction that relatives are those whose existence is relative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the famous correction of the definition of relative from the initial definition and defend it against the charge of circularity using the transfer of genus-to-species terminology.

Core Idea
Relatives are defined by their existence being relative, not merely by their quiddity in relation.

The initial definition of the category of relation (al-mudaf) was too broad, including substances that merely have relations. The famous correction restricts the definition to things whose very existence is relative. However, some critics claim this correction is identical to the first or leads to circularity. In this lesson, we dissect the correction and the author's rebuttal, showing how term transfer from genus to species is common and non-circular using the example of 'possible' (mumkin).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Find the initial definition (ما يقال ماهيته بالقياس إلى غيره) and the corrected definition (الوجود لها مضافة) in the Arabic passage. Note that the initial focuses on quiddity, the corrected on existence.
The initial definition says a relative is 'what is said of its quiddity in relation to another'.
2
Compare their scopes
The initial definition includes anything that can be described in relation, even if it has independent existence (like a substance). The corrected definition excludes such things because their existence is not relative.
Substances exist independently; relations attach to them accidentally.
3
Analyze the circularity objection
Some argue that defining 'relative' using 'existence is relative' is circular because 'relative' appears in the definition. Understand why this seems problematic.
Circularity occurs when the definiendum is used in the definiens.
4
Examine the rebuttal with 'possible'
The author shows that a term can be transferred from a broader genus to a specific species without circularity. 'Possible' originally meant 'not impossible' (genus), but specialists restricted it to 'neither necessary nor impossible' (species). Similarly, 'relative' originally covered all things said in relation (genus), and the correction restricts it to those whose existence is relative (species).
The transfer does not use the specific meaning in the definition; it uses the genus meaning.
5
Apply to another field
Think of an example in usul al-fiqh where a term was transferred from a general to a specific meaning (e.g., 'qiyas' from 'measuring' to 'analogical reasoning'). Explain how this is analogous.
This step reinforces the concept of term transfer as a legitimate semantic shift.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالرسم الأول لا يمنع من أن يكون هنا أمور هي من باب الجوهر وغيره داخلة في المضاف فليس هذا الرسم إذن رسم المقولة وإلا لا تشترك في رسم واحد أمور من مقولات شتى وإن لم يكن رسمًا لها بل هو اسم معنى يعمها ؛ فيجب إذن تدارك هذا الخلل. والتدارك المشهور هو أن الأمور التي من المضاف هي التي الوجود لها مضافة؛ وهذا تدارك صحيح لكن بعض الناس يظن أن هذا بعينه هو الرسم الأول؛ وليس كذلك وإلا لكان بالأول عنه عني على الخصوص إذا كان الأول يدل على المعنى الذي يدل عليه دلالة واضحة لا يدل عليه الثاني. وإن دل دلالة بذلك الوضوح وبعضهم يظن أنه يقضي إلى الدور لما أن المضاف جزء لما يعرف به وليس كذلك أيضًا. فإن من الأشياء ما يكون جنسه أشهر عند الجمهور فيكون الاسم بحسب الوضع الأول موضوعًا لجنسه أو لما هو كالجنس. ثم إن الخواص يعرفون معنى نوعيا تحته أو معنى هو كالنوعي فينقلون اسم الجنس إليه كما في الممكن فإنه اسم لما ليس بممتنع بحسب الوضع الأول. فالخواص يُنقلونه إلى ما ليس بواجب الوجود، ولا يمتنع أيضًا فخصوه باسم الممكن من حيث هو غير ضروري؛ وهذا هو نقل اسم ما هو كالجنس إلى ما كالنوع. وعلى هذا في المضاف فإنه كان مقولا في الوضع الأول عند جمهور الفلاسفة على المعنى المذكور؛ وهو أنه ما يقال ماهيته على الصفة المذكورة من غير اعتبار أن له وجودًا غير ذلك أو ليس له وجود غير ذلك حتى إذا كان الشيء من الجوهر أو من غيره؛ ثم لحقه نسبة كان من حيث هو كذلك مقول الماهية بالقياس إلى غيره. فكان من المضاف فله ماهية مخصوصة من جملة ما لا يقال بالقياس، أو ماهية مقولة بالقياس إلى الغير كالأخوة مثلا؛ فكان المضاف يقع على معنيين جميعًا. وإن لم يكن لهما جنسًا فليس كل ما يحمل
So the first description does not prevent there being things from the domain of substance and others entering into the relative. Hence this description is not the description of the category; otherwise, things from diverse categories would not share a single description, even if it is not a description for them but a name for a meaning that encompasses them. So it is necessary to remedy this defect. The famous remedy is that the things that belong to the relative are those whose existence is relative. This is a correct remedy, but some people think that this is exactly the first description; it is not so, otherwise the first would specify it particularly if the first indicates the meaning that it indicates clearly, which the second does not. And if it indicates with that clarity, some people think it leads to circularity because the relative is part of what defines it, but it is not so either. For among things, its genus is more famous among the common people, so the name in the original convention is assigned to its genus or what is like a genus. Then the specialists know a specific meaning under it or a meaning that is like a species, so they transfer the name of the genus to it, like 'possible' (mumkin) which in the original convention is a name for what is not impossible. So the specialists transfer it to what is neither necessary nor impossible, and they specify it with the name 'possible' from the aspect of being non-necessary. This is a transfer of a name from what is like a genus to what is like a species. Similarly for 'relative': it was predicated in the original convention among the majority of philosophers on the mentioned meaning—that it is what is said of its quiddity in the described manner without considering whether it has another existence or not, such that even if a thing is from substance or other, then a relation attaches to it, it becomes from that aspect predicated of its quiddity in relation to another. So it becomes a relative: it has a specific quiddity from among those not said in relation, or a quiddity said in relation to another, like brotherhood. So the relative falls on both meanings. And if it does not have a genus for them, not everything that is predicated...
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents the famous correction to the definition of the category of relation. The author explains that the initial description (ما يقال ماهيته بالقياس إلى غيره) is too broad because it includes things from other categories. The remedy is to restrict it to those things whose very existence is relative (الوجود لها مضافة). This means that the being of a relative is not independent; it is only realized in relation to another. The author then addresses two criticisms: (1) that this correction is the same as the first description, which he denies because the first does not mention existence; (2) that it leads to circularity because the definition of relative includes 'relative' itself. To rebut the second, he uses the example of 'possible' (ممكن). Originally, 'possible' meant 'not impossible' (a broad genus), but specialists transferred it to a specific sense (what is neither necessary nor impossible). Similarly, 'relative' was originally a broad concept, and the correction refines it without circularity. The transfer of names from genus to species is common. The passage concludes by noting that 'relative' has two meanings: one as a genus for relatives, and one as a specific category. However, since they share no single genus, the correction is needed.

Key Takeaway
The famous correction refines the definition of relative by specifying that its existence, not just its quiddity, is relative, and this refinement is neither redundant nor circular.

Defining the Category of the Relative (al-Mudaf)
Author defines the category of the relative and distinguishes it from other relatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defining characteristic of the category of the relative and distinguish it from other relatives using the concept of thingness.

Core Idea
The relative as a category has no existence apart from its relation.

In this passage, the author defines the category of the relative (al-mudaf), one of the ten Aristotelian categories in Islamic logic. He clarifies that this categorical relative is unique: its essence and existence are entirely constituted by its being said of another in relation. This definition distinguishes it from ordinary relative terms like 'head' or 'knowledge', which have independent existence. The author also addresses an objection by explaining how 'thingness' differs between categorical and non-categorical relatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the definition of al-mudaf as a category. The author states that its essence and existence is that it is said of another in relation, with no other existence.
The term 'relative' here is specific to the category, not any relative term.
2
Extract the genus and differentia
The genus (used loosely) is the common meaning of being relative; the differentia is that it has no existence other than the relation. The author emphasizes this distinguishes it from true genus/species.
The term 'jins' here means 'general', not the technical category.
3
Compare with ordinary relatives
See why terms like 'head', 'knowledge', and 'weakness' are not categorical relatives. They have inherent existence apart from any relation, so they do not meet the definition.
Ordinary relatives may acquire relations but have independent essence.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The objection claims that the categorical relative shares 'thingness' with other relatives, blurring the distinction. The author responds by specifying that thingness is determined by the existence it is attached to: for the categorical relative, it is the relational existence alone.
This leads to the logical consequence that positing one end of a relative pair necessitates the other.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالمعنى على مقولتين أو على شيئين من مقولتين أو على شيئين من مقولة واحدة فهو جنس المقولتين. ولو كان كذلك لكان معنى المضاف المأخوذ في التعريف هو هذا المعنى العام. ومعنى المضاف المعرف هو هذا المعنى الخاص؛ فكما أن الممكن الحقيقي يعرف بأنه هو الذي يمكن أن يكون، ويمكن أن لا يكون؛ ولا دور فيه فكذلك المضاف الحقيقي يعرف بأنه هو الذي ماهيته ووجوده أنه مضاف يعني أنه الذي وجوده وماهيته هو أنه مقول الماهية بالقياس ولا وجود له غيره ؛ والمعنى من الجنس والنوع هنا هو العام والخاص لا الجنس الحقيقي ولا النوع كذلك. فإذا كان رسم المضاف الذي هو المقولة هو هذا الرسم فالرأس لا يكون مضافًا بالمعنى الذي للمقولة. وكذلك العلم والضعف فإنه لا يكون كما يعرض له نسبة. ولقائل أن يقول [إن] المضاف الذي هو المقولة فهو أيضًا شيء ذو إضافة لأنه شيء مقول ماهيته بالقياس إلى غيره، وإذا كان كذلك فقد شارك الذي هو المقولة غير المقولة فلا يكون بينهما فرق . وأما الشيئية فهي أمر لا ينفك عنه المضاف الذي هو المقولة لكن الفرق بين فإن الشيئية التي تحمل على المقولة هي التي يخصصها الوجود الذي للمضاف من حيث هو مضاف. وأما الشيئية التي تحمل على الغير فهي التي يخصصها وجود آخر كما مرَّ في هذا الباب من الجوهر وغيره من قبل. وأما شيئية الإضافة فهي الإضافة بالحقيقة. فلو قلنا إن المضاف الذي هو المقولة هو الشيء الذي ليس له وجود إلا الوجود الذي هو به مضاف فلسنا نعني به الوجود العام بل نحو أمر الوجود مخصصًا للعام ليس هو التخصيص بكونه إضافة فقط بل قد يخصص تخصيصا في أنه إضافة. ثم إذا كان المضاف لا وجود له إلا أنه مضاف فيلزم أن يكون إذا جعل أحد طرفيه كان الآخر محصلا بسببه حتى إذا قلت نصف مطلقًا كان بإزائه ضعف من غير تحصيل.
By meaning upon two categories or upon two things from two categories or upon two things from one category, then it is the genus of the two categories. And if it were like that, then the meaning of 'relative' taken in the definition would be this general meaning, and the meaning of the defined relative would be this specific meaning. Just as the true possible is defined as that which can be and can not be, and there is no circularity in it, so likewise the true relative is defined as that whose essence and existence is that it is relative, meaning that its existence and essence is that it is said of the essence in relation to another, and it has no existence other than that. And the meaning of genus and species here is general and specific, not the true genus nor the true species. So if the description of the relative that is the category is this description, then the head is not relative in the sense of the category, and likewise knowledge and weakness are not as a relation occurs to them. And one may say that the relative that is the category is also a thing with addition, because it is a thing whose essence is said in relation to another, and if so, it shares with the thing that is the category and the non-category, so there is no difference between them. But 'thingness' is something from which the relative that is the category does not separate, but the difference is that the thingness that is predicated of the category is that which is specified by the existence that belongs to the relative insofar as it is relative, while the thingness that is predicated of the other is that which is specified by another existence, as has passed in this chapter regarding substance and others before. And the thingness of addition is the addition in reality. So if we say that the relative that is the category is the thing that has no existence except the existence by which it is relative, we do not mean by it the general existence, but rather a type of existence that specifies the general, not the specification by being an addition only, but it may specify as an addition. Then if the relative has no existence except that it is relative, it follows that if one of its two ends is posited, the other is realized by it, so that when you say 'half' absolutely, there is a double opposite it without realization.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage defines the category of the relative (al-mudaf) and distinguishes it from other uses of the term 'relative'. The author begins by refining the definition: if the meaning of 'relative' were common to multiple categories, it would be a genus. But here, the relative as a category has a specific meaning: it is that whose essence and existence consists entirely in its being said of something else in relation. This is like the 'true possible' which is defined without circularity as that which can be or not be. The author emphasizes that the terms 'genus' and 'species' here mean 'general' and 'specific', not the technical categories. The 'rasm' (description) of the relative as a category is given, and then it is shown that things like 'head', 'knowledge', and 'weakness' are not relative in this categorical sense because they have inherent existence. An objection is raised: if the categorical relative is also a thing with addition, it shares thingness with other relatives, so how is it distinct? The answer distinguishes between the thingness that specifies the categorical relative (its existence is solely as relative) and the thingness of other relatives (which have another existence). The author clarifies that the categorical relative has no existence except the existence by which it is relative, and this existence is a specific type, not general existence. This leads to the logical consequence that positing one end of a relative pair necessitates the other, as with 'half' and 'double'.

Key Takeaway
The category of the relative is defined by having no existence except as a relation, distinguishing it from other relatives that have independent existence.

Defining the Category of Where and Distinguishing It from the Relative
Author defines the category of where and distinguishes it from the relative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'where' (al-ayn) and explain how it differs from the category of the relative.

Core Idea
Where is a state, not just a relation—a subtle but crucial distinction.

In this passage, the author provides two definitions of al-ayn and warns against confusing it with the category of the relative. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the ten categories in Islamic metaphysical discussions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Find the two ways the author defines al-ayn: first as a relation of a body to its place, second as the state by which a body answers 'where is it?'.
These definitions are complementary.
2
Analyze the relation to the relative
Notice the author's caution: al-ayn resembles the relative but is not. If you take only the relation, it becomes a relative. Why? Because relation alone is a distinct category.
The relation between container and contained is an example.
3
Understand the completion
The author says al-ayn is a state completed by relation. The relation is necessary but not sufficient; what else makes it a state?
The state is the body's being-in-place itself.
4
Apply the distinction
Given 'Zayd is in the mosque', decide which part is from al-ayn and which from the relative. Al-ayn: the state of Zayd being in the mosque. The relative: the relation between Zayd and the mosque.
If you isolate the relation, it belongs to the relative category.

فصل : [ في الأين] وأما الأين فإنه يعرف بأنه هو نسبة متمكن إلى المكان الذي هو فيه، ويعرف بأنه هو الحالة التي للجسم يجاب بها حين يُسأل أنه «أين هو؟»، وهي كون الشيء في مكانه بالحقيقة. وقد مرّ من قبل أنه كيف يباين المضاف وإن كان أشد اشتباها بالمضاف بالنسبة إلى غيره. ولا يظن أنه هو مجرد نسبة إلى المكان بل هو حالة يتم حصولها بالنسة إلى المكان. فإذا أخذت تلك النسبة وحدها كانت هي من مقولة المضاف كما هي بين الحاوي والمحوي؛ فالمكان من حيث هو المكان ليس من باب المضاف بل هو سطح يعرض عليه الاحتواء على المحوي فهذا العارض من المضاف؛ وهي النسبة التي بين المحوي والحاوي والكون في المكان ليس هو الكون في الأعيان الذي هو الوجود. فإنا قد بينا أن الوجود ليس جنسًا لما تحته. ولو كان الكون في المكان هو الوجود لكان الكون في الزمان أيضًا كذلك فيكون للشيء وجودات كثيرة؛ وذلك محال فهو إذن غير الوجود وإنه جنس الأنواع. فإن الكون فوق وتحت وفي الهواء وفي الماء أين ومنه ما هو حقيقي أولى وهو كون الشيء في المكان الحقيقي له وهو مكانه الذي لا يسعه فيه غيره حين هو فيه نحو الهواء في السطح المقعر من الأثير. وأما قول من قال إنه على مثال كون الماء في الكوز»، فذلك باطل لاستحالة كون الماء في الكوز؛ والكوز هو ذلك الحرف الذي يستقر في جوفه الماء. ومنه غير حقيقي أولى وهو الذي لا يكون كذلك كما يقال مثلا هذا الشيء في الماء، وذلك في السماء؛ ولا يوصف بالحقيقي منهما إلا جسم واحد على خلاف غير الحقيقي . فإن من الأجسام في الماء والسماء وغيرهما كما في البيت مثلا فإن جميع البيت لا يكون مشغولا به، وأبعد منه في الدار وأبعد منه في المحلة وأبعد منه في البلدة وأبعد منه في الإقليم
Chapter: On Where. As for where, it is defined as a relation of a body in place to the place in which it is, and it is defined as the state of the body by which it is answered when asked 'Where is it?', and it is the being of a thing in its place in reality. It has passed before how it differs from the relative, although it is more similar to the relative in relation to others. And let one not think that it is merely a relation to place, but rather a state that is completed by relation to place. For if that relation were taken alone, it would be from the category of the relative, as it is between container and contained. So place, insofar as it is place, is not from the category of the relative, but rather it is a surface on which containment of the contained occurs; this accident is from the relative, and it is the relation between contained and container. And being in place is not the being in individuals that is existence. For we have shown that existence is not a genus for what is under it. And if being in place were existence, then being in time would also be so, and then a thing would have many existences, which is impossible. Therefore, it is other than existence, and it is a genus of species. For being above, below, in air, in water is 'where', and among it is real foremost, which is the being of a thing in its real place, which is its place that does not hold another at the same time, like air in the concave surface of the ether. As for the saying that it is like the being of water in the jug, that is false because water being in the jug is impossible; the jug is that rim in which water settles inside. And among it is non-real foremost, which is not like that, as when one says, for example, 'this thing is in water' and 'that is in the sky'; and only one body is described by the real one, unlike the non-real. For there are bodies in water, sky, and others, as in a house, for example, the entire house is not occupied by it, and farther in the courtyard, farther in the neighborhood, farther in the town, farther in the region.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage defines the category of 'where' (al-ayn) and clarifies its nature. The author gives two definitions: first, as a relation of a situated body to its place, and second, as the state by which a body answers the question 'Where is it?' in reality. He then distinguishes it from the category of the relative, noting that while it seems similar, it is not merely a relation; rather, it is a state that is completed by that relation. If the relation alone were taken, it would belong to the relative category. Place itself is not relative; it is a surface that accidentally contains the contained. The author emphasizes that 'being in place' is not the same as existence, because if it were, then being in time would also be existence, leading to multiple existences for one thing, which is absurd. Thus, 'where' is a genus with species. He then differentiates between real 'where' (haqiqi awwali) and non-real 'where' (ghayr haqiqi awwali). Real where-ness is when a body occupies its specific place such that nothing else coexists in that same place, like air in the concave surface of the ether. The example of water in a jug is rejected as impossible because the jug is the rim containing the water; the water's place is the interior, not the jug itself. Non-real where-ness is when a thing is said to be in a larger area without exclusive occupancy, like 'in water' or 'in the sky', and multiple bodies can be described by such non-real where-ness.

Key Terms
الأين (al-ayn): The category of where, defined as the state of a body in its place, completed by relation to place. [Note: Stable term for the category of place across the logical tradition.]
النسبة (al-nisba): A relation, but here distinguished from the category of the relative; it is only part of the definition of where. [Note: Nisba is a general term for relation; in logic it often refers to the relation in the category of the relative.]
الكون في المكان (al-kawn fi al-makan): Being in place, distinguished from existence (al-wujud); it is a genus of species of where-ness. [Note: Kawn is used broadly; here it specifically means positional being.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author say 'if that relation were taken alone, it would be from the category of the relative'?
A) Because the relation between container and contained is not part of where.
B) Because the relation alone is a relative, but where is a state that includes the relation.
C) Because the relation is the genus of where.
D) Because the relative is a broader category.

Key Takeaway
Al-ayn is a state of being in a place, completed by a relation, and distinct from the relative category.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a cup of water. According to this passage, is the water's 'where' a relation or a state? Write a short explanation.
?
If someone says 'The book is on the table', which part of this statement belongs to the category of 'where' and which part might be from the category of 'relative'? Justify using the passage.

Real vs Non-Real Where-ness and the Refutation of the Jug Example
Author distinguishes real vs. non-real where-ness and refutes the jug example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish real where-ness (haqiqi awwali) from non-real where-ness and identify the fallacy in the water-jug example.

Core Idea
Real where-ness means exclusive occupancy; non-real is mere location in a broader space.

After defining al-ayn, the author classifies it into real and non-real types. This distinction helps clarify when an object truly occupies a place exclusively. He also debunks a common misconception involving water in a jug, demonstrating the precision required in applying the categories.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Find the terms 'haqiqi awwali' and 'ghayr haqiqi awwali' in the text. The former is real where-ness, the latter non-real.
Real: exclusive; non-real: not exclusive.
2
Understand real where-ness
Real where-ness requires that the place is the thing's exclusive place at that moment, not shared. Example: air in the concave surface of the ether.
The place cannot hold another thing at the same time.
3
Analyze the jug refutation
The author rejects 'water in the jug' as an example of real where-ness. Why? Because the jug itself is not the place; the interior surface is the place. So water is actually in the interior, not in the jug.
The jug is the container, not the place.
4
Contrast non-real where-ness
Non-real where-ness includes statements like 'the object is in water' or 'in the sky', which are broader and can apply to multiple objects simultaneously.
Multiple bodies can be said to be 'in water'.

Defining al-Ayn: The Category of Where
Definition of al-ayn and its differentiation from mudaf and wujud.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of al-ayn and distinguish it from the category of relative (al-mudaf) and from existence (al-wujud).

Core Idea
al-Ayn is a state of being in place, not a mere relation and not existence itself.

In the system of categories, one of the most subtle is the category of 'where' (al-ayn). It is easy to confuse with the relative or even with existence. The author carefully defines it and draws sharp boundaries. Understanding this distinction is key to using categories correctly in logical analysis of Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions for al-ayn
Find both definitions in the passage: one as a relation of a situated thing to its place, and another as the state of the body that answers 'where is it?'.
2
Distinguish al-ayn from al-mudaf
Note that if taken as a mere relation, it would fall under the category of relative (mudaf). But al-ayn is a state realized by that relation.
Place itself is a surface; the containing relation is from mudaf, not al-ayn.
3
Separate al-ayn from al-wujud
Follow the reductio argument: if being in place were existence, then being in time would also be existence, giving multiple existences, which is impossible.
Existence is not a genus, whereas al-ayn is a genus containing species.
4
Confirm the category status
The author states that al-ayn is a genus of species, meaning it is a category itself.
This affirms its place among the ten categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في الأين] وأما الأين فإنه يعرف بأنه هو نسبة متمكن إلى المكان الذي هو فيه، ويعرف بأنه هو الحالة التي للجسم يجاب بها حين يُسأل أنه «أين هو؟»، وهي كون الشيء في مكانه بالحقيقة. وقد مرّ من قبل أنه كيف يباين المضاف وإن كان أشد اشتباها بالمضاف بالنسبة إلى غيره. ولا يظن أنه هو مجرد نسبة إلى المكان بل هو حالة يتم حصولها بالنسبة إلى المكان. فإذا أخذت تلك النسبة وحدها كانت هي من مقولة المضاف كما هي بين الحاوي والمحوي؛ فالمكان من حيث هو المكان ليس من باب المضاف بل هو سطح يعرض عليه الاحتواء على المحوي فهذا العارض من المضاف؛ وهي النسبة التي بين المحوي والحاوي والكون في المكان ليس هو الكون في الأعيان الذي هو الوجود. فإنا قد بينا أن الوجود ليس جنسًا لما تحته. ولو كان الكون في المكان هو الوجود لكان الكون في الزمان أيضًا كذلك فيكون للشيء وجودات كثيرة؛ وذلك محال فهو إذن غير الوجود وإنه جنس الأنواع.
Chapter: On al-Ayn. As for al-Ayn, it is defined as being a relation of a situated thing to the place it is in, and it is defined as being the state of the body with which one answers when asked 'where is it?', which is the existence of a thing in its place in reality. It has already been mentioned how it differs from the relative (mudaf) even though it is more similar to the relative in relation to other things. And one should not think that it is merely a relation to place, but rather it is a state that is realized by the relation to place. So if that relation alone is taken, it belongs to the category of relative, as between the container and the contained. So place, insofar as it is place, is not from the category of relative, but rather a surface that accidentally has the containing of the contained, so this accidental property is from the relative; and it is the relation between the contained and the container. And 'being in place' is not 'being in external entities' which is existence. For we have already shown that existence is not a genus for what is under it. And if being in place were existence, then being in time would also be such, and thus a thing would have many existences; that is impossible. So it is not existence, and it is the genus of species.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage defines the category of 'ayn (where) and distinguishes it from two related concepts: the category of relative (al-mudaf) and the concept of existence (al-wujud). The author begins by giving two definitions: first, 'ayn is a relation of a located thing to its place; second, it is the state by which one answers the question 'where is it?', which is the thing's being in its actual place. He then clarifies that 'ayn is not mere relation, because if taken purely as a relation, it would fall under the category of relative (mudaf), which is a different category. Place itself, as a surface, is not relative; rather, the containing relation is what is relative. Then he argues that 'being in place' is not the same as existence (al-wujud), because existence is not a genus for its instances, whereas 'ayn includes species. He uses a reductio: if being in place were existence, then being in time would also be existence, giving a thing multiple existences, which is absurd. Therefore, 'ayn is not existence and is itself a genus of species (i.e., a category containing species).

Key Terms
الأين
المضاف
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is being in place not the same as existence?
A) Because existence is a genus.
B) Because if it were, a thing would have multiple existences.
C) Because existence is not a state.
D) Because place is relative.

Key Takeaway
Al-ayn is a distinct category defined as the state of a thing being in its actual place, not reducible to relation or existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In a passage from a usul al-fiqh text, a jurist defines 'istitan' (residence) as 'being of a person in his home for more than a certain period.' Identify whether this definition fits under al-ayn, al-mudaf, or al-wujud, and justify your answer using the criteria from this lesson.
?

Primary and Non-Primary 'Ayn
Distinguishes primary and non-primary 'ayn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between primary (haqiqi awwali) and non-primary (ghayr haqiqi) 'ayn and identify examples of each from the passage.

Core Idea
Not every 'in' means true containment—primary 'ayn is exclusive, non-primary is inclusive.

This passage from the logic text distinguishes two kinds of spatial relation. Understanding this distinction is crucial for precise definitions in Islamic sciences, especially when discussing the location of bodies in kalam or the meaning of 'being in' something.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of 'ayn
Read the passage and note the two categories: al-haqiqi al-awwal (primary real) and ghayr al-haqiqi (non-primary).
The author explicitly labels them.
2
Understand the criteria for primary 'ayn
Primary 'ayn means a body occupies a place that exclusively contains it, with no room for anything else. The example given is air in the concave surface of the ether.
The air fills that exact surface completely.
3
Analyze the refutation of a false analogy
The author rejects the analogy of water in a jug, arguing that water is not actually in the jug itself but in the inner void of the jug. The jug is the rim that contains the water.
This reinforces the exclusivity condition: the jug's material does not contain water directly.
4
Examine non-primary 'ayn examples and hierarchy
Non-primary 'ayn applies when a body is said to be in a place that can contain multiple bodies, like 'water in the sky' or 'a person in a house'. The passage lists increasingly inclusive places: house, district, town, region, etc.
Only one body can have primary 'ayn at a time, but many can share non-primary 'ayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن الكون فوق وتحت وفي الهواء وفي الماء أين ومنه ما هو حقيقي أولى وهو كون الشيء في المكان الحقيقي له وهو مكانه الذي لا يسعه فيه غيره حين هو فيه نحو الهواء في السطح المقعر من الأثير. وأما قول من قال إنه على مثال كون الماء في الكوز، فذلك باطل لاستحالة كون الماء في الكوز؛ والكوز هو ذلك الحرف الذي يستقر في جوفه الماء. ومنه غير حقيقي أولى وهو الذي لا يكون كذلك كما يقال مثلا هذا الشيء في الماء، وذلك في السماء؛ ولا يوصف بالحقيقي منهما إلا جسم واحد على خلاف غير الحقيقي . فإن من الأجسام في الماء والسماء وغيرهما كما في البيت مثلا فإن جميع البيت لا يكون مشغولا به، وأبعد منه في الدار وأبعد منه في المحلة وأبعد منه في البلدة وأبعد منه في الإقليم بل في المعمورة بل في الأرض كلها بل في العالم.
For being above, below, in air, and in water is 'where; and from it there is the primary real, which is a thing's being in its true place, which is its place that does not contain anything else while it is in it, like air in the concave surface of the ether. As for the saying of one who said that it is like the existence of water in a jug, that is false, because it is impossible for water to be in a jug; the jug is that rim inside which the water settles. And from it there is the non-primary real, which is not like that, as it is said, for example, 'this thing is in water', 'that is in the sky'; and only one body is described with the primary one of the two, unlike the non-primary. For among bodies are those in water, sky, and others, like in a house, for the entire house is not occupied by it, and farther from that in the house, and farther in the district, and farther in the town, and farther in the region, rather in the inhabited part, rather in the whole earth, rather in the world.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage classifies 'ayn into primary (haqiqi awwali) and non-primary (ghayr haqiqi). Primary 'ayn is when a body occupies its exact, exclusive place, e.g., air in the concave surface of the ether. The author refutes an incorrect analogy comparing it to water in a jug, arguing that water is not actually in the jug but in the space inside it. Non-primary 'ayn is when a body is said to be in a place that can hold multiple bodies, e.g., 'this thing is in water' or 'in the sky'. Only one body can be described with primary 'ayn at a time, whereas many can share non-primary 'ayn. The examples show a hierarchy: being in a house, district, town, region, inhabited quarter, earth, world – each is a more inclusive non-primary place.

Key Terms
الحقيقي الأول
غير الحقيقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is an example of primary (haqiqi) 'ayn?
A) Water in a jug.
B) Air in the concave surface of the ether.
C) A person in a house.
D) The same body in the world.

Key Takeaway
Primary 'ayn is exclusive containment; non-primary 'ayn allows multiple bodies to be described as 'in' the same place.

Classification of 'Ayn: Essential, Accidental, and Scopes
Author classifies 'ayn into essential/accidental and generic/specific/individual

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any instance of 'ayn as essential or accidental, and as generic, specific, or individual.

Core Idea
'Ayn is not a single kind; it divides by essence and by scope.

In the previous lesson, we learned that 'ayn is one of the nine categories. Now the author shows that 'ayn itself has subdivisions: some are essential to the thing, some accidental, and it can be generic, specific, or individual.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish essential vs accidental 'ayn
Look at the examples: fire being above (essential) vs stone in air (accidental). Essential means the attribute follows from the thing's nature; accidental means it does not.
Essential 'ayn: the thing by its nature requires that location. Accidental: the thing is merely there but could be elsewhere.
2
Identify the generic 'ayn
The genus is 'being in place' (al-kawn fi al-makan). This is the broadest level, covering all instances of 'ayn.
3
Identify the specific 'ayn
Species like 'being in water' specify the type of place. This is a narrower classification under the genus.
4
Identify the individual 'ayn
The particular being of this thing in this place, e.g., Zayd in this room. This is the most concrete level.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الأين ما يكون مأخوذا بذاته ككون النار فوق على أنه في باطن سطح الماء، ومنه ما هو بالعرض مثل كون الحجر في الهواء. وربما كان في الأين إضافة ككون الهواء فوق بالقياس إلى الماء. والأين منه جنسي وهو الكون في المكان، ومنه نوعي نحو الكون في الماء مثلا، ومنه شخصي نحو كون هذا الشيء في هذا المكان. ومنهم من زعم أن الواحد من الأين قد يوجد في جواهر كثيرة ككون عدة في السوق وذلك غلط فإن الأين الحقيقي لا يوجد في هذا المعنى بل لا يمكن. وأما غير الحقيقي نحو الكون في السوق فليس هو الواحد من الأين؛ إذ السوق وإن كان واحدًا فليس هو الأين بل الأين هو كون زيد في السوق وهو صفة لزيد بها يكون كائناً في السوق ولا يكون بها عمرو كائنا في السوق؛ فيكون التكثر بالعدد كما في السواد والبياض مثلا . ولا يقال ليس حال الأين كحال السواد والبياض فإن البياض الذي في زيد إذا عدم فلا يلزم أن يعدم الذي في عمرو. فإنه إذا كان الأين هو كونه في السوق فزيد يبطل ببطلان كونه في السوق وإن لم يبطل كون عمرو في السوق.
And of 'ayn, some are taken essentially, like the being of fire above, on the basis that it is in the inner surface of water; and some are accidentally, like the being of a stone in air. And sometimes in 'ayn there is addition, like the being of air above in relation to water. And 'ayn is generic, which is being in place; and specific, like being in water, for example; and individual, like being of this thing in this place. And some have claimed that the one of 'ayn can exist in many substances, like the existence of several in the market, and that is a mistake, for real 'ayn does not exist in this sense, rather it is impossible. As for the non-real, like being in the market, it is not the one of 'ayn; for the market, even if it is one, is not the 'ayn, but rather 'ayn is Zayd's being in the market, and it is an attribute of Zayd by which he is in the market, and it is not by it that 'Amr is in the market; thus there is multiplicity by number, as in blackness and whiteness, for example. And it is not said that the case of 'ayn is not like the case of blackness and whiteness, for the whiteness in Zayd, when it ceases, does not necessitate that the whiteness in 'Amr ceases. For if 'ayn is his being in the market, Zayd ceases with the cessation of his being in the market, even if 'Amr's being in the market does not cease.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage further classifies 'ayn into three divisions: essential (bi-dhatihi) vs. accidental (bi'l-'arad), then generic (jinsi), specific (naw'i), and individual (shakhsi). It then addresses a potential objection: some claim that the same 'ayn (e.g., 'being in the market') can be shared by multiple substances. The author refutes this by distinguishing real 'ayn from non-real. For non-real 'ayn, like 'being in the market', although the market is one place, the 'ayn itself is each person's individual state of being in that market; it is not a single entity but multiple, like whiteness in different subjects. The attribute inheres in each subject individually, so the cessation of one does not affect the other. This is clarified by the analogy with blackness and whiteness.

Key Terms
بالذات
بالعرض
جنسي
نوعي
شخصي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Being in water.
C) Zayd's being in this specific room.
D) A stone being in air accidentally.

Key Takeaway
'Ayn is classified into essential/accidental and generic/specific/individual, enabling precise philosophical analysis.

?

?
