Understanding the Obscure Category 'al-Judda'
Author introduces a problematic category 'al-judda' defined as a body's relation to an adjacent thing that moves with it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of 'al-judda' and distinguish its particular and universal instances as outlined by the author.

Core Idea
Al-judda is a problematic category that tests the limits of the Aristotelian categorical framework.

In this unique passage, the logician introduces a category that is not well-defined: 'al-judda', the relation of a body to an adjacent moving object. He then questions whether it can be a genuine genus, and lists various instances to illustrate its ambiguity. This lesson will guide you through understanding his analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Find the phrase: 'the relation of a body to an adjacent thing that moves with it.' Note the key components: relation, body, adjacency, movement.
2
Recognize the author's doubt
The author says 'it is among those things that require investigation' and its understanding is not agreed upon. This indicates uncertainty about whether 'al-judda' is a genuine higher genus.
3
Distinguish particular vs. universal examples
The author gives particular examples (e.g., 'this putting-on-a-shirt') and universal examples (e.g., 'putting-on-a-shirt' in general). The difference is specificity: particular refers to a specific act, universal to the general concept.
4
Differentiate essential vs. accidental instances
The author mentions 'essential like the state when in its hide' and 'accidental'. Essential instances are inherent properties, accidental are not. Understand this distinction.
5
Reflect on the significance for category theory
This passage shows that not all relations fit neatly into the ten categories. It highlights the need for careful ontological analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [في الجدة] وأما «الجُدة» وهي نسبة الجسم إلى ملاصق ينتقل بانتقال ما هو المنسوب إليه ؛ فإنها من جملة ما فيه نظر فإنه لم يتفق إلى هذه الغاية فهمها وهي أن تكون معقولة من المعقولات التي هي الأجناس العالية حتى يكون تحتها من الأمور التي هي كالأنواع. والأمور التي هي تحتها فهي التي يقال عليها هذا المعنى باشتراك الاسم أو بالتشابه. وكما يقال الشيء من الشيء، والشيء في الشيء والشيء على الشيء والشيء مع الشيء. ولا يدرى أي / شيء يوجب أن يكون مقولة الجُدة جنسًا لتلك الأمور الجزئية ولا يوجب مثله منها ولكن منه جزئي كهذا التقمص وهذا التسلح وهذا التنعل مثلا، ومنه كلي كالتقمص والتسلح والتعمم والتجثم مطلقا. ومنه ذاتي كحال عند إهابها، ومنه عرضي
Section: [On al-judda] And as for 'al-judda', it is the relation of a body to an adjacent [thing] that moves with the movement of that to which it is attributed; indeed it is among those things that require investigation, for its understanding has not been agreed upon up to this point, and [the question is] whether it is intelligible among the intelligibles that are the higher genera such that there are under it things that are like species. And the things under it are those to which this meaning is said by homonymy or by similarity. And just as one says 'the thing from the thing', 'the thing in the thing', 'the thing on the thing', and 'the thing with the thing'. And it is not known what thing necessitates that the category of al-judda be a genus for those particular things, nor does it necessitate the like of it from them, but from it [there is] particular like this putting-on-a-shirt, this arming-oneself, and this putting-on-shoes for example, and from it [there is] universal like putting-on-a-shirt, arming-oneself, putting-on-a-turban, and sitting-on-the-ground absolutely. And from it [there is] essential like the state when in its hide, and accidental.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage discusses an obscure category called 'al-judda' (possibly a variant of 'al-malaka' meaning 'state' or 'habit'). The author defines it as the relation of a body to something adjacent that moves when the body moves—i.e., an attachment that travels with the object, like a garment on a person. The author notes that this category is not well-understood: it is unclear whether it is a genuine higher genus under which particular cases fall. The particular examples include specific acts like 'putting on this shirt' or 'arming oneself with this sword', while universal examples include the general concepts of wearing a shirt, arming, turban-wearing, and sitting on the ground. The author also distinguishes between essential (dhati) instances, like the state of an animal in its hide, and accidental ('aradi) instances. The passage highlights the difficulty of classifying such relations into the standard Aristotelian categories.

النسبة
ملاصق

The Category of Position and Its Subdivisions
Author defines position and its subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of the category of position and distinguish its essential and accidental subdivisions.

Core Idea
Position is a relation, not a simple quality.

The category of position (al-jidda) is one of the ten Aristotelian categories, but it often causes confusion because it is a relational notion rather than a simple quality. In this lesson, we examine the author's definition and the divisions he proposes within this category, including particular vs. universal and essential vs. accidental. Understanding these distinctions helps in classifying actions and states in Islamic thought, such as postures in prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of al-jidda
Find the sentence in the Arabic that defines position. It is a relation of a body to an adjacent thing that moves with the body. Note the three key elements: body, adjacent thing, and movement.
The phrase 'نسبة الجسم إلى ملاصق ينتقل بانتقال ما هو المنسوب إليه' captures the essence.
2
Recognize the difficulty the author raises
The author says it is unclear how position can be a genus for its species. Understand that the relationship between the category and its instances is not straightforward; some instances are called by the same name only by homonymy or analogy.
This highlights the complexity of the category: not all 'positions' are of the same kind.
3
Classify the subdivisions: particular vs. universal
Look for examples: a particular act (putting on this shirt) vs. a universal act (putting on shirts in general). The author distinguishes instances that are specific (جزئي) and those that are general (كلي).
The same distinction applies to other examples: armor, sandals, turban.
4
Classify the subdivisions: essential vs. accidental
The passage ends by mentioning essential (ذاتي) and accidental (عرضي) positions. Think of the essential as inherent to the body's state (like the hide being attached), and accidental as external adornments.
This division helps in fiqh when determining necessary vs. recommended postures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter: On Position. As for 'position' (al-jidda), it is the relation of a body to an adjacent thing that moves with the movement of that to which it is attributed. It is among matters requiring reflection, for it has not been agreed upon up to this point to understand it, namely that it be an intelligible among the intelligibles that are the highest genera, such that under it are matters that are like species. The matters under it are those to which this meaning is applied by homonymy or by analogy. Just as one says: a thing from a thing, a thing in a thing, a thing on a thing, and a thing with a thing. And it is unknown what necessitates that the category of position be a genus for those particular matters, and it does not necessitate the like from them. But from it is a particular like this putting on a shirt, this putting on armor, and this putting on sandals, for example; and from it is a universal like putting on a shirt, putting on armor, wearing a turban, and taking shelter absolutely. And from it is essential like the state of its skin, and from it is accidental.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage defines the category of position (al-jidda), one of the ten Aristotelian categories. The author begins by stating that position is a relation of a body to an adjacent thing that moves with the body. This relation is difficult to fully grasp because it is a higher genus under which many specific positions fall. The author notes that the specific instances of position (e.g., wearing a shirt, armor, sandals) are called by the same name but differ in reality, either by homonymy (common name) or analogy. The author then lists types of positions: particular (like a specific instance of putting on a shirt) and universal (like the act of wearing shirts in general). The division into essential and accidental is also mentioned. The commentary follows the author's exposition: first the definition, then the difficulty, then the subdivisions.

Action and Passion as Process Categories
Author defines action and passion as non-permanent qualities of agent and patient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the categories of action (an yaf'ala) and passion (an yanfa'ila) from their completed states (fi'l and infi'al) and correctly identify which term applies in a given ongoing process.

Core Idea
Action and passion are ongoing processes, not finished results.

In this passage, the author defines two of Aristotle's categories: action and passion. Unlike the common nouns 'act' and 'passion', which often refer to completed events, the verbal nouns 'an yaf'ala' and 'an yanfa'ila' describe the continuous state of effecting or being affected. Understanding this distinction is crucial for precise logical definition, especially when discussing change and causality in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories defined
Locate the terms 'an yaf'ala' and 'an yanfa'ila' in the Arabic. They are the subjects of the definition.
2
Extract the genus of these categories
The author says they are 'a quality that exists in a thing' (هيئة توجد في الشيء). So the genus is 'quality' (hay'a).
3
Determine the differentia
The quality is not present before or after the process, only during it. It is continuous and changes as the process continues. So the differentia is 'existing only during an ongoing process of change'.
4
Contrast with completed state terms
The author explains why they did not use 'fi'l' and 'infi'al': those nouns refer to the result after the process stops, like 'the garment is torn' after tearing. The categories are the processes themselves.
This is a key clarification for logical precision.
5
Apply to examples
Use examples like heating (التسخين) – 'an yaf'ala' for the heater while heating, 'an yanfa'ila' for the object being heated. The completed states would be 'heat' (sukhuna) and 'heating' (tasakhkhun?) as results.
Practice with other processes like cutting, cooling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: [ في أن يفعل وفي أن ينفعل ] وأما أن يفعل وأن ينفعل فيفهم تصورهما هيئة توجد في الشيء لا يكون قبلها ولا بعدها البتة في الحد الذي يكون معها من الكيف والكم والأين والوضع بل لا يزال يفارق الشيء شيئًا ويتوجه إلى شيء ما دامت موجودة كالسواد ما دام في التسود؛ فالشيء الذي فيه هذه الهيئة على اتصالها فهو منفعل وحاله أن ينفعل. والشيء الذي منه هذه الحالة فهو فاعل وحاله أن يفعل. وبالجملة أن يفعل هو تأثير الشيء في غيره أثرًا غير قار الذات؛ فحالة ما دام تؤثر هي أن يفعل؛ وذلك مثل التسخين ما دام يسخن والتبريد ما دام يبرد مثلا. وأن ينفعل هو تأثر الشيء من غيره ما دام في التأثر كالتسخن والتبرد والتقطع ونحو ذلك. وإنما قالوا أن يفعل وأن ينفعل ولم يقولوا فعل وانفعال؛ لأن الانفعال قد يقال للحاصل الذي انقطعت الحركة إليه كما يقال في هذا الثوب افتراق إذا حصل واستقر . ويقال انفعال إذا كان الشيء يعد في الحركة وكذلك الفعل الذي هو كالقطع مثلا قد يقال عند استكماله، وقد يقال حين ما قطع.
Chapter: On 'To Act' and 'To Be Acted Upon'. As for 'to act' and 'to be acted upon', understanding them is a quality that exists in a thing which is not before it nor after it at all, in the limit that is with it of quality, quantity, place, and position. Rather, it continually departs from a thing and faces something as long as it exists, like blackness as long as it is in the process of becoming black. So the thing in which this quality is in its continuity is passive, and its state is 'to be acted upon'. And the thing from which this state comes is active, and its state is 'to act'. In summary, 'to act' is the effect of a thing upon another with a non-enduring effect; so the state as long as it effects is 'to act', such as heating as long as it heats and cooling as long as it cools, for example. And 'to be acted upon' is the being affected of a thing from another as long as it is in the state of being affected, like being heated, being cooled, being cut, and the like. They said 'to act' and 'to be acted upon' and did not say 'act' and 'passion' because 'passion' may be said for the result when movement has ceased, as one says 'this garment is torn' when it has occurred and settled. And 'passion' is said when the thing is in movement, and likewise 'act' which is like cutting, for example, may be said upon its completion, and may be said at the time of cutting.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage defines the categories of action (an yaf'ala) and passion (an yanfa'ila). The author explains that they are qualities that exist in a thing only during a process, not before or after. They are continuous and changeable. The author distinguishes between the agent (active) and patient (passive) by the direction of the effect. The explanation uses examples from natural processes like heating and cooling. The author also clarifies why the verbal nouns (an yaf'ala/an yanfa'ila) are used instead of the nouns (fi'l/infi'al): the latter often refer to completed states, while the categories refer to ongoing processes. This is a key distinction for understanding temporal change in Aristotelian categories. The commentary follows the sequence: definition, distinction of active/passive, summary with examples, and the linguistic choice.

Key Terms
أن يفعل (an yaf'ala): The category of action; the quality of being an agent effecting change in another continuously.
أن ينفعل (an yanfa'ila): The category of passion; the quality of being a patient receiving change continuously.
فعل (fi'l): Act; may refer to the completed state, not the ongoing process. [Note: In logic, fi'l is distinguished from the category 'an yaf'ala' by being the accomplished effect.]
انفعال (infi'al): Passion; may refer to the resulting state after change. [Note: Similar to fi'l, infi'al often denotes the post-process state.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because 'fi'l' refers to the completed act, while the category describes the ongoing process.
B) Because 'fi'l' is grammatically incorrect.
C) Because 'an yaf'ala' is a verbal noun but 'fi'l' is not.
D) Because the category is only about potential actions.

Key Takeaway
The categories of action and passion describe ongoing processes, and the verbal nouns (an yaf'ala, an yanfa'ila) are used precisely to capture that dynamic nature.

Action and Passion as Dynamic Categories
Author defines action and passion as non-permanent states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the categories of action (أن يفعل) and passion (أن ينفعل) from static categories and completed states, and identify their contrariety and intensity.

Core Idea
Action and passion are processes, not permanent states.

The categories of action and passion are among the ten Aristotelian categories adopted in Islamic logic. Unlike categories like quality or posture, which describe static attributes, action and passion describe processes of change. This passage carefully defines them as temporary 'states' (هيئات) that accompany an ongoing effect. Understanding these categories is key to correctly analysing causal and metaphysical discussions in Islamic kalam and falsafa.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the core definitions
The author defines أن يفعل as the influence of one thing on another producing a non-permanent effect, and أن ينفعل as being affected by another as long as the impact lasts. Identify the agent (فاعل) and patient (منفعل) in each definition.
Focus on the phrase 'غير قار الذات' (non-permanent essence) for action.
2
Examine the example of blackening
The passage uses blackness during the process of blackening (السواد ما دام في التسود). Identify what is the agent (cause of blackening) and patient (thing becoming black). Note that the state exists only during the process.
Compare with the completed state of blackness.
3
Distinguish from completed states
The author contrasts أن يفعل/أن ينفعل with فعل/انفعال. The former are the ongoing processes, the latter are the results after motion ceases. Find the example of tearing (افتراق) and cutting to see the difference.
This distinction prevents confusion between dynamic and static categories.
4
Understand contrariety
The passage says that blackening is opposite to whitening, just as black is opposite to white. Contrariety applies to processes: the direction from one opposite to another is opposite to the reverse direction.
Contrariety in processes mirrors that in static qualities.
5
Analyse intensity
The author discusses two measures of intensity: proximity to the endpoint (e.g., blackening closer to black is more intense) and speed (faster arrival to goal is more intense). Consider how both measures apply.
Intensity is relative to the subject's starting point or speed of change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في أن يفعل وفي أن ينفعل ]
وأما أن يفعل وأن ينفعل فيفهم تصور هما هيئة توجد في الشيء لا يكون قبلها ولا بعدها البتة في الحد الذي يكون معها من الكيف والكم والأين والوضع بل لا يزال يفارق الشيء شيئًا ويتوجه إلى شيء ما دامت موجودة كالسواد ما دام في التسود؛ فالشيء الذي فيه هذه الهيئة على اتصالها فهو منفعل وحاله أن ينفعل. والشيء الذي منه هذه الحالة فهو فاعل وحاله أن يفعل. وبالجملة أن يفعل هو تأثير الشيء في غيره أثرًا غير قار الذات؛ فحالة ما دام تؤثر هي أن يفعل؛ وذلك مثل التسخين ما دام يسخن والتبريد ما دام يبرد مثلا. وأن ينفعل هو تأثر الشيء من غيره ما دام في التأثر كالتسخن والتبرد والتقطع ونحو ذلك. وإنما قالوا أن يفعل وأن ينفعل ولم يقولوا فعل وانفعال؛ لأن الانفعال قد يقال للحاصل الذي انقطعت الحركة إليه كما يقال في هذا الثوب افتراق إذا حصل واستقر . ويقال انفعال إذا كان الشيء يعد في الحركة وكذلك الفعل الذي هو كالقطع مثلا قد يقال عند استكماله، وقد يقال حين ما قطع.
وأما لفظة أنه يفعل وأنه ينفعل فهي مخصوصة بالحالة التي فيها التوجه إلى الغاية، وكذا القيام الذي هو النهوض والجلوس الذي هو المصير إلى الأمر الذي يستقر فيهما: إما أن يكونا من هذه المقولة أو مما يناسب هذه المقولة. وأما هيئة القيام المستقرة وهيئة الجلوس كذلك؛ فهما من الوضع، وهيئة الاحتراق من الكيف، وهيئة تمام النشو من الكم، وهيئة الاستقرار في المكان من الأين؛ فهذه المقولة وما يناسبها هي توجه إلى إحدى هذه الغايات كما مر : وهذه المقولة مما يقبل التضاد فإن التوجه من ضد إلى ضد مخالف بالحد التوجه من ذلك إليه . وموضوعهما واحد وبينهما أبعد الخلاف؛ وذلك كابيضاض الأسود واسوداد الأبيض وكصعود السافل ونزول العالي. فكما أن السواد ضد البياض فكذلك التسود ضد التبيض؛ وإنهما مما يقبل الأشد والأضعف لا من جهة القرب إلى الطرف الذي هو السواد بل بالقياس إلى الأسود. وفرق بين الاسوداد عن الحاصل القار وبين السواد؛ إذ الاسوداد لا يفتقر في تعقله سوادًا إلى تعقل حركة إليه بخلاف الاسوداد الذي هو غاية الحركة.
ثم التسود يكون أشد من تسود آخر إذا كان أقرب من الاسوداد الذي هو الطرف، وكذلك السواد أشد من سواد آخر إذا كان أقرب من السواد هو الطرف. وأيضًا فالاسوداد أشد من جهة السرعة فإن وصول الأسرع إلى الغاية قبل وصول الأبطأ إليها. والفرق بين هذا وذلك أن أحدهما في ذلك مبتدئ من حد أقرب إلى البياض والآخر من حد أبعد منه غير أن يتفاوت بينهما في السرعة والبطء على خلاف هذا؛ وهو الاشتداد من جهة السرعة.
Section: [On 'to affect' and 'to be affected']
As for 'to affect' and 'to be affected', their concept is understood as a state (hay'a) existing in a thing that neither was before it nor after it at all in the limit [moment] in which it is with [the categories of] quality, quantity, position, and posture; rather, it never ceases to leave one thing and turn towards another as long as it exists, like blackness as long as it is in the process of blackening. So the thing in which this state exists in its continuity is the patient (munfa'il), and its condition is 'to be affected'. And the thing from which this condition [emanates] is the agent (fa'il), and its condition is 'to affect'. In summary, 'to affect' is the influence of one thing upon another in a non-permanent essence; so the state as long as it influences is 'to affect'; such as heating as long as it heats, and cooling as long as it cools, for example. And 'to be affected' is the being-affected of a thing from another as long as it is in the state of being affected, like being heated, being cooled, being torn, and so on. They only said 'to affect' and 'to be affected' and did not say 'effect' and 'affection', because 'affection' may be said for the result towards which the motion has ceased, as it is said of this garment 'tearing' when it has occurred and settled. And 'affection' is said when the thing is [still] in motion; likewise, the act which is like cutting, for example, may be said at its completion, and may be said at the moment of cutting.
As for the expression 'that it affects' and 'that it is affected', they are specific to the state in which there is direction towards the goal, like standing which is arising, and sitting which is the transition to the condition in which they settle: either they are from this category or from what is appropriate to this category. As for the settled state of standing and the settled state of sitting similarly, they are from posture (wad'); the state of burning is from quality (kayf); the state of completed growth is from quantity (kamm); the state of settling in a place is from position (ayn). So this category and what is appropriate to it are direction towards one of these goals, as mentioned. And this category admits of contrariety, for the direction from one opposite to another is different in definition from the direction to that [opposite]. Their subject is one, and between them is the furthest difference. That is like the whitening of the black and the blackening of the white, and like the ascent of the lower and the descent of the higher. Just as blackness is opposite to whiteness, so too blackening is opposite to whitening. And they admit of intensity and weakness, not from the perspective of proximity to the endpoint which is blackness, but by comparison to the black [subject]. There is a difference between 'being black' (al-iswidad) as a permanent acquisition and 'blackness' (al-sawad); for 'being black' does not require in its conceptualization of blackness a conceptualization of motion towards it, unlike [the process of] 'blackening' (al-iswidad) which is the goal of motion.
Then blackening is more intense than another blackening if it is closer to the blackness which is the endpoint, and likewise blackness is more intense than another blackness if it is closer to the blackness which is the endpoint. Also, blackening is more intense in terms of speed, for the arrival of the faster to the goal is before the arrival of the slower to it. And the difference between this and that is that one of them starts from a limit closer to whiteness and the other from a limit farther from it, except that they differ in speed and slowness contrary to this: and that is intensification in terms of speed.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of two of Aristotle's categories: action (أن يفعل) and passion (أن ينفعل). The author begins by describing these as temporary states that exist in a thing only during a process of change, not before or after. They are associated with movement from one quality, quantity, position, or posture to another. The example given is blackness (السواد) during the process of blackening (التسود). The thing undergoing the process is the patient (منفعل) and its state is 'to be affected' (أن ينفعل); the thing causing the change is the agent (فاعل) and its state is 'to affect' (أن يفعل). The author clarifies that action is an influence producing a non-permanent effect, like heating as long as it heats. Passion is being affected by another, like being heated. They distinguish these from the completed states (فعل and انفعال), which refer to the result after motion has ceased. The second part discusses the expressions 'that it affects' and 'that it is affected' as specific to states directed towards a goal. Examples like standing (as arising) and sitting (as transitioning to a settled state) are given, and then contrasted with the settled states, which belong to other categories: settled standing is posture (الوضع), settled sitting is posture, burning is quality, completed growth is quantity, settling in place is position. So action/passion are the processes directed towards these outcomes. The passage then addresses contrariety: the direction from one opposite to another is itself opposite to the direction in reverse, e.g., blackening vs. whitening. They admit of intensity: not simply by proximity to the endpoint, but relative to the subject's starting point. A distinction is made between the process of becoming black (الاسوداد) and the permanent state of blackness (السواد): the process implies motion towards blackness, while the acquired state does not. Intensity can be measured by closeness to the endpoint or by speed of change. This passage provides careful metaphysical analysis of these dynamic categories.

Key Takeaway
Action and passion are dynamic categories that describe ongoing processes of affecting and being affected, and they admit of contrariety and intensity.

Types of Motion in Categories and the Ancient Restriction
Author classifies motion within categories and notes the ancient restriction of motion to four categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two types of change (instantaneous vs gradual) and identify which categories the ancients restricted motion to.

Core Idea
Motion is not all change; substantial generation is instantaneous, not motion.

In this passage, the author explains how motion is classified within the Aristotelian categories. He introduces the concepts of stationary (qar) and flowing (sayyal) in each category, noting that all categories can have change. However, he contrasts this with the ancient Peripatetic view that restricts motion to only four categories where change is gradual. Understanding this distinction helps clarify the precise meaning of motion in Islamic natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of change in each category
Find the terms القار (stationary) and السيال (flowing) in the Arabic text. These represent the two states within each category: fixed and changing.
2
Understand the author's broad view
The author states that 'the exit from potentiality to actuality is not confined to some categories' – meaning change can occur in all ten categories.
3
Contrast with the ancients' view
The ancients restrict the term 'motion' to gradual change, which only occurs in four categories: quantity, quality, place, and position (al-kawn, al-kayf, al-ayn, al-wad'). They exclude substance because substantial change is instantaneous.
4
Recognize why substantial change is not motion
The author explains that generation and corruption of substance happen 'دفعة' (instantaneously), not gradually, so they are called motion only metaphorically.
5
Summarize the key distinction
The term 'motion' can be used broadly (all change) or strictly (gradual change). The author adopts the broad usage but acknowledges the strict view of the ancients.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم للحركة أنواع وأصناف من المقولات؛ فالجوهر منه قار ومنه سيال وهو الحركة في الجوهر أي الكون والفساد. وكذلك الكم والكيف وغيرهما فإن منها ما هو قار، ومنها ما هو سيال لما أن الخروج من القوة إلى الفعل لا يختص بالبعض منها؛ وهذا على خلاف ما ذهب إليه القدماء في استعمال لفظ الحركة فإنه لا يشترك فيها جميع أنواع هذه الخروجات عن القوة إلى الفعل عندهم بل ما يكون خروجًا على سبيل التدريج؛ وذلك لا يكون إلا في مقولات معدولة مثل الكم والكيف والأين والوضع؛ إذ الحركة في المقولة معناها أن الجسم يتغيَّر من صنف من تلك المقولة إلى صنف آخر منها؛ وذلك لا يمكن إلا في هذه الأربع. فقولنا إن الجوهر فيه حركة قول مجازي لما أن الطبيعة الجوهرية إذا فسدت فسدت دفعة وإذا حدثت حدثت دفعة؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد.
Then motion has types and kinds within the categories. Substance (jawhar) includes that which is stationary (qar) and that which is flowing (sayyal), which is motion in substance, namely generation and corruption. Similarly, quantity, quality, and others include both stationary and flowing, because the exit from potentiality to actuality is not confined to some of them. This is contrary to what the ancients [predecessors] held in using the term 'motion'; they do not apply it to all types of these exits from potentiality to actuality, but only to that which is gradual. Thus motion only occurs in certain categories like quantity, quality, place, and position (wad') – because motion in a category means the body changes from one species of that category to another, which is only possible in these four. So our saying that substance has motion is metaphorical, because the substantial nature, when it corrupts, corrupts instantaneously, and when it comes to be, comes to be instantaneously; this will be known later.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage discusses the classification of motion within the Aristotelian categories. The author distinguishes between stationary (قار) and flowing (سيال) in each category, noting that the exit from potentiality to actuality occurs in all categories, not just some. However, he contrasts this with the view of the 'ancients' (presumably earlier Peripatetics) who restrict the term 'motion' to gradual change, which only occurs in four categories: quantity, quality, place, and position. According to them, substantial change (generation/corruption) is instantaneous, not motion, so saying 'motion in substance' is metaphorical. The author acknowledges this but suggests the broader usage. The passage highlights a key point in Islamic physics: the division of types of change and the specific meaning of motion. For instance, the growth of a plant is a gradual change in quantity, while the creation of a new species is instantaneous generation.

Defining Gradual Change and Refining the Concept of Motion
Author defines gradual change and critiques ancient view restricting motion to four categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish gradual change from instantaneous change and evaluate the ancient claim that motion occurs only in four categories.

Core Idea
True motion is gradual change, instant-by-instant difference in state, not every kind of transition.

In this passage, the author refines the concept of motion by defining gradual change precisely. He challenges the ancient philosophers who restricted motion to four categories, arguing instead that motion as exit from potentiality to actuality occurs across all categories. He then provides a rigorous definition of gradual origination and responds to potential objections about the circularity of defining time, asserting that time concepts are primary intuitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing views on motion's scope
Find where the author states that motion exists in substance, quantity, quality, etc., and contrast it with the ancient view that motion is only in quantity, quality, position, and place. Note the reasoning: the exit from potentiality to actuality is not restricted to a few categories.
Look for the phrase 'على خلاف ما ذهب إليه القدماء' (contrary to what the ancients held).
2
Extract the definition of gradual change (al-huduth 'ala al-tadrij)
Locate the sentence beginning with 'والحدوث على التدريج عبارة عن...' and paraphrase it. The definition states that a body undergoes gradual change when at every instant its state is different from the state at the instant before or after.
This is a rasm (descriptive definition) not a hadd (essential definition).
3
Apply the definition to the semen example
The author addresses the objection that semen gradually transforms into an animal (a substantial change). According to the definition, is this really gradual? Notice that the author breaks it down into a series of accidental (quantity/quality) changes, not a single gradual substantial change. The substance changes instantaneously at each stage.
The key phrase: 'الحركة في الأربع التي مرَّ ذكرها' (motion is in the four mentioned).
4
Analyze the response about primary intuitions
The author anticipates a circularity objection: understanding 'now', 'before', 'after' requires time, which requires motion. He counters that these concepts are primary intuitions (awwaliyat) that do not need definition. This shows how the definition of gradual change is foundational.
Look for 'فإن تصورات هذه الأمور من الأوليات' (the conceptions of these matters are among primary intuitions).

ثم للحركة أنواع وأصناف من المقولات؛ فالجوهر منه قار ومنه سيال وهو الحركة في الجوهر أي الكون والفساد. وكذلك الكم والكيف وغيرهما فإن منها ما هو ،قار، ومنها ما هو سيال لما أن الخروج من القوة إلى الفعل لا يختص بالبعض منها؛ وهذا على خلاف ما ذهب إليه القدماء في استعمال لفظ الحركة فإنه لا يشترك فيها جميع أنواع هذه الخروجات عن القوة إلى الفعل عندهم بل ما يكون خروجًا على سبيل التدريج؛ وذلك لا يكون إلا في مقولات معدولة مثل الكم والكيف والأين والوضع؛ إذ الحركة في المقولة معناها أن الجسم يتغيَّر من صنف من تلك المقولة إلى صنف آخر منها؛ وذلك لا يمكن إلا في هذه الأربع. فقولنا إن الجوهر فيه حركة قول مجازي لما أن الطبيعة الجوهرية إذا فسدت فسدت دفعة وإذا حدثت حدثت دفعة؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد. ولا يقال كيف هو والمني مثلا يتغيّر من صورة جوهرية إلى صورة جوهرية أخرى على سبيل التدريج. فإن المني إلى أن يتكون حيوانا تعرض له تكونات أخرى مشتملة على استحالات في الكيف والكم فيكون المني لا يزال يستحيل يسيرًا يسيرًا وهو بعد مني إلى أن تنخلع صورته وتصير علقة. وكذلك إلى أن يستحيل مضغة ثم عظما ثم لحما وعصبا وغير ذلك بل أمرًا من الأمور التي لا ندركها؛ وكذلك إلى أن يقبل صورة الحياة. ثم يستحيل كذلك إلى أن يشتد فينفصل لكن ظاهر الحال يفهم أن يكون في الجوهر حركة؛ وليس كذلك بل الحركة في الأربع التي مرَّ ذكرها لما فيها من الحدوث على سبيل التدريج. والحدوث على التدريج عبارة عن كون الجسم بحيث لا يعرض آن من آنات إلا وتكون حاله فيه بخلاف حاله في الآن الذي يكون قبله أو بعده. ولا يقال إن تصور الآن والقبلية والبعدية متوقف على تصور الزمان المتوقف على تصور الحركة. فإن تصورات هذه الأمور من الأوليات فلا حاجة لحصولها إلى شيء من التعريفات.
Then motion has types and kinds from the categories: substance has a stable and a fluid, which is motion in substance, i.e., generation and corruption. Likewise quantity, quality, and others, for among them there is what is stable and what is fluid, because the exit from potentiality to actuality is not specific to some of them. This is contrary to what the ancients held in their use of the term 'motion', for it does not include all types of these exits from potentiality to actuality according to them, but only what is an exit in a gradual manner. That is only in modified categories such as quantity, quality, 'where', and position. For motion in a category means that the body changes from one species of that category to another species of it, and that is only possible in these four. So our saying that there is motion in substance is a metaphorical saying, because the substantial nature, when it perishes, perishes instantaneously, and when it occurs, occurs instantaneously; that is known later. And it is not said how is it that the semen, for example, changes from one substantial form to another substantial form gradually. For the semen, until it becomes an animal, undergoes other formations including transformations in quality and quantity. So the semen continues to transform little by little, while it is still semen, until its form is shed and it becomes a clot. Likewise until it transforms into a foetus, then bone, then flesh, nerves, and other things, even regarding matters we do not perceive. Likewise until it accepts the form of life. Then it transforms similarly until it becomes strong and separates. But the apparent situation suggests that there is motion in substance, yet it is not so. Rather motion is in the four mentioned, because in them there is origination in a gradual manner. Gradual origination is an expression for the body being such that no moment occurs but its state at it is different from its state at the moment before or after. And it is not said that the conception of 'now', 'before', and 'after' depends on the conception of time, which depends on the conception of motion. For the conceptions of these matters are among primary intuitions, so there is no need for them to obtain any definitions.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage discusses the types of motion across the categories and refines the ancient view. The author states that motion, as exit from potentiality to actuality, occurs in substance, quantity, quality, etc., each having both stable and fluid aspects. This contradicts the ancients who restrict motion to gradual change and only in four categories: quantity, quality, position, and place. The author argues that motion in substance is metaphorical because substantial change happens instantaneously. He addresses the objection of gradual human development from semen to animal, explaining that the apparent gradual change is actually a series of qualitative and quantitative transformations, not a single substantial motion. He then defines gradual origination (al-huduth 'ala al-tadrij) as the body's state being different at every moment. He responds to an objection that concepts like 'now', 'before', and 'after' depend on time, which depends on motion, by stating that these are primary intuitions (awwaliyat) needing no definition.

Motion in Quantity: Proof and Classification
Author proves motion in quantity and defines its types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of motion in quantity (rarefaction/condensation and growth/shriveling) and explain why motion in quantity is possible.

Core Idea
Prime matter has no quantity, so it can receive any quantity.

In this passage, the author establishes that motion can occur in the category of quantity, and he distinguishes between two fundamental kinds: that which involves addition or disintegration of matter (growth and shriveling) and that which does not (rarefaction and condensation). Understanding these distinctions is crucial for later discussions in natural philosophy and kalam about change in bodies.

Guided Path
1
Trace the author's proof
The author begins by asserting motion in quantity is undeniable. His proof: a body is composed of matter and form; prime matter has no inherent quantity, so it can receive any magnitude. Therefore quantity can increase or decrease, implying motion. Identify this chain.
This uses the concept of prime matter (hayula), which is receptive to all quantities.
2
Identify the two primary categories
The author says motion in quantity is of two kinds: by rarefaction/condensation and by growth/shriveling. Note that he pairs increase (growth and rarefaction) and decrease (shriveling and condensation).
3
Distinguish the subtypes by their causes
For increase: if due to addition of matter in accordance with nature, it is growth; if due to expansion without addition, it is rarefaction. For decrease: if due to disintegration of parts, it is shriveling; if without disintegration, it is condensation.
The key is whether addition or loss of matter occurs.
4
Relate to observable phenomena
The author states these states are perceptible. Think of Islamic natural examples: a growing plant (growth), heated metal expanding (rarefaction), a withering leaf (shriveling), water vapor condensing on a cool surface (condensation).
These examples cement the definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الحركة في الكم فإنها من جملة ما لا يسع إنكاره لأحد لما كان الجسم مركباً من الهيولى والصورة. والهيولى ليس لها في ذاتها مقدار ، وما لا مقدار له في ذاته كانت نسبته إلى جميع المقادير واحدة. فمادة الجسم الكبير إذن قابلة لمقدار صغيرة وبالعكس فتكون الكمية فيه قابلة للتزيد والتنقص . وما يكون كذلك ففيه من الحركات وذلك فيما نحن فيه على وجهين؛ إما بالتخلخل والتكاثف وإما بالنبو والذبول؛ وذلك لأن الأخذ منه إلى الزيادة إن كان بدخول الزائد على وفق الطبيعة يسمى نموا، وإن كان انبساطه جوهره لا عن زيادة يسمى تخلخلا . وأما الأخذ إلى النقصان فإن كان تخلل أجزائه يسمى ذبولا، وإن كان من غير تخلل يسمى تكاثفا، وهذه الأحوال محسوسة في الأجسام.
As for motion in quantity, it is among that which no one can deny, because the body is composed of matter and form. Matter has no quantity in itself, and what has no quantity in itself, its relation to all quantities is equal. Thus the matter of a large body is receptive to a small quantity, and vice versa, so quantity in it is susceptible to increase and decrease. What is such has motions, and that in our subject is of two kinds: either by rarefaction and condensation, or by growth and shriveling. That is because the taking towards increase, if it is by entry of the increase in accordance with nature, is called growth; if it is expansion of its substance without increase, it is called rarefaction. As for the taking towards decrease, if it is by disintegration of its parts, it is called shriveling; if without disintegration, it is called condensation. And these states are perceptible in bodies.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage defines motion in quantity and its subtypes. The author begins by asserting that motion in quantity is undeniable because bodies consist of matter (hayula) and form. Prime matter has no inherent quantity, so it can accept any quantity. Thus quantity in a body can increase or decrease. This gives rise to two kinds of motion: one through rarefaction (takhalkhul) and condensation (takathuf), and another through growth (numuww) and shriveling (dhubul). Growth occurs when increase is by addition of matter in accordance with nature; rarefaction occurs by expansion without addition. Shriveling occurs when parts disintegrate; condensation occurs without disintegration. These phenomena are observable in bodies, e.g., in natural and artificial objects.

Gradual Generation and Motion in Substance
Definition of gradual generation and defense against circularity objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define gradual generation and distinguish motion in substance from motion in the four categories.

Core Idea
Gradual change in substance is generation, not motion; motion is in quantity, quality, place, or position.

The author uses the example of semen developing into an animal to illustrate substantial change. He then clarifies that true motion occurs in the categories of quantity, quality, place, and position, not in substance itself. He defines gradual generation as a state where a body's condition differs at every instant. This lesson will help you understand the distinction between substantial change and motion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the example of substantial change
Find the specific example of semen transforming into an animal in the Arabic. Notice the stages: semen → clot → chewed lump → bone → flesh, etc.
This illustrates change in substance (جوهر).
2
Extract the definition of gradual generation
Locate the sentence beginning with 'والحدوث على التدريج' and note the precise condition: at every instant the body's state differs from the instant before or after.
This is a definition (hadd) of gradual generation.
3
Note the author's assertion about motion
The author says motion is in the four previously mentioned categories, not in substance itself. Understand why: substantial change is generation, not gradual motion.
The four categories are likely quantity, quality, place, and position.
4
Understand the rebuttal about circularity
The author anticipates an objection: concepts of time depend on motion, leading to circularity. He responds that these concepts are primary (awwaliyyāt) and need no definition.
This shows the author's epistemological stance.

Another substantial form gradually. For the semen until it becomes an animal undergoes other formations including transformations in quality and quantity. So the semen continues to transform little by little while still being semen until its form is stripped off and it becomes a clot. Likewise until it transforms into a chewed lump, then bone, then flesh, nerve, and other things, even things we do not perceive; likewise until it receives the form of life. Then it transforms similarly until it strengthens and separates. But the apparent understanding is that there is motion in substance; but it is not so. Rather motion is in the four [categories] previously mentioned because of the gradual generation in them. Gradual generation is the state of a body such that no instant occurs except its state in it is different from its state in the instant before or after. And it is not said that the conception of 'instant', 'before', and 'after' depends on the conception of time, which depends on the conception of motion. For the conceptions of these things are among the primaries, so they do not need any definitions. As for motion in quantity, it is among what no one can deny, because the body is composed of matter and form. Matter in itself has no magnitude, and what has no magnitude in itself, its relation to all magnitudes is one. So the matter of a large body is receptive to a small magnitude and vice versa. Thus quantity in it is receptive to increase and decrease. And what is so has motion in it. That is in our subject in two ways: either by rarefaction and condensation, or by growth and withering. Because the taking towards increase, if it is by the entry of the increase in accordance with nature, it is called growth; and if it is the expansion of its substance without addition, it is called rarefaction. As for the taking towards decrease, if it is by the separation of its parts, it is called withering; and if without separation, it is called condensation. And these states are perceived in bodies. And it is not said: from growth nothing is understood except that the small becomes large, and from withering the opposite, and that does not happen by motion; for motion is between contraries, and there is no contrariety between small and large. For motion is not confined to contraries; rather it is sufficient that there be non-coexistence between two things: if a thing goes out from one of them to the other little by little, that thing is called moving. And if you say: 'Supposing it is so, but growth is motion in place because place changes in it, so it would be instantaneous motion,' we say: it is not impossible for growth to be motion in quantity and with it motion in place; for it is possible that in the subject of growth there is change in relation to quantity and change in relation to 'where', so that there are two motions in one state. As for motion in quality, that is with respect to what is sensible, namely affections. Affections are among what is not hidden; for motion is the change of a thing from one state to another, and that is apparent in blackening, whitening, heating, cooling, for example, and it is called alteration. As for other [qualities], then no: for some people deny motion in all types of quality except those mentioned. We say: as for disposition and state, they pertain to the soul, so they have no subject among natural bodies. As for power and lack of power and the like, they follow accidents that occur to the subject. The subject with some of those accidents becomes a subject for them, so the subject for power is not exactly the subject for lack of power; similarly for hardness and softness. As for what is specific to quantity, because it is impossible for the subject of straightness to be exactly the subject of curvature; similarly for evenness and oddness. As for their argument from the impossibility of contrariety in it, that is subject to denial for what preceded. Then one might say: the discussion about disposition and state is not complete unless their subject is the soul alone; it is not necessary that it be so, but it is possible that
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is an extended definition and defense of the existence of motion in three categories: substance, quantity, and quality. The author begins by providing an example of substantial change: the gradual transformation of semen into an animal. This illustrates motion in substance, but the author clarifies that true motion is not in substance itself but in the four categories previously mentioned (likely place, quantity, quality, and position) because substantial change is not gradual in the same sense. He then defines gradual generation (الحدوث على التدريج) as the state of a body such that at every instant its condition differs from the preceding or following instant. He anticipates an objection that the concepts of 'instant', 'before', and 'after' depend on time, which depends on motion, leading to circularity. He rebuts that these concepts are primary (أوليات) and need no definition.

Next, the author discusses motion in quantity. He argues that since bodies are composed of matter and form, and matter has no inherent magnitude, it is receptive to different magnitudes. Therefore, quantity can increase or decrease, constituting motion in quantity. This occurs in two ways: (1) rarefaction and condensation, and (2) growth and withering. He defines each: growth is increase by addition in accordance with nature; rarefaction is expansion without addition; withering is decrease by separation of parts; condensation is decrease without separation. These are perceptible in bodies.

An objection is raised: growth and withering seem to involve only size change, which is not motion because motion requires contraries, and small and large are not contraries. The author responds that motion does not require contraries; it only requires non-coexistence—if a thing changes gradually from one state to another, it is in motion. A further objection: growth might be motion in place (since the body occupies different places). The author replies that growth can be both motion in quantity and motion in place simultaneously; two motions can coexist.

Then the author addresses motion in quality. Sensible qualities like blackening and whitening are clearly forms of motion (called alteration). However, some deny motion in other types of quality, such as disposition and state (which pertain to the soul), power and lack of power (which depend on accidents), and qualities specific to quantity like straightness/curvature. The author argues that these involve different subjects, so motion in them is possible. He rejects the argument based on impossibility of contrariety in these cases, as already mentioned.

The passage ends with a new objection about disposition and state, suggesting that their subject is the soul. The author begins a response but the text cuts off. This demonstrates the dialectical style of the work: presenting definitions and defending them against objections.

آن

Key Takeaway
Gradual generation is defined by differing states at every instant, and motion in substance is not true motion; true motion occurs in the four accidental categories.

Motion in Quantity: Growth, Withering, Rarefaction, Condensation
Definition and defense of motion in quantity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the four types of motion in quantity and respond to objections about their validity.

Core Idea
Motion in quantity includes growth and withering (by addition/separation) and rarefaction and condensation (by expansion/compression without addition).

The author defends motion in quantity by arguing that matter has no fixed magnitude, so quantity can increase or decrease. He then defines four types: growth (increase by addition), rarefaction (increase by expansion), withering (decrease by separation), and condensation (decrease by compression). He also addresses objections that growth and withering are not motion because they lack contrariety, and that growth is actually motion in place.

Guided Path
1
Understand the basis: matter is receptive to different magnitudes
The author states that matter (hayūlā) has no inherent magnitude, so its relation to all magnitudes is the same. This allows for change in quantity.
This is a metaphysical foundation for motion in quantity.
2
Identify the two ways of increase: growth vs. rarefaction
Growth (numuww) involves addition of matter in accordance with nature; rarefaction (takhalkhul) involves expansion of the substance without addition.
Both result in larger size but by different mechanisms.
3
Identify the two ways of decrease: withering vs. condensation
Withering (dhubūl) involves separation of parts; condensation (takāthuf) involves compression without separation.
Both result in smaller size but by different mechanisms.
4
Analyze the objection about contrariety and the response
Objection: growth and withering are not motion because there is no contrariety between small and large. Response: motion does not require contraries; it only requires non-coexistence and gradual change.
The author broadens the definition of motion.
5
Analyze the objection about growth being motion in place
Objection: growth involves change of place, so it would be motion in place. Response: two motions (quantitative and local) can coexist simultaneously in the same subject.
This shows the author's nuanced understanding of concurrent motions.

لمقدار / صغيرة وبالعكس فتكون الكمية فيه قابلة للتزيد والتنقص . وما يكون كذلك ففيه من الحركات وذلك فيما نحن فيه على وجهين؛ إما بالتخلخل والتكاثف وإما بالنمو والذبول؛ وذلك لأن الأخذ منه إلى الزيادة إن في الأجسام. ولا يقال لا يفهم من النمو إلا وأن يصير الصغير كبيرا، ومن الذبول بالعكس، وذلك لا يكون بالحركة؛ إذ الحركة بين المتضادات ولا تضاد بين الصغير والكبير. فإن الحركة لا تكون منحصرة في المتضادات بل فيما يكون من المحسوسات وهي الانفعالات والانفعالات من جملة ما لا خفاء فيه؛ إذ الحركة عبارة عن تبدل الشيء من حالة إلى حالة وإنه في التسود والتبيض والتسخن والتبرد مثلًا ظاهر ويسمى استحالة. فأما في الغير فلا فإن من الناس من يُنكر الحركة في جميع أنواع الكيف إلا فيما مر ذكره منه. فنقول: أما الملكة والحال فإنه يتعلق بالنفس فلا موضوع له من الأجسام الطبيعية. وأما القوة واللا قوة وما أشبه ذلك فإنه يتبع Another substantial form gradually. For the semen until it becomes an animal undergoes other formations including transformations in quality and quantity. So the semen continues to transform little by little while still being semen until its form is stripped off and it becomes a clot. Likewise until it transforms into a chewed lump, then bone, then flesh, nerve, and other things, even things we do not perceive; likewise until it receives the form of life. Then it transforms similarly until it strengthens and separates. But the apparent understanding is that there is motion in substance; but it is not so. Rather motion is in the four [categories] previously mentioned because of the gradual generation in them. Gradual generation is the state of a body such that no instant occurs except its state in it is different from its state in the instant before or after. And it is not said that the conception of 'instant', 'before', and 'after' depends on the conception of time, which depends on the conception of motion. For the conceptions of these things are among the primaries, so they do not need any definitions. As for motion in quantity, it is among what no one can deny, because the body is composed of matter and form. Matter in itself has no magnitude, and what has no magnitude in itself, its relation to all magnitudes is one. So the matter of a large body is receptive to a small magnitude and vice versa. Thus quantity in it is receptive to increase and decrease. And what is so has motion in it. That is in our subject in two ways: either by rarefaction and condensation, or by growth and withering. Because the taking towards increase, if it is by the entry of the increase in accordance with nature, it is called growth; and if it is the expansion of its substance without addition, it is called rarefaction. As for the taking towards decrease, if it is by the separation of its parts, it is called withering; and if without separation, it is called condensation. And these states are perceived in bodies. And it is not said: from growth nothing is understood except that the small becomes large, and from withering the opposite, and that does not happen by motion; for motion is between contraries, and there is no contrariety between small and large. For motion is not confined to contraries; rather it is sufficient that there be non-coexistence between two things: if a thing goes out from one of them to the other little by little, that thing is called moving. And if you say: 'Supposing it is so, but growth is motion in place because place changes in it, so it would be instantaneous motion,' we say: it is not impossible for growth to be motion in quantity and with it motion in place; for it is possible that in the subject of growth there is change in relation to quantity and change in relation to 'where', so that there are two motions in one state. As for motion in quality, that is with respect to what is sensible, namely affections. Affections are among what is not hidden; for motion is the change of a thing from one state to another, and that is apparent in blackening, whitening, heating, cooling, for example, and it is called alteration. As for other [qualities], then no: for some people deny motion in all types of quality except those mentioned. We say: as for disposition and state, they pertain to the soul, so they have no subject among natural bodies. As for power and lack of power and the like, they follow accidents that occur to the subject. The subject with some of those accidents becomes a subject for them, so the subject for power is not exactly the subject for lack of power; similarly for hardness and softness. As for what is specific to quantity, because it is impossible for the subject of straightness to be exactly the subject of curvature; similarly for evenness and oddness. As for their argument from the impossibility of contrariety in it, that is subject to denial for what preceded. Then one might say: the discussion about disposition and state is not complete unless their subject is the soul alone; it is not necessary that it be so, but it is possible that
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is an extended definition and defense of the existence of motion in three categories: substance, quantity, and quality. The author begins by providing an example of substantial change: the gradual transformation of semen into an animal. This illustrates motion in substance, but the author clarifies that true motion is not in substance itself but in the four categories previously mentioned (likely place, quantity, quality, and position) because substantial change is not gradual in the same sense. He then defines gradual generation (الحدوث على التدريج) as the state of a body such that at every instant its condition differs from the preceding or following instant. He anticipates an objection that the concepts of 'instant', 'before', and 'after' depend on time, which depends on motion, leading to circularity. He rebuts that these concepts are primary (أوليات) and need no definition.

Next, the author discusses motion in quantity. He argues that since bodies are composed of matter and form, and matter has no inherent magnitude, it is receptive to different magnitudes. Therefore, quantity can increase or decrease, constituting motion in quantity. This occurs in two ways: (1) rarefaction and condensation, and (2) growth and withering. He defines each: growth is increase by addition in accordance with nature; rarefaction is expansion without addition; withering is decrease by separation of parts; condensation is decrease without separation. These are perceptible in bodies.

An objection is raised: growth and withering seem to involve only size change, which is not motion because motion requires contraries, and small and large are not contraries. The author responds that motion does not require contraries; it only requires non-coexistence—if a thing changes gradually from one state to another, it is in motion. A further objection: growth might be motion in place (since the body occupies different places). The author replies that growth can be both motion in quantity and motion in place simultaneously; two motions can coexist.

Then the author addresses motion in quality. Sensible qualities like blackening and whitening are clearly forms of motion (called alteration). However, some deny motion in other types of quality, such as disposition and state (which pertain to the soul), power and lack of power (which depend on accidents), and qualities specific to quantity like straightness/curvature. The author argues that these involve different subjects, so motion in them is possible. He rejects the argument based on impossibility of contrariety in these cases, as already mentioned.

The passage ends with a new objection about disposition and state, suggesting that their subject is the soul. The author begins a response but the text cuts off. This demonstrates the dialectical style of the work: presenting definitions and defending them against objections.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between growth (النمو) and rarefaction (التخلخل)?
A) Growth involves addition of matter, rarefaction involves expansion without addition.
B) Growth is increase by separation, rarefaction is increase by addition.
C) Growth and rarefaction are the same thing.
D) Growth occurs in plants, rarefaction occurs in animals.

Key Takeaway
Motion in quantity is real and includes four processes, each defined by the mechanism of increase or decrease.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify each of the following as growth, rarefaction, withering, or condensation: (a) a balloon expanding when heated, (b) a plant absorbing water and increasing in size, (c) a dried fruit shrinking, (d) compressing a spring.
?
Explain in your own words why the author argues that motion does not require contraries.

Motion in Quality: Alteration and its Limits
Definition of alteration and defense against denial of motion in other qualities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify motion in quality (alteration) and evaluate arguments about its existence in different types of qualities.

Core Idea
Motion in quality (alteration) is evident in sensible qualities like color and temperature, but debatable in other qualities like disposition and power.

The author defines motion in quality as alteration (istiḥāla), giving examples like blackening, whitening, heating, cooling. He then addresses the view that motion in quality only occurs in sensible qualities (affections) and not in other types of quality such as disposition/state, power/lack of power, and qualities specific to quantity (like straightness/curvature). He argues that these latter qualities involve different subjects, so motion in them is possible, though he begins a response to a further objection about disposition and state.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of alteration
Find the sentence defining alteration as change from one state to another in quality. Note the examples: blackening, whitening, heating, cooling.
Alteration is motion in quality.
2
Understand the denial: some deny motion in other qualities
The author mentions that some people deny motion in all types of quality except sensible affections (e.g., color, temperature).
This sets up the debate.
3
Analyze the author's response for each quality type
For disposition/state (malaka/ḥāl): they pertain to the soul, so subject is soul, not natural bodies. For power/lack of power (quwwa/ lā quwwa): they depend on accidents in the subject, and the subject changes. For qualities specific to quantity (istiqāma/inḥinā'): the subject of straightness is not the same as the subject of curvature.
Each type involves a change of subject.
4
Follow the beginning of the final objection about disposition and state
A new objector says: the discussion about disposition and state is incomplete unless their subject is the soul alone. The author begins to respond but the passage cuts off.
This shows the dialectical nature of the work.

He defines each: growth is increase by addition in accordance with nature; rarefaction is expansion without addition; withering is decrease by separation of parts; condensation is decrease without separation. These are perceptible in bodies.

An objection is raised: growth and withering seem to involve only size change, which is not motion because motion requires contraries, and small and large are not contraries. The author responds that motion does not require contraries; it only requires non-coexistence—if a thing changes gradually from one state to another, it is in motion. A further objection: growth might be motion in place (since the body occupies different places). The author replies that growth can be both motion in quantity and motion in place simultaneously; two motions can coexist.

Then the author addresses motion in quality. Sensible qualities like blackening and whitening are clearly forms of motion (called alteration). However, some deny motion in other types of quality, such as disposition and state (which pertain to the soul), power and lack of power (which depend on accidents), and qualities specific to quantity like straightness/curvature. The author argues that these involve different subjects, so motion in them is possible. He rejects the argument based on impossibility of contrariety in these cases, as already mentioned.

The passage ends with a new objection about disposition and state, suggesting that their subject is the soul. The author begins a response but the text cuts off. This demonstrates the dialectical style of the work: presenting definitions and defending them against objections.

Motion Beyond Place: Defending Gradual Change in All Categories
Author defends motion in categories beyond place, refuting objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and defend the position that motion (ḥaraka) exists in quantity, quality, disposition, and position by applying the principle that motion only requires gradual change, not strict contrariety.

Core Idea
Motion is gradual change between any two states, not only between opposites.

In this passage, the author faces objections from those who deny motion in categories beyond place. Critics argue that growth, changes in quality, and transitions in position are not true motion because they lack contrariety or occur instantaneously. The author systematically refutes these objections, expanding the concept of motion to cover any gradual change. This lesson will walk you through his dialectical strategy and the logical moves he uses to defend his position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested categories
The author lists motion in quantity (growth), quality (sensible and non-sensible), disposition/state, and position. Note which ones are widely accepted and which are debated.
2
Extract the core principle
Find the author's statement that motion is not confined to contraries; lack of coexistence and gradual change suffice.
The phrase 'لا تكون منحصرة في المتضادات بل يكفي فيها عدم الاجتماع' is key.
3
Analyze the objections
For each category, note the objection: e.g., growth has no contrariety, transition from standing to sitting is instantaneous.
4
Examine the author's responses
See how the author applies the core principle to each objection, and note any additional arguments (e.g., coexistence of two motions, distinction between instantaneous and gradual).
For position, the author concedes the endpoint may be instantaneous but emphasizes the gradual transition.
5
Evaluate the celestial argument
Understand how the author avoids the fallacy of composition by distinguishing 'every part' from 'all the parts'.
The sphere has no actual parts; even if assumed, the whole does not leave its place.

ولا يقال لا يفهم من النمو إلا وأن يصير الصغير كبيرا، ومن الذبول بالعكس، وذلك لا يكون بالحركة؛ إذ الحركة بين المتضادات ولا تضاد بين الصغير والكبير. فإن الحركة لا تكون منحصرة في المتضادات بل يكفي فيها عدم الاجتماع بين الشيئين فإنه إذا خرج الشيء من أحدهما إلى الآخر يسيرًا يسيرًا يسمى ذلك الشيء متحركًا. وإن قلت هب أنه كذلك لكن النمو حركة في المكان لما أن المكان تتبدل فيه فتكون حركة آنية، فنقول لا يمتنع أن يكون النمو حركة في الكم ومعه حركة في المكان؛ فإن من الممكن أن يكون في موضوع النمو تبدل بل بالنسبة إلى الكم وتبدل بالنسبة إلى الأين فتكون فيه حركتان في حالة واحدة. وأما في الكيف فذلك فيما يكون من المحسوسات وهي الانفعالات والانفعالات من جملة ما لا خفاء فيه؛ إذ الحركة عبارة عن تبدل الشيء من حالة إلى حالة وإنه في التسود والتبيض والتسخن والتبرد مثلًا ظاهر ويسمى استحالة. فأما في الغير فلا فإن من الناس من يُنكر الحركة في جميع أنواع الكيف إلا فيما مر ذكره منه. فنقول: أما الملكة والحال فإنه يتعلق بالنفس فلا موضوع له من الأجسام الطبيعية. وأما القوة واللا قوة وما أشبه ذلك فإنه يتبع أعراضًا يعرض للموضوع. فالموضوع مع البعض من تلك الأعراض يصير موضوعًا لها فلا يكون الموضوع للقوة هو بعينه الموضوع لعدم القوة وعلى هذا في الصلابة واللين. وأما ما يكون مختصا بالكم فلأنه لا يمكن أن يكون الموضوع للاستقامة هو بعينه الموضوع للانحناء؛ وعلى هذا في الزوجية والفردية. وأما استدلالهم باستحالة التضاد فيه فذلك في حيز المنع لما مر من قبل.
ثم لقائل أن يقول أما الكلام في الملكة والحال فذلك لا يتم إلا وأن يكون الموضوع فيهما هو النفس لا غير؛ ولا يلزم أن يكون كذلك بل يمكن أن يكون البدن بنفس أو النفس مع البدن فإنه يوجد فيه كمال ما بالقوة من جهة ما هو بالقوة لجوهر ما . وأما في القوة واللا قوة والصلابة واللين فذلك يتنقص بالنمو والذبول مثلا؛ إذ الموضوع فيهما هو الموضوع مع البعض من الأعراض أيضًا. [٦] وأما فيما يكون مختصا بالكم فكذلك فإنه لا يلزم / أن يكون الموضوع في هذا هو بعينه الموضوع في ذلك؛ ولا يلزم أن تكون الحركة في كل فرد من أفراد كل نوع على الخصوص في كل فرد من أفراد هذا النوع الذي يُنكر الحركة فيه من لا يُنكِرُ في الغير من الأنواع. وأما في الوضع فقد قيل إنه لا حركة فيه أصلا؛ إذ لا تضاد فيه فإنه إذا انتقل الشخص من القيام إلى القعود مثلا فإنه لا يزال في حكم القائم إلى أن يصير قاعدًا دفعة. والحق فيه أنه من جملة ما فيه الحركة بالذات فإن التضاد غير لازم لما مرَّ في الكم. وأما الانتقال من القيام إلى القعود فإن كان المراد منه أن القعود الذي هو الطرف يحصل دفعة؛ فمسلم. فأما إذا كان المراد منه غيره فلا ؛ إذ الانتقال من القيام إلى القعود قليلا قليلا نحو الانتقال من العلو إلى السفل. وأما تبدل الوضع من حال إلى حال فذلك في الجسم الذي لا مكان له كالفلك الأعظم وفيما له مكان لكنه لا يخرج عن مکانه كسائر الأفلاك فإنه إذا تحرك فيه؛ فتلك الحركة لا تكون مكانية ولا غيرها كذلك سوى الحركة التي هي في الوضع.
بین
ولا يقال حركة كل جزء من أجزاء الفلك حركة مكانية فكذلك حركة الكل؛ إذ الفلك لا جزء له بالفعل حتى يتحرك . ولو فرض له أجزاء فليس الجزء منه يفارق مكانه بل يفارق كل جزء منه جزءًا من مكان الكل إن كان كله في مكان ولسنا سلّمنا بأنه يفارق مكانه لكن لا يلزم منه أن الكل يفارق مكانه. وقد علمت الفرق بين قولنا كل جزء وبين قولنا كل الأجزاء، أو لعل قائلاً يقول الجسم إذا تحرك وهو في مكان كانت حركته حركة مكانية؛ إذ الحركة
And it is not said that growth is only understood as something small becoming large, and wilting the opposite, and that is not by motion because motion is between contraries and there is no contrariety between small and large. For motion is not confined to contraries; rather it is sufficient that there is no coexistence between the two things — if a thing exits from one to the other gradually, that thing is called moving. And if you say: 'Granted it is so, but growth is motion in place because place changes in it, so it becomes instantaneous motion,' we say: It is not impossible for growth to be motion in quantity and along with it motion in place; for it is possible that in the subject of growth there is change with respect to quantity and change with respect to 'where', so there are two motions in one state. As for quality, that is in sensible things, namely affections, and affections are among those which are obvious; for motion is the change of a thing from state to state, and it is apparent in blackening, whitening, heating, cooling, for example, and it is called transformation (istihāla). As for other [types of quality], some people deny motion in all types of quality except those previously mentioned. We say: As for disposition and state (al-malaka wa al-ḥāl), they pertain to the soul, so they have no subject from natural bodies. As for potency and impotence and the like, they follow accidents that occur to the subject. The subject with some of those accidents becomes a subject for them, so the subject for potency is not identical to the subject for lack of potency; and likewise for hardness and softness. As for what is specific to quantity, it is impossible for the subject of straightness to be identical to the subject of curvature; and likewise for evenness and oddness. As for their argument by the impossibility of contrariety in it, that is subject to denial (manʿ) for what has preceded.
Then someone might say: 'As for the discussion of disposition and state, that is only complete if the subject in them is the soul alone; it is not necessary that it be so, but it can be the body with the soul, or the soul with the body, for in it is found a perfection from potentiality insofar as it is potential for some substance.' As for potency and impotence and hardness and softness, those are diminished by growth and wilting, for example, because the subject in them is the subject along with some accidents as well. [6] As for what is specific to quantity, likewise it is not necessary that the subject in this is identical to the subject in that; nor is it necessary that motion be in every individual of every type specifically in every individual of this type which is denied motion by one who does not deny it in other types. As for position (al-waḍʿ), it is said that there is no motion in it at all because there is no contrariety in it; for if a person transitions from standing to sitting, for example, he remains in the ruling of standing until he becomes sitting instantaneously. The truth is that it is among those things in which there is motion essentially, for contrariety is not necessary as was previously established for quantity. As for the transition from standing to sitting, if what is intended is that sitting, which is the endpoint, occurs instantaneously, that is conceded. But if something else is intended, then no; for the transition from standing to sitting little by little is like the transition from above to below. As for the change of position from state to state, that is in a body that has no place, like the outermost sphere, and in what has a place but does not leave its place, like the other spheres; for if it moves in it, that motion is not local nor other than it except the motion that is in position.
Between
And it is not said that the motion of every part of the sphere is local motion, so likewise the motion of the whole; for the sphere has no actual part to move. And if parts are assumed, it is not that a part leaves its place; rather each part leaves a part of the place of the whole, if the whole is in a place. But we do not concede that it leaves its place, but it does not follow that the whole leaves its place. And you have known the difference between 'every part' and 'all the parts'. Or perhaps someone says: 'When a body moves while in a place, its motion is local motion; for motion...'
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical defense of motion in categories beyond place (makan). The author is responding to objections that deny motion in quantity (like growth), quality (like alteration), disposition (malaka/hal), and position (wad'). He begins by addressing growth (numuww) and wilting (dhabul): critics claim no motion exists because there is no contrariety between small and large. The author counters that motion does not require strict contrariety; gradual change from one state to another suffices. He then rejects the claim that growth is merely instantaneous motion in place, arguing that two motions (in quantity and in place) can coexist. Next, he tackles quality: sensible qualities like blackening/whitening obviously involve motion (istihala). For other qualities like disposition (e.g., knowledge, health) and potency/impotence, he argues that the subject changes, so motion is possible. He also addresses quantity-related qualities (straight/curved) and denies that contrariety is necessary. The second part addresses an objection about dispositional qualities: some say they inhere only in the soul, but the author allows the body or body-soul composite as subject. For potency and hardness, growth/wilting shows they are subject to change. The third part concerns position (wad'): despite claims that change from standing to sitting is instantaneous, the author insists it can be gradual, like motion from high to low. Finally, he discusses celestial spheres: their motion is not local because they have no actual parts leaving place. He distinguishes 'every part' from 'all the parts' to avoid the fallacy of composition. Throughout, the author relies on the earlier principle that motion does not require contrariety, only lack of coexistence.
