The Four Categories with Essential Motion
Author identifies four categories with essential motion and refutes objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four categories that have essential motion and explain why motion is possible in each, using examples from natural phenomena and refuting common objections.

Core Idea
Only four categories admit motion essentially: quantity, quality, place, and position.

In this passage, the author (likely Ibn Sina) examines which of the ten categories of being have motion intrinsically. He argues that only four categories – quantity, quality, place, and position – qualify, while others like relation do not. This classification is foundational for understanding change in the natural world, from growth to locomotion. We will unpack his reasoning and see how he defends the inclusion of position against the objection that it lacks contrariety.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
Read the passage and list the four categories the author explicitly mentions: quantity (al-kamm), quality (al-kayf), place (al-ayn), and position (al-wadʿ).
2
Find examples for each category
For each category, locate the examples the author uses: growth/withering for quality, increase/decrease for quantity, locomotion for place, and standing/sitting for position.
3
Analyze the objection about position
Identify the objection: some deny motion in position because there is no contrariety (e.g., standing vs. sitting are not opposites). Note the author's refutation: contrariety is not necessary for motion; gradual change qualifies.
4
Examine the argument on celestial spheres
The author argues that the circular motion of spheres is positional, not spatial. Understand why each part's motion cannot be used to claim the whole moves spatially.
5
Summarize the principle
The overarching principle: motion exists essentially only in these four categories; all other categories either derive motion from these or do not admit motion at all.

البدن بنفس أو النفس مع البدن فإنه يوجد فيه كمال ما بالقوة من جهة ما هو بالقوة لجوهر ما . وأما في القوة واللا قوة والصلابة واللين فذلك يتنقص بالنمو والذبول مثلا؛ إذ الموضوع فيهما هو الموضوع مع البعض من الأعراض أيضًا. [٦] وأما فيما يكون مختصا بالكم فكذلك فإنه لا يلزم أن يكون الموضوع في هذا هو بعينه الموضوع في ذلك؛ ولا يلزم أن تكون الحركة في كل فرد من أفراد كل نوع على الخصوص في كل فرد من أفراد هذا النوع الذي يُنكر الحركة فيه من لا يُنكِرُ في الغير من الأنواع. وأما في الوضع فقد قيل إنه لا حركة فيه أصلا؛ إذ لا تضاد فيه فإنه إذا انتقل الشخص من القيام إلى القعود مثلا فإنه لا يزال في حكم القائم إلى أن يصير قاعدًا دفعة. والحق فيه أنه من جملة ما فيه الحركة بالذات فإن التضاد غير لازم لما مرَّ في الكم. وأما الانتقال من القيام إلى القعود فإن كان المراد منه أن القعود الذي هو الطرف يحصل دفعة؛ فمسلم. فأما إذا كان المراد منه غيره فلا ؛ إذ الانتقال من القيام إلى القعود قليلا قليلا نحو الانتقال من العلو إلى السفل. وأما تبدل الوضع من حال إلى حال فذلك في الجسم الذي لا مكان له كالفلك الأعظم وفيما له مكان لكنه لا يخرج عن مکانه كسائر الأفلاك فإنه إذا تحرك فيه؛ فتلك الحركة لا تكون مكانية ولا غيرها كذلك سوى الحركة التي هي في الوضع. بین ولا يقال حركة كل جزء من أجزاء الفلك حركة مكانية فكذلك حركة الكل؛ إذ الفلك لا جزء له بالفعل حتى يتحرك . ولو فرض له أجزاء فليس الجزء منه يفارق مكانه بل يفارق كل جزء منه جزءًا من مكان الكل إن كان كله في مكان ولسنا سلّمنا بأنه يفارق مكانه لكن لا يلزم منه أن الكل يفارق مكانه. وقد علمت الفرق بين قولنا كل جزء وبين قولنا كل الأجزاء، أو لعل قائلاً يقول الجسم إذا تحرك وهو في مكان كانت حركته حركة مكانية؛ إذ الحركة المكانية عبارة عن هذا لا غير. فيقال هذا بحث لفظي فإن الجسم إذا تحرك وهو في مكان فلا يخلو من أن يخرج عن ذلك المكان إلى مكان آخر؛ وحينئذ تكون الحركة حركة مكانية ، أو لا يخرج بل يتحرك حركة مستديرة في ذلك المكان؛ وحينئذ لا تكون الحركة حركة مكانية بل وضعية: يعني هي الحركة في الوضع. وأما في الأين وهي انتقال الجسم لكليته من مكان إلى مكان فذلك من جملة ما لا حاجة له إلى البيان. فهذه الأربع من المقولات وهي الكم والكيف والأين والوضع مما فيه الحركة بحسب الذات على خلاف ما عداها منها نحو المضاف وغيره.
The body by itself or the soul with the body, for it contains some perfection in potentiality from the aspect that it is in potentiality for some substance. As for strength and weakness, hardness and softness, that decreases by growth and withering, for example; since the subject in them is the subject together with some accidents as well. [6] And as for what is specific to quantity, likewise, it does not necessarily follow that the subject in this one is exactly the subject in that one; nor does it necessarily follow that motion in every individual of every species particularly in every individual of this species which one who denies motion in it does not deny in other species. As for position, it has been said that there is no motion in it at all, since there is no contrariety in it; for if a person moves from standing to sitting, for example, he remains under the ruling of standing until he becomes sitting instantaneously. The truth is that it is among those things in which there is motion essentially, for contrariety is not necessary, as passed in quantity. As for the transition from standing to sitting, if what is meant is that sitting which is the endpoint occurs instantaneously, it is granted; but if what is meant is other than that, then no, since the transition from standing to sitting little by little is like the transition from high to low. As for the change of position from state to state, that is in a body which has no place, like the highest sphere, and in what has a place but does not exit its place, like the other spheres; for if it moves within it, that motion is neither spatial nor other than that, except the motion which is in position. And one should not say that the motion of each part of the sphere is spatial, so likewise the motion of the whole; since the sphere has no part in actuality such that it moves. And if parts were assumed for it, its part does not leave its place; rather each part of it leaves a part of the place of the whole, if the whole is in a place; and we do not concede that it leaves its place, but it does not necessitate that the whole leaves its place. And you have known the difference between our saying 'every part' and our saying 'all the parts'. Or perhaps a sayer says: when a body moves while it is in a place, its motion is spatial, since spatial motion is nothing but this. So it is said: this is a verbal inquiry; for when a body moves while it is in a place, it is not free from either exiting that place to another place – and then the motion is spatial – or it does not exit but moves circularly within that place – and then the motion is not spatial but positional: meaning it is motion in position. As for 'where' (place), which is the transition of the body in its entirety from place to place, that is among what needs no explanation. So these four categories – quantity, quality, place, and position – are those in which motion exists essentially, contrary to what is other than them, such as relation and others.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage identifies the four categories in which motion occurs essentially: quantity, quality, place, and position. The author begins with a brief remark about quality, using the example of strength/weakness and hardness/softness, which change by growth and withering, noting that the subject in such changes includes some accidents. Then he turns to quantity, arguing that motion in quantity – e.g., increase or decrease – does not require the same subject across species, and that denying motion in one species while affirming it in another is inconsistent. Next, he treats position (wad'), addressing the objection that there is no motion in position because there is no contrariety (e.g., standing vs. sitting are not opposites like hot and cold). He refutes this by pointing out that contrariety is not required for motion, as already shown in quantity. He distinguishes a sudden transition (which is not motion) from a gradual one, which is genuine motion. He then discusses the motion of celestial spheres, arguing that their circular motion is positional, not spatial, and addresses a potential counterexample about parts of the sphere. Finally, he briefly mentions place (ayn) as obvious. The passage concludes by listing the four categories that have essential motion, contrasting them with others like relation.

Motion in Substance and Other Categories as Accidental
Author argues that substance, relation, when, possession, and action have motion only accidentally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which categories admit motion essentially and which admit motion only accidentally, and explain the reason for each.

Core Idea
Motion in substance, relation, when, possession, and action is always accidental—never essential.

In the previous lesson, you learned that motion exists essentially in four categories: quantity, quality, place, and position. Now the author turns to the remaining categories, arguing that motion in them is merely accidental. This distinction is critical for understanding how change occurs in the world according to the Aristotelian framework adopted by Islamic philosophers and theologians.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category under examination
For each category (substance, relation, when, possession, action), locate the term in the Arabic and note the author's claim about motion.
Each category begins with 'أما في'.
2
Extract the reason for accidental motion
For each category, find the specific reason why motion cannot be essential. For example, substance is self-subsistent, relation changes instantaneously.
The reasons are explicit in the commentary.
3
Compare with essential motion categories
Notice that the four essential categories (quantity, quality, place, position) all involve gradual change in a subject that remains, whereas these accidental categories either change instantaneously or are derivative.
Refer back to the previous lesson if needed.
4
Apply the pattern to the missing category (passion)
Since the passage is cut off, infer that passion (an yanfa'ila) would follow the same logic as action, as it is its counterpart.
The author likely intended to include passion.

أما في الجوهر فلأنه قائم بذاته والحركة لا تكون إلا فيما يكون قائمًا بالغير؛ إذ الغير من اللوازم؛ وذلك في ذات الجوهر محال. وأما في المضاف فالانتقال فيه إنما هو من حال إلى حال دفعة؛ وذلك لا يكون من الحركات فإذا أضيف إليه الحركة فالحركة بالحقيقة أولا لما يعرض له الإضافة؛ إذ الإضافة من شأنها أن تلحق لكل مقولة من المقولات. فإذا كانت المقولة مما يقبل التزيد والتنقص عرض للإضافة مثل ذلك. فالسخونة / مثلًا [٧٠و] تقبل التزيد والتنقص فيكون الأسخن كذلك لكن الحركة في الأمر العارض؛ والإضافة بالعرض بعد ذلك. وأما في متى فالانتقال فيه من حال إلى حال دفعة نحو الانتقال من سنة إلى سنة أو من شهر إلى شهر فلا تكون فيه الحركة بل الحركة أولاً في الكيف أو في الكم ويكون الزمان؛ لأن ما لتلك الحركة فتعرض له الحركة إذن بذلك السبب ؛ وذلك بالعرض كما في المضاف. وأما في الملك فالتبدل لا يكون أولا فيه بل في الأين ثم فيه بالعرض؛ وذلك لأن الملك يدل على نسبة الجسم إلى ما يشتمله فيكون تبدل هذه النسبة أولا إنما هو في السطح الحاوي؛ وذلك في المكان. وأما في أن يفعل فالتبدل لا يكون أولا فيه أيضًا بل
As for substance, because it is self-subsistent, and motion only occurs in what is subsistent through another; for the other is among the accidents, and that is impossible in the essence of substance. As for relation, transition in it is only from state to state instantaneously; and that is not among motions. So if motion is attributed to it, motion in reality is first for what the relation attaches to; for relation is such that it attaches to every category. So if the category admits increase and decrease, relation undergoes something similar. So for example, heat [folio 70] admits increase and decrease, so the hotter is likewise, but motion is in the accidental thing, and relation is accidentally thereafter. As for 'when' (time), transition in it is from state to state instantaneously, like transition from year to year or month to month; there is no motion in it, but motion is first in quality or quantity and time exists; because that which is for that motion then motion attaches to it by that cause; and that is accidentally, as in relation. As for possession, change is not first in it but in place, then accidentally in it; because possession indicates the relation of the body to what contains it, so the change of this relation is first in the encompassing surface, and that is in place. As for action, change is not first in it either, but
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage discusses the remaining categories where motion occurs only accidentally: substance, relation, when, possession, and action (with passion implied but cut off). The author begins with substance (jawhar), arguing that it cannot have motion because it is self-subsistent; motion requires dependence on another, which substance lacks. Next, relation (mudhāf) is considered. Change in relation is instantaneous (e.g., from one relation to another), which is not motion proper. If motion is attributed to relation, it is really in the subject that the relation attaches to, and relation follows accidentally. For example, heat increases, so the relation of being hotter changes, but the motion is in the heat, not the relation itself. The author then treats 'when' (matā), i.e., time. Transition from one time to another is also instantaneous, so motion is not in time itself but in quality or quantity that occurs over time; time is merely the measure. Then possession (milk) is discussed: change in possession (e.g., wearing a garment) depends on place; the primary change is in the containing surface, so possession changes accidentally. Finally, action (an yaf‘ala) is mentioned, but the passage breaks off mid-sentence. The pattern is clear: all these categories have motion only in a derivative sense, while the four categories (quantity, quality, place, position) have motion essentially.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that motion in the category of relation is accidental?
A) Because relation changes gradually like quantity
B) Because relation changes instantaneously, so motion is really in the subject that relation attaches to
C) Because relation is not a real category
D) Because relation only exists in the mind

Key Takeaway
Motion in substance, relation, when, possession, and action is always accidental—it derives from motion in another category and is not inherent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the category of 'when' (matā). The author says motion in 'when' is accidental. Explain in your own words why, and give an example from the passage (e.g., moving from year to year).
?
The category of possession (milk) changes accidentally due to change in place. Identify a real fiqh example where a change in possession (e.g., renting a house) involves motion in place first.

The Ten Categories
intermediate
5 min
Motion Occurs in Only Four Categories
Author argues that motion primarily occurs in quality, quantity, where, and position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four categories in which motion occurs essentially and distinguish them from accidental motion in other categories.

Core Idea
Motion is gradual change; only quality, quantity, where, and position admit it directly.

This passage from a logic textbook examines which categories of being admit motion. The author argues that motion, defined as gradual change (tadrīj), belongs essentially to only four of the ten categories. Understanding this distinction helps in analyzing definitions and physical changes in Islamic theology and philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish gradual vs instantaneous change
Motion is defined as gradual change (tadrīj). Instantaneous changes, like changing from year to year in the category of time, are not motion. Identify which changes in the text are described as instantaneous.
The author uses examples like relation (mudaf) and time (mata) as instantaneous.
2
Identify the four essential categories
From the text, the author names quantity (kamm), quality (kayf), where (ayn), and position (wadʿ) as the categories in which motion occurs essentially. List them and note their definitions.
3
Understand why other categories are excluded
For each non-essential category (substance, relation, time, possession, acting, being acted upon), the author gives a reason. For example, substance is self-subsisting and motion requires being subsisting through another. Read each argument to see why motion cannot directly occur there.
The author's reasoning is based on the nature of each category.
4
Recognize accidental motion
Motion can be attributed to other categories accidentally, if it first occurs in one of the four essential categories. For instance, change in relation is accidental because it follows change in quality or quantity. Identify such cases in the passage.
The phrase 'bi al-ʿaraḍ' (accidentally) is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المكانية عبارة عن هذا لا غير. فيقال هذا بحث لفظي فإن الجسم إذا تحرك وهو في مكان فلا يخلو من أن يخرج عن ذلك المكان إلى مكان آخر؛ وحينئذ تكون الحركة حركة مكانية، أو لا يخرج بل يتحرك حركة مستديرة في ذلك المكان؛ وحينئذ لا تكون الحركة حركة مكانية بل وضعية: يعني هي الحركة في الوضع. وأما في الأين وهي انتقال الجسم لكليته من مكان إلى مكان فذلك من جملة ما لا حاجة له إلى البيان. فهذه الأربع من المقولات وهي الكم والكيف والأين والوضع مما فيه الحركة بحسب الذات على خلاف ما عداها منها نحو المضاف وغيره. أما في الجوهر فلأنه قائم بذاته والحركة لا تكون إلا فيما يكون قائمًا بالغير؛ إذ الغير من اللوازم؛ وذلك في ذات الجوهر محال. وأما في المضاف فالانتقال فيه إنما هو من حال إلى حال دفعة؛ وذلك لا يكون من الحركات فإذا أضيف إليه الحركة فالحركة بالحقيقة أولا لما يعرض له الإضافة؛ إذ الإضافة من شأنها أن تلحق لكل مقولة من المقولات. فإذا كانت المقولة مما يقبل التزيد والتنقص عرض للإضافة مثل ذلك. فالسخونة / مثلًا [٧٠و] تقبل التزيد والتنقص فيكون الأسخن كذلك لكن الحركة في الأمر العارض؛ والإضافة بالعرض بعد ذلك. وأما في متى فالانتقال فيه من حال إلى حال دفعة نحو الانتقال من سنة إلى سنة أو من شهر إلى شهر فلا تكون فيه الحركة بل الحركة أولاً في الكيف أو في الكم ويكون الزمان؛ لأن ما لتلك الحركة فتعرض له الحركة إذن بذلك السبب؛ وذلك بالعرض كما في المضاف. وأما في الملك فالتبدل لا يكون أولا فيه بل في الأين ثم فيه بالعرض؛ وذلك لأن الملك يدل على نسبة الجسم إلى ما يشتمله فيكون تبدل هذه النسبة أولا إنما هو في السطح الحاوي؛ وذلك في المكان. وأما في أن يفعل فالتبدل لا يكون أولا فيه أيضًا بل في اكتساب الهيئة والصورة التي بها يصح أن يصدر الفعل؛ وذلك على سبيل التدرج فيكون أن يفعل غاية لذلك التدرج. ثم التبدل الذي يتقدمه بوجوه مختلفة منها أن يكون بالطبع ومنها أن يكون بالإرادة؛ وذلك قد يكون بالآلة وقد لا يكون. فإذن التبدل إما في القوة، وإما في العزيمة (١)، وإما في الآلة وبعد ذلك في الفعل بالعرض. وعلى هذا في أن ينفعل فإنه غاية لذلك التدرج أيضًا. ولا يمتنع أن يكون الشيء قد يتغير من أن لا يكون ينفعل بالحد إلى أن ينفعل بالحد ويكون ذلك يسيرًا يسيرًا فيظن أنه حركة. فيعلم من هذه الجملة أن الحركة إنما تقع في المقولات الأربع وهي الكيف والكم والأين والوضع كما مرَّ، وفي الباقي منها لا تكون إلا وأن تكون في واحد منها أولا؛ وذلك بالعرض على ما عرف.
The motion in place is expressed as this only. So it is said: this is a verbal investigation, for when a body moves while it is in a place, it is inevitable either that it exits that place to another place; in that case the motion is a motion in place, or it does not exit but rather moves in a circular motion in that same place; in that case the motion is not a motion in place but a motion in position: meaning it is motion in position. As for motion in 'where' (ayn), which is the transfer of the body entirely from one place to another, that is among the things that need no clarification. So these four of the categories, which are quantity, quality, where, and position, are those in which motion occurs essentially, unlike the rest of them, such as relation and others. As for substance, because it is self-subsisting, and motion only occurs in what is subsisting through another, since the other is among the concomitants; and that is impossible in the essence of substance. As for relation, the transition in it is only from state to state instantaneously; and that is not among motions. So if motion is attributed to it, the motion is in reality first for what happens to it of relation; for relation is such that it attaches to every category. So if the category admits increase and decrease, relation is affected by that. So heat, for example [70v], admits increase and decrease, thus the hotter is likewise, but the motion is in the accidental thing; and relation is accidentally afterwards. As for when (mata), the transition in it is from state to state instantaneously, like the transition from year to year or from month to month, so there is no motion in it; rather motion occurs first in quality or quantity, and time becomes something that accrues to that motion; so motion is attributed to it thereby for that reason; and that is by accident, as in relation. As for possession (milk), the change does not occur first in it but rather in 'where', and then in it accidentally; because possession indicates the relation of a body to what encompasses it, so the change of this relation first occurs in the encompassing surface; and that is in place. As for acting (an yaf'al), the change does not occur first in it either, but rather in acquiring the state and form by which the action can issue; and that is gradual, so that acting is the end of that gradual process. Then the change that precedes it has various modes: sometimes by nature, sometimes by will; and that may be with an instrument or without. So change is either in power, or in resolve (1), or in the instrument, and thereafter in the act accidentally. Likewise for being acted upon (an yanfa'il), it is the end of that gradual process as well. And it is not impossible that a thing changes from not being acted upon at all to being acted upon to the limit, and that happens little by little, so it is thought to be motion. So from this summary it is known that motion occurs only in the four categories: quality, quantity, where, and position, as mentioned; and in the rest of them, motion only occurs if it is first in one of these four; and that is by accident, as has been known.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage defines the categories in which motion occurs essentially. The author begins by distinguishing motion in place (makaniyya) from motion in position (wad'iyya): if a body moves from one place to another, it is motion in place; if it rotates within the same place, it is motion in position. He then states that the four categories of quantity (kamm), quality (kayf), where (ayn), and position (wad') are those that admit motion essentially, unlike the other categories. The author proceeds to justify why motion does not occur essentially in each of the remaining categories. For substance (jawhar), motion is impossible because substance is self-subsisting, whereas motion requires something subsisting through another. For relation (mudaf), change is instantaneous, not gradual, so motion is only accidentally attributed to relation via the underlying category that admits increase and decrease, such as heat. For time (mata), change is instantaneous (e.g., from year to year), so motion first occurs in quality or quantity, and then time is associated accidentally. For possession (milk), change is first in place, then accidentally in possession. For acting and being acted upon, change is gradual in acquiring a form, and the action is the endpoint. Finally, the author concludes that motion is essentially found only in the four categories mentioned, and in others it is accidental. All examples used are logical and abstract, but the reasoning applies to physical changes, such as the gradual heating of water or the movement of a body from one location to another.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following categories does NOT admit motion essentially?
A) Quantity (kamm)
B) Quality (kayf)
C) Substance (jawhar)

Key Takeaway
Motion is gradual change and occurs essentially only in quality, quantity, where, and position; all other categories have motion only accidentally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a pot of water being heated. In which of the four categories does the change occur essentially? Explain why this is motion, not an instantaneous change, using the author's definition.
?
Identify a change that appears to be motion but is actually accidental per the author: e.g., a son becomes older than his father (relation). Why is this not essentially motion?
